First Proof of the Necessitarians: Only One Effect
Presentation of the first argument that a necessary cause can only produce one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument used by the Necessitarians to prove that a necessary cause can only produce one effect, and why they consider it impossible for a single cause to produce multiple distinct effects directly.

Core Idea
A simple necessary cause cannot have two distinct effect-concepts without becoming composite or leading to infinite regress.

This passage presents the first proof of the Necessitarians, who argue that a necessary cause (like God in their view) can only produce one effect directly. The argument uses logical analysis of the concepts of being a source for different effects. Although the text is truncated, the reasoning unfolds clearly up to that point. We will walk through its steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure of the argument.
The passage is truncated at the end, so the final conclusion is not fully stated.
2
Identify the three cases
The argument considers whether both effect-concepts are inside the quiddity, both outside, or one inside and one outside. Each case is examined.
3
Analyze the consequences
For each case, the argument shows that either composition of the cause or infinite regress follows. Both are considered impossible for a necessary being.
The impossibility of composition and regress is taken as given.
4
Draw the intended conclusion
From the analysis, the Necessitarians conclude that only one effect can proceed from a necessary cause. The truncated text hints at this.
The final part about the internal concept not being an effect is given, but the full conclusion is cut off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الوجوه المذكورة في أن الحق هو القادر المختار ، لا الموجب بالذات فقد تقدم ذكرها مع شبهات القائلين بالموجب. فلا فائدة في الإعادة . ثم إن القائلين بالموجب الذين يقولون : إنه يمتنع أن يصدر عنه أكثر من معلول واحد . فقد احتجوا بوجوه : الأول : إن مفهوم كونه مصدراً للألف ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونه مصدراً للباء . فهذان المفهومان إن كانا داخلين في تقويم ماهية العلة ، لزم أن تكون العلة مركبة . وذلك محال . وإن كانا خارجين عن تقويم الماهية ، كانا لاحقين للماهية ، فكانا مفتقرين إليها ، فكانا ممكنين ومعلمين بتلك الماهية ، فيعود البحث المذكور من أن المفهوم من كون تلك الماهية علة لأحدهما ، غير المفهوم من كونها علة للثاني . ويلزم التسلسل . وإن كان أحدهما داخلا في الماهية ، والآخر خارجاً عنها ، لزم تركب تلك الماهية ، ولزم كون المعلول واحداً . أما تركب تلك الماهية فهو أن كل ماله جزء فهو مركب . وأما كون المعلول واحداً ، فلان الداخل في الماهية لا يكون معلولاً لها ، لأن الداخل في الماهية
As for the faces mentioned that the Truth is the Powerful Chooser, not the necessary by essence, they have already been presented along with the doubts of those who affirm the necessary cause. So there is no benefit in repetition. Then, those who affirm the necessary cause and say that it is impossible for more than one effect to issue from it have argued with proofs: The first: The concept of its being a source for A is different from the concept of its being a source for B. These two concepts, if they enter into the constitution of the quiddity of the cause, then the cause would be composed, and that is impossible. If they are external to the constitution of the quiddity, then they would be accidents of the quiddity, thus they would be dependent upon it, thus they would be possible and [لاحقين?] known by that quiddity, then the mentioned inquiry returns: that the concept of that quiddity being a cause for one of them is different from the concept of its being a cause for the second, and an infinite regress follows. If one of them is internal to the quiddity and the other external, then composition of that quiddity follows, and it follows that the effect is one. As for the composition of that quiddity, it is because everything that has a part is composed. As for the effect being one, it is because the internal to the quiddity is not an effect of it, because the internal to the quiddity
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the proponents of necessary emanation (القائلون بالموجب). It begins by noting that earlier arguments for the free agent have already been given, so they are not repeated. Then it states that the necessitarians, who claim a necessary cause can only produce one effect, have several proofs. The first proof is based on the concepts of being a source for two different effects (A and B). If the cause's quiddity includes both concepts, the cause would be composite, which is impossible (since the necessary cause must be simple). If both concepts are outside the quiddity, they become accidents dependent on the quiddity, and then the question of how the quiddity could be a cause for two different concepts arises, leading to an infinite regress. If one concept is inside and the other outside, then the quiddity is composite, and the internal concept is not an effect of the quiddity, so only the external concept can be an effect, meaning only one effect is possible. This argument uses the impossibility of composition and infinite regress to defend the necessitarian position.

Key Terms
القائلون بالموجب
معلول واحد
تسلسل
تركب
ماهية العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument assumes the cause's quiddity is composed, but it actually shows that any attempt to have two distinct effect-concepts leads to composition or regress, thus supporting the simplicity of the cause.
Correction: The argument does not assume composition; it demonstrates that composition or regress are unavoidable consequences of trying to have two distinct effects, thereby proving that a necessary cause must be simple and produce only one effect.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof of the necessitarians, what happens if both concepts (being a source for A and for B) are included in the constitution of the cause's quiddity?
A) The cause would be simple
B) The cause would be composite, which is impossible
C) The concepts would be accidents
D) Infinite regress would follow

Key Takeaway
The first proof of the Necessitarians argues that a necessary cause cannot have two distinct effect-concepts without leading to composition or infinite regress, so it can only produce one effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah's actions (like creating, providing, forgiving), do you tend to imagine multiple 'parts' or 'roles' in His essence? This argument shows that such multiplicity would imply composition. How does this challenge your mental habit? Take a moment to reflect on the simplicity of Allah and let it transform how you understand His absolute oneness.
?
If Allah's essence is perfectly simple and can only 'produce' one effect (the first creation), does that make you feel distant or close? This might seem abstract, but it means that everything after that first effect is linked through a chain of causality that ultimately ends in Him. Does this deepen your trust that all events come from one simple source? What would change in your daily reliance on Allah if you truly internalized this?
?
The argument forces you to choose: either the cause is composite (impossible for Allah) or only one effect can issue directly. Yet we see countless things in existence. Are you willing to accept that the multiplicity we see must come through intermediaries, not directly from Allah's essence? Does your heart accept that, or do you feel a tension? Let that tension lead you to deeper study and greater submission to what the intellect and revelation agree upon.

First Argument: Conceptual Composition of the Cause
Presents the first argument that a cause cannot have two distinct concepts of causation without composition or regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument that a single cause cannot be the source of two different effects without leading to composition, regress, or a single effect.

Core Idea
A simple cause cannot contain two distinct aspects of causation.

The first argument against a single cause producing multiple effects is rooted in conceptual analysis. It examines the relationship between the cause's essence and the concepts of being a source of each effect, showing that any way of relating them leads to absurdity. This argument highlights the importance of simplicity in the necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
The argument uses two hypothetical effects, 'alif' and 'ba', to examine the conceptual relationship between the cause and its effects. The concept of being the source of 'alif' is distinct from that of being the source of 'ba'.
These symbols represent any two different effects.
2
Follow the three possibilities
Consider whether both concepts are internal to the essence, both external, or one internal and one external. Each leads to a problem: composition, infinite regress, or a single effect.
Composition is impossible for a necessary being.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The only consistent outcome is that only one effect is possible, because an internal aspect cannot be an effect (it is prior) and an external aspect leads to regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الفصل الثالث. أما الوجوه المذكورة في أن الحق هو القادر المختار، لا الموجب بالذات. فقد تقدم ذكرها مع شبهات القائلين بالموجب. فلا فائدة في الإعادة. ثم إن القائلين بالموجب الذين يقولون: إنه يمتنع أن يصدر عنه أكثر من معلول واحد. فقد احتجوا بوجوه: الأول: إن مفهوم كونه مصدراً للألف، مغاير للمفهوم من كونه مصدراً للباء. فهذان المفهومان إن كانا داخلين في تقويم ماهية العلة، لزم أن تكون العلة مركبة. وذلك محال. وإن كانا خارجين عن تقويم الماهية، كانا لاحقين للماهية، فكانا مفتقرين إليها، فكانا ممكنين ومعلمين بتلك الماهية، فيعود البحث المذكور من أن المفهوم من كون تلك الماهية علة لأحدهما، غير المفهوم من كونها علة للثاني. ويلزم التسلسل. وإن كان أحدهما داخلاً في الماهية، والآخر خارجاً عنها، لزم تركب تلك الماهية، ولزم كون المعلول واحداً. أما تركب تلك الماهية فهو أن كل ماله جزء فهو مركب. وأما كون المعلول واحداً، فلإن الداخل في الماهية لا يكون معلولاً لها، لأن الداخل في الماهية متقدم عليها بالرتبة. والمعلول متأخر، والمتقدم غير المتأخر. الحجة الثانية: إنه لو صدر عنه الألف، وصدر عنه الباء أيضاً. والباء ليس نفس الألف، فحينئذ يصدق أن من الاعتبار الذي صدر عنه الألف [لم يصدر عنه الألف] لأن بذلك الاعتبار صدر عنه الباء مع أن الباء ليس بالألف. فيلزم أن يقال: إنه بالاعتبار الواحد، صدر عنه [الألف] ولم يصدر عنه الألف. وذلك محال. الحجة الثالثة: إنا لما علمنا: أن طبيعة الماء توجب البرودة، وأن طبيعة النار توجب تسخين، لاجرم استدللنا باختلاف هذين الأثرين، على أن طبيعة الماء مخالفة لطبيعة النار. فلما دل اختلاف [الآثار على اختلاف] طبائع المؤثرات فبأن تدل على تغايرها [أولى]. الحجة الرابعة: إن العلة لا بد وأن تكون بينها وبين المعلول مشابهة ومناسبة. فإن العلم لا يكون علة للمتحركية، والحركة لا تكون علة للعالمية. لأنه ليس بين العلم وبين المتحركية مناسبة. وأما العلم فإنه يوجب العالمية، لأنه حصل بينهما مناسبة ومشابهة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو اقتضت العلة الواحدة معلولين مختلفين، لزم كون تلك العلة الواحدة متشابهة بالوجه الواحد، لشيئين مختلفين، والمشابه للمختلفين مختلف. فيلزم كون ذلك الواحد مخالفاً لنفسه. وإنه محال. الحجة الخامسة: إن الموجب الواحد، إذا تعدى عن إيجاب المعلول الواحد، لم يكن بأن يوجب عدداً، أولى من أن يوجب عدداً آخر. فيلزم أن توجب تلك العلة الواحدة، معلولات لا نهاية لها. وذلك محال.
Discourse on the third chapter. As for the aforementioned arguments that the Truth is the Powerful and Free Chooser, not the necessitator by essence, they have already been mentioned with the doubts of those who hold the necessitator, so there is no benefit in repetition. Then those who hold the necessitator, who say that it is impossible for more than one effect to proceed from Him, have argued with proofs: The first: The concept of His being a source of 'alif' is different from the concept of His being a source of 'ba'. These two concepts: if they are internal to the constitution of the essence of the cause, then the cause would be composite, and that is impossible. If they are external to the constitution of the essence, then they are accidents of the essence, so they are dependent on it, so they are possible and known by that essence, so the aforementioned inquiry returns: the concept of that essence being a cause for one is different from the concept of it being a cause for the second, and an infinite regress follows. If one is internal and the other external, then that essence is composite, and the effect is one. As for the composition of that essence, it is because everything that has a part is composite. As for the effect being one, because what is internal to the essence is not an effect of it, because what is internal to the essence is prior to it in rank, and the effect is posterior, and the prior is not the posterior. The second argument: If from Him issued 'alif' and also issued 'ba', and 'ba' is not identical to 'alif', then it would be true that from the consideration from which 'alif' issued, 'alif' did not issue, because by that consideration 'ba' issued while 'ba' is not 'alif'. So it would be necessary to say: by the one consideration, 'alif' issued and did not issue. That is impossible. The third argument: Since we know that the nature of water necessitates coldness, and the nature of fire necessitates heating, we accordingly inferred from the difference of these two effects that the nature of water is different from the nature of fire. So if difference of effects indicates difference of the natures of the causes, then it is more fitting that it indicates their distinctness. The fourth argument: There must necessarily be a similarity and suitability between the cause and the effect. For knowledge is not a cause for being movable, and motion is not a cause for being knowledgeable, because there is no suitability between knowledge and being movable. As for knowledge, it necessitates being knowledgeable because there is suitability and similarity between them. This being established, we say: If one cause necessitated two different effects, then that one cause would be similar in one respect to two different things, and what is similar to different things is itself different. Thus that one would be contrary to itself, and that is impossible. The fifth argument: If a single necessitator goes beyond necessitating a single effect, it is not more appropriate for it to necessitate one number than another number. So that one cause would necessitate infinite effects, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents five arguments (hujaj) used by those who maintain that a cause (al-'illa) which necessitates (al-mujib bi al-dhat) cannot produce more than one effect (ma'lul). The author begins by noting that these arguments have been mentioned before alongside counter-arguments, so he will not repeat the refutations, but he lists the proofs for reference. The first argument is conceptual: if the cause is the source of two different effects (symbolized as 'alif' and 'ba'), then the concepts of being the source of each are distinct. If both concepts are internal to the essence of the cause, the cause becomes composite (since it contains two distinct aspects), which is impossible for a simple necessary being. If both are external, they are accidents dependent on the essence, leading to an infinite regress of concepts. If one is internal and the other external, the essence is composite and the effect is one (since the internal aspect is not an effect). Thus, in all cases, only one effect is possible. The second argument is a reductio: if two different effects issued from the same cause by the same consideration (i'tibar), then by that consideration one effect would both issue and not issue (since the other effect is different), a contradiction. The third argument uses analogy from natural phenomena: different effects (cold from water, heat from fire) indicate different natures (taba'i') of the causes. So if a single cause produced different effects, its nature would be different from itself, which is absurd. The fourth argument posits that there must be similarity (mushabaha) and suitability (munasaba) between cause and effect. For example, knowledge necessitates being knowledgeable, not being movable. Therefore, one cause cannot be similar to two different effects simultaneously, as that would require it to be both similar and dissimilar to itself. The fifth argument is that if a necessary cause could produce more than one effect, there would be no reason to limit the number, leading to an infinite number of effects, which is impossible. These arguments collectively aim to prove the necessitarian position that a single cause necessitates a single effect.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
علة
معلول
مفهوم
تقويم الماهية
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author endorses these arguments as correct.
Correction: The author notes that these arguments are from the necessitarians and have been refuted elsewhere. He presents them for reference, not as his own position.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, if both concepts (being a source of 'alif' and being a source of 'ba') are internal to the essence of the cause, what follows?
A) The cause is simple and necessary.
B) The cause becomes composite, which is impossible.
C) The cause produces both effects without contradiction.
D) The cause becomes dependent on the effects.

Key Takeaway
A simple necessary cause cannot be the source of two distinct effects without entailing composition, regress, or contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you attribute multiple roles to God in your daily thoughts—Provider, Healer, Judge—do you unconsciously picture Him as having separate 'parts' to handle each? How does this argument's warning against conceptual composition reveal a hidden shirk in your heart that you must repent from today?
?
If God's essence cannot contain distinct concepts of causation, then your salvation, your next breath, and every detail of your life come from the same indivisible act of His will. Are you truly relying on that one God alone, or do you still search for other 'causes' in your life? What will you change right now to make your trust purely in Him?
?
This argument exposes the absurdity of dividing the Creator into aspects. Look at your own life: where have you mentally split God's oneness—thinking one prayer for provision, another for forgiveness, another for guidance—as if He is a machine with different switches? How will you begin talking to Him as the singular, undivided Reality that He is, starting with your next prayer?

Second Argument: Contradiction in a Single Consideration
Presents the second argument using reductio to show that two effects cannot issue from one consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument that a single cause cannot produce two different effects by the same consideration, as that leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
A single consideration cannot result in both issuing and not issuing the same effect.

The second argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if a single cause produces two different effects by the same consideration (i'tibar), then by that consideration one effect both issues and does not issue, which is a logical contradiction. This argument emphasizes the need for a unified causal relation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reductio setup
Assume a single cause produces two distinct effects, A and B, by the same consideration. Then the consideration that produced A also produced B (which is not A).
2
Identify the contradiction
By that same consideration, since it produced B (not A), it did not produce A. So we say: by the one consideration, A issued and did not issue.
This is a violation of the principle of non-contradiction.
3
Conclude
Therefore, it is impossible for two different effects to proceed from a single cause by the same consideration.

Source Text

Discourse on the third chapter. As for the aforementioned arguments that the Truth is the Powerful and Free Chooser, not the necessitator by essence, they have already been mentioned with the doubts of those who hold the necessitator, so there is no benefit in repetition. Then those who hold the necessitator, who say that it is impossible for more than one effect to proceed from Him, have argued with proofs: The first: The concept of His being a source of 'alif' is different from the concept of His being a source of 'ba'. These two concepts: if they are internal to the constitution of the essence of the cause, then the cause would be composite, and that is impossible. If they are external to the constitution of the essence, then they are accidents of the essence, so they are dependent on it, so they are possible and known by that essence, so the aforementioned inquiry returns: the concept of that essence being a cause for one is different from the concept of it being a cause for the second, and an infinite regress follows. If one is internal and the other external, then that essence is composite, and the effect is one. As for the composition of that essence, it is because everything that has a part is composite. As for the effect being one, because what is internal to the essence is not an effect of it, because what is internal to the essence is prior to it in rank, and the effect is posterior, and the prior is not the posterior. The second argument: If from Him issued 'alif' and also issued 'ba', and 'ba' is not identical to 'alif', then it would be true that from the consideration from which 'alif' issued, 'alif' did not issue, because by that consideration 'ba' issued while 'ba' is not 'alif'. So it would be necessary to say: by the one consideration, 'alif' issued and did not issue. That is impossible. The third argument: Since we know that the nature of water necessitates coldness, and the nature of fire necessitates heating, we accordingly inferred from the difference of these two effects that the nature of water is different from the nature of fire. So if difference of effects indicates difference of the natures of the causes, then it is more fitting that it indicates their distinctness. The fourth argument: There must necessarily be a similarity and suitability between the cause and the effect. For knowledge is not a cause for being movable, and motion is not a cause for being knowledgeable, because there is no suitability between knowledge and being movable. As for knowledge, it necessitates being knowledgeable because there is suitability and similarity between them. This being established, we say: If one cause necessitated two different effects, then that one cause would be similar in one respect to two different things, and what is similar to different things is itself different. Thus that one would be contrary to itself, and that is impossible. The fifth argument: If a single necessitator goes beyond necessitating a single effect, it is not more appropriate for it to necessitate one number than another number. So that one cause would necessitate infinite effects, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents five arguments (hujaj) used by those who maintain that a cause (al-'illa) which necessitates (al-mujib bi al-dhat) cannot produce more than one effect (ma'lul). The author begins by noting that these arguments have been mentioned before alongside counter-arguments, so he will not repeat the refutations, but he lists the proofs for reference. The first argument is conceptual: if the cause is the source of two different effects (symbolized as 'alif' and 'ba'), then the concepts of being the source of each are distinct. If both concepts are internal to the essence of the cause, the cause becomes composite (since it contains two distinct aspects), which is impossible for a simple necessary being. If both are external, they are accidents dependent on the essence, leading to an infinite regress of concepts. If one is internal and the other external, the essence is composite and the effect is one (since the internal aspect is not an effect). Thus, in all cases, only one effect is possible. The second argument is a reductio: if two different effects issued from the same cause by the same consideration (i'tibar), then by that consideration one effect would both issue and not issue (since the other effect is different), a contradiction. The third argument uses analogy from natural phenomena: different effects (cold from water, heat from fire) indicate different natures (taba'i') of the causes. So if a single cause produced different effects, its nature would be different from itself, which is absurd. The fourth argument posits that there must be similarity (mushabaha) and suitability (munasaba) between cause and effect. For example, knowledge necessitates being knowledgeable, not being movable. Therefore, one cause cannot be similar to two different effects simultaneously, as that would require it to be both similar and dissimilar to itself. The fifth argument is that if a necessary cause could produce more than one effect, there would be no reason to limit the number, leading to an infinite number of effects, which is impossible. These arguments collectively aim to prove the necessitarian position that a single cause necessitates a single effect.

علة

C) The cause would be dependent on the effects.
D) The effects would be identical.

Key Takeaway
A single cause cannot produce two distinct effects by the same consideration without leading to a logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Third Argument: Analogy from Natural Natures
Presents the third argument using analogy from natural natures to show that one cause cannot produce different effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that different effects indicate different natures, so a single nature cannot produce multiple different effects.

Core Idea
Different effects prove different causes, so one cause cannot have multiple effects.

The third argument draws an analogy from natural phenomena: we observe that water's nature causes coldness, and fire's nature causes heat. From the difference in effects, we infer different natures. Therefore, if a single cause produced multiple different effects, its nature would have to be different from itself, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Understand the analogy
We know water's nature necessitates cold, fire's nature necessitates heat. The difference in effects leads us to conclude the natures are different.
This is a standard inductive inference.
2
Apply to the single cause case
If one cause produces two different effects, then by analogy, the cause would have to have two different natures, which is impossible for a single cause.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Difference of effects entails difference of causes, so a single cause cannot have multiple different effects.

Source Text

يمتنع أن يصدر عنه أكثر من معلول واحد. فقد احتجوا بوجوه: الأول: إن مفهوم كونه مصدراً للألف، مغاير للمفهوم من كونه مصدراً للباء. فهذان المفهومان إن كانا داخلين في تقويم ماهية العلة، لزم أن تكون العلة مركبة. وذلك محال. وإن كانا خارجين عن تقويم الماهية، كانا لاحقين للماهية، فكانا مفتقرين إليها، فكانا ممكنين ومعلمين بتلك الماهية، فيعود البحث المذكور من أن المفهوم من كون تلك الماهية علة لأحدهما، غير المفهوم من كونها علة للثاني. ويلزم التسلسل. وإن كان أحدهما داخلاً في الماهية، والآخر خارجاً عنها، لزم تركب تلك الماهية، ولزم كون المعلول واحداً. أما تركب تلك الماهية فهو أن كل ماله جزء فهو مركب. وأما كون المعلول واحداً، فلإن الداخل في الماهية لا يكون معلولاً لها، لأن الداخل في الماهية متقدم عليها بالرتبة. والمعلول متأخر، والمتقدم غير المتأخر. الحجة الثانية: إنه لو صدر عنه الألف، وصدر عنه الباء أيضاً. والباء ليس نفس الألف، فحينئذ يصدق أن من الاعتبار الذي صدر عنه الألف [لم يصدر عنه الألف] لأن بذلك الاعتبار صدر عنه الباء مع أن الباء ليس بالألف. فيلزم أن يقال: إنه بالاعتبار الواحد، صدر عنه [الألف] ولم يصدر عنه الألف. وذلك محال. الحجة الثالثة: إنا لما علمنا: أن طبيعة الماء توجب البرودة، وأن طبيعة النار توجب تسخين، لاجرم استدللنا باختلاف هذين الأثرين، على أن طبيعة الماء مخالفة لطبيعة النار. فلما دل اختلاف [الآثار على اختلاف] طبائع المؤثرات فبأن تدل على تغايرها [أولى]. الحجة الرابعة: إن العلة لا بد وأن تكون بينها وبين المعلول مشابهة ومناسبة. فإن العلم لا يكون علة للمتحركية، والحركة لا تكون علة للعالمية. لأنه ليس بين العلم وبين المتحركية مناسبة. وأما العلم فإنه يوجب العالمية، لأنه حصل بينهما مناسبة ومشابهة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو اقتضت العلة الواحدة معلولين مختلفين، لزم كون تلك العلة الواحدة متشابهة بالوجه الواحد، لشيئين مختلفين، والمشابه للمختلفين مختلف. فيلزم كون ذلك الواحد مخالفاً لنفسه. وإنه محال. الحجة الخامسة: إن الموجب الواحد، إذا تعدى عن إيجاب المعلول الواحد، لم يكن If one is internal and the other external, then that essence is composite, and the effect is one. As for the composition of that essence, it is because everything that has a part is composite. As for the effect being one, because what is internal to the essence is not an effect of it, because what is internal to the essence is prior to it in rank, and the effect is posterior, and the prior is not the posterior. So that one cause would necessitate infinite effects, and that is impossible.

Commentary
This passage presents five arguments (hujaj) used by those who maintain that a cause (al-'illa) which necessitates (al-mujib bi al-dhat) cannot produce more than one effect (ma'lul). The author begins by noting that these arguments have been mentioned before alongside counter-arguments, so he will not repeat the refutations, but he lists the proofs for reference. If both are external, they are accidents dependent on the essence, leading to an infinite regress of concepts. The fifth argument is that if a necessary cause could produce more than one effect, there would be no reason to limit the number, leading to an infinite number of effects, which is impossible. Fourth Argument: Suitability Between Cause and Effect
Presents the fourth argument that similarity between cause and effect prevents a single cause from having multiple different effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that a cause must be similar and suitable to its effect, so one cause cannot be suitable to two different effects.

Core Idea
A cause must resemble its effect; one thing cannot resemble two different things.

The fourth argument posits a necessary relation of similarity (mushabaha) and suitability (munasaba) between cause and effect. For example, knowledge causes being knowledgeable, not being movable. Therefore, if one cause produced two different effects, it would have to be similar to both, which would require it to be different from itself — a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of suitability
A cause and effect must share some similarity or suitability. Example: knowledge is suitable for being knowledgeable, not for being movable.
2
Apply to one cause with two effects
If one cause produced two different effects, it would have to be similar to each. But being similar to two different things makes the cause itself different from itself (since to be similar to X and to Y, if X and Y are different, the similarity relation must differ).
3
Conclude
This is impossible, so one cause cannot produce two different effects.

Source Text

Causality and Created Things

Fifth Argument: Impossibility of Infinite Effects
Presents the fifth argument that multiple effects would lead to an infinite regress, which is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that if a single necessary cause could produce more than one effect, it would lead to an infinite number of effects, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If one cause can produce multiple effects, there is no reason to stop at a finite number.

The fifth argument is a 'slippery slope' reasoning: if a single necessitator can produce more than one effect, there is no logical reason why it should produce exactly two or three rather than any other number. Since the cause is necessary and undetermined, it would have to produce an infinite number of effects, which is impossible. Thus, it must produce exactly one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of indifference
If a necessary cause does not have a limiting factor, it would have no reason to prefer one number of effects over another.
2
Apply to multiple effects
If it can produce more than one, then it could produce any number. Without a reason to stop, it would produce an infinite number.
3
Conclude
Infinite effects are impossible (presumably because they would lead to a chain without end), so the cause must produce only one effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الفصل الثالث. أما الوجوه المذكورة في أن الحق هو القادر المختار، لا الموجب بالذات. فقد تقدم ذكرها مع شبهات القائلين بالموجب. فلا فائدة في الإعادة. ثم إن القائلين بالموجب الذين يقولون: إنه يمتنع أن يصدر عنه أكثر من معلول واحد. فقد احتجوا بوجوه: الأول: إن مفهوم كونه مصدراً للألف، مغاير للمفهوم من كونه مصدراً للباء. فهذان المفهومان إن كانا داخلين في تقويم ماهية العلة، لزم أن تكون العلة مركبة. وذلك محال. وإن كانا خارجين عن تقويم الماهية، كانا لاحقين للماهية، فكانا مفتقرين إليها، فكانا ممكنين ومعلمين بتلك الماهية، فيعود البحث المذكور من أن المفهوم من كون تلك الماهية علة لأحدهما، غير المفهوم من كونها علة للثاني. ويلزم التسلسل. وإن كان أحدهما داخلاً في الماهية، والآخر خارجاً عنها، لزم تركب تلك الماهية، ولزم كون المعلول واحداً. أما تركب تلك الماهية فهو أن كل ماله جزء فهو مركب. وأما كون المعلول واحداً، فلإن الداخل في الماهية لا يكون معلولاً لها، لأن الداخل في الماهية متقدم عليها بالرتبة. والمعلول متأخر، والمتقدم غير المتأخر. الحجة الثانية: إنه لو صدر عنه الألف، وصدر عنه الباء أيضاً. والباء ليس نفس الألف، فحينئذ يصدق أن من الاعتبار الذي صدر عنه الألف [لم يصدر عنه الألف] لأن بذلك الاعتبار صدر عنه الباء مع أن الباء ليس بالألف. فيلزم أن يقال: إنه بالاعتبار الواحد، صدر عنه [الألف] ولم يصدر عنه الألف. وذلك محال. الحجة الثالثة: إنا لما علمنا: أن طبيعة الماء توجب البرودة، وأن طبيعة النار توجب تسخين، لاجرم استدللنا باختلاف هذين الأثرين، على أن طبيعة الماء مخالفة لطبيعة النار. فلما دل اختلاف [الآثار على اختلاف] طبائع المؤثرات فبأن تدل على تغايرها [أولى]. الحجة الرابعة: إن العلة لا بد وأن تكون بينها وبين المعلول مشابهة ومناسبة. فإن العلم لا يكون علة للمتحركية، والحركة لا تكون علة للعالمية. لأنه ليس بين العلم وبين المتحركية مناسبة. وأما العلم فإنه يوجب العالمية، لأنه حصل بينهما مناسبة ومشابهة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو اقتضت العلة الواحدة معلولين مختلفين، لزم كون تلك العلة الواحدة متشابهة بالوجه الواحد، لشيئين مختلفين، والمشابه للمختلفين مختلف. فيلزم كون ذلك الواحد مخالفاً لنفسه. وإنه محال. الحجة الخامسة: إن الموجب الواحد، إذا تعدى عن إيجاب المعلول الواحد، لم يكن بأن يوجب عدداً، أولى من أن يوجب عدداً آخر. فيلزم أن توجب تلك العلة الواحدة، معلولات لا نهاية لها. وذلك محال.
Discourse on the third chapter. As for the aforementioned arguments that the Truth is the Powerful and Free Chooser, not the necessitator by essence, they have already been mentioned with the doubts of those who hold the necessitator, so there is no benefit in repetition. Then those who hold the necessitator, who say that it is impossible for more than one effect to proceed from Him, have argued with proofs: The first: The concept of His being a source of 'alif' is different from the concept of His being a source of 'ba'. These two concepts: if they are internal to the constitution of the essence of the cause, then the cause would be composite, and that is impossible. If they are external to the constitution of the essence, then they are accidents of the essence, so they are dependent on it, so they are possible and known by that essence, so the aforementioned inquiry returns: the concept of that essence being a cause for one is different from the concept of it being a cause for the second, and an infinite regress follows. If one is internal and the other external, then that essence is composite, and the effect is one. As for the composition of that essence, it is because everything that has a part is composite. As for the effect being one, because what is internal to the essence is not an effect of it, because what is internal to the essence is prior to it in rank, and the effect is posterior, and the prior is not the posterior. The second argument: If from Him issued 'alif' and also issued 'ba', and 'ba' is not identical to 'alif', then it would be true that from the consideration from which 'alif' issued, 'alif' did not issue, because by that consideration 'ba' issued while 'ba' is not 'alif'. So it would be necessary to say: by the one consideration, 'alif' issued and did not issue. That is impossible. The third argument: Since we know that the nature of water necessitates coldness, and the nature of fire necessitates heating, we accordingly inferred from the difference of these two effects that the nature of water is different from the nature of fire. So if difference of effects indicates difference of the natures of the causes, then it is more fitting that it indicates their distinctness. The fourth argument: There must necessarily be a similarity and suitability between the cause and the effect. For knowledge is not a cause for being movable, and motion is not a cause for being knowledgeable, because there is no suitability between knowledge and being movable. As for knowledge, it necessitates being knowledgeable because there is suitability and similarity between them. This being established, we say: If one cause necessitated two different effects, then that one cause would be similar in one respect to two different things, and what is similar to different things is itself different. Thus that one would be contrary to itself, and that is impossible. The fifth argument: If a single necessitator goes beyond necessitating a single effect, it is not more appropriate for it to necessitate one number than another number. So that one cause would necessitate infinite effects, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage presents five arguments (hujaj) used by those who maintain that a cause (al-'illa) which necessitates (al-mujib bi al-dhat) cannot produce more than one effect (ma'lul). The author begins by noting that these arguments have been mentioned before alongside counter-arguments, so he will not repeat the refutations, but he lists the proofs for reference. The first argument is conceptual: if the cause is the source of two different effects (symbolized as 'alif' and 'ba'), then the concepts of being the source of each are distinct. If both concepts are internal to the essence of the cause, the cause becomes composite (since it contains two distinct aspects), which is impossible for a simple necessary being. If both are external, they are accidents dependent on the essence, leading to an infinite regress of concepts. If one is internal and the other external, the essence is composite and the effect is one (since the internal aspect is not an effect). Thus, in all cases, only one effect is possible. The second argument is a reductio: if two different effects issued from the same cause by the same consideration (i'tibar), then by that consideration one effect would both issue and not issue (since the other effect is different), a contradiction. The third argument uses analogy from natural phenomena: different effects (cold from water, heat from fire) indicate different natures (taba'i') of the causes. So if a single cause produced different effects, its nature would be different from itself, which is absurd. The fourth argument posits that there must be similarity (mushabaha) and suitability (munasaba) between cause and effect. For example, knowledge necessitates being knowledgeable, not being movable. Therefore, one cause cannot be similar to two different effects simultaneously, as that would require it to be both similar and dissimilar to itself. The fifth argument is that if a necessary cause could produce more than one effect, there would be no reason to limit the number, leading to an infinite number of effects, which is impossible. These arguments collectively aim to prove the necessitarian position that a single cause necessitates a single effect.

Key Terms
موجب واحد
معلول
عدد
لا نهاية

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, if a single necessitator could produce more than one effect, what would follow?
A) It would produce exactly two effects.
B) It would produce no effects.
C) It would produce an infinite number of effects.
D) It would produce effects randomly.

Key Takeaway
If a necessary cause could produce multiple effects, the lack of a limiting reason would force an infinite number of effects, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the divine attributes, do you ever assume that God's power allows Him to create multiple things without a specific reason? How does this argument challenge you to trust that every act of creation has a wise purpose?
?
Reflect on your own decision-making: how often do you fail to limit your actions by a sufficient reason, leading to unnecessary complications? What would change if you applied the principle of sufficient reason to your daily choices?
?
If you truly believe that God's necessary essence dictates a single necessary effect, how does that affect your understanding of God's freedom and your own dependence on Him? Are you willing to let go of the notion that God 'chooses' arbitrarily?

Second Proof: One Cause Necessitates One Effect
Presents the second proof that one cause necessitates only one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that a single cause, under one consideration, cannot produce two distinct effects.

Core Idea
One cause, one effect: the impossibility of producing two under the same respect.

This lesson presents the second proof from the text, which demonstrates through a logical contradiction that one cause cannot give rise to two different effects. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum, using simple symbols 'alif' and 'ba' to represent distinct effects. As you read, pay attention to how the contradiction arises from the assumption that the cause produces both effects under the same consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument settle in your mind before analyzing it.
Notice the repetition of 'alif' – the contradiction hinges on it.
2
Identify the assumption and the contradiction
The argument assumes one cause produces two effects. Trace how this leads to the contradictory statement: 'by the same consideration, it produced alif and did not produce alif.'
3
Understand the role of 'consideration' (اعتبار)
The key term 'اعتبار' refers to the specific aspect or respect under which the cause acts. The contradiction arises because the same respect cannot yield two different effects.
Different effects would require different considerations.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since affirming a contradiction is impossible, the original assumption must be false. Therefore, one cause in one respect can only produce one effect.
This proof applies to all efficient causes, including divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إنه لو صدر عنه الألف ، وصدر عنه الباء أيضاً . والباء ليس نفس الألف ، فحينئذ يصدق أن من الاعتبار الذي صدر عنه الألف لم يصدر عنه الألف لأن بذلك الاعتبار صدر عنه الباء مع أن الباء ليس بالألف . فيلزم أن يقال : إنه بالاعتبار الواحد ، صدر عنه الألف ولم يصدر عنه الألف . وذلك محال .
The second proof: If the one (cause) produced 'alif' and also produced 'ba', and 'ba' is not the same as 'alif', then it would be true that with respect to the consideration by which it produced 'alif', it did not produce 'alif', because by that consideration it produced 'ba' while 'ba' is not 'alif'. Thus it necessarily follows that it is said: by one consideration, it produced 'alif' and did not produce 'alif'. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that one cause cannot produce more than one effect. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum. It assumes a single cause (العلة الواحدة) producing two different effects, symbolized as 'alif' and 'ba'. The key idea is that the cause must produce each effect under a specific consideration (اعتبار). If it produces both, then under the same consideration by which it produces 'alif', it must also produce 'ba', which is not 'alif'. This implies that under that consideration, it both produces 'alif' (since it does produce it) and does not produce 'alif' (since it produces 'ba' instead). This contradiction shows the impossibility of one cause producing two different effects. The proof relies on the principle that an action cannot be simultaneously affirmed and denied in the same respect.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
اعتبار
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One cause can produce multiple different effects under the exact same respect.
Correction: The proof shows this leads to a logical contradiction. A cause can produce multiple effects only under different considerations or aspects.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, what contradiction arises if one cause produces two different effects?
A) The cause would be both eternal and temporal.
B) The same effect would be both produced and not produced in the same respect.
C) The cause would have to be identical to its effect.
D) The effects would be indistinguishable.

Key Takeaway
One cause, under one consideration, cannot produce two distinct effects because that would entail a logical contradiction.

Third Proof: Analogy from Natural Kinds
Argues from the differing effects of natural essences to the impossibility of one cause producing two effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the differing effects of natural kinds (like water and fire) demonstrate the impossibility of a single cause producing two distinct effects.

Core Idea
Distinct effects prove distinct causes; one cause cannot produce two effects.

This passage presents the third proof in a series of arguments for the oneness of the necessary being. By observing that water's nature necessitates coldness and fire's nature necessitates heat, we infer that the natures themselves are different. The argument then generalizes: if differing effects indicate differing causes, it is even more evident that one cause cannot produce two different effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and English side by side to get the flow of the argument. Notice the specific example of water and fire.
The Arabic uses the terms طبيعة (nature) and أثر (effect).
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument has two parts: a concrete example (water and fire) and a general principle. In the example, different effects (cold vs. heat) lead to the conclusion that the natures are different. Then the principle is stated: since differing effects prove differing causes, it is even more certain that a single cause cannot produce two effects.
3
Consider the implication
Reflect on why the author says 'all the more indicative' (أولى). If effects differ, the causes must differ; if one cause were to produce two effects, it would require it to have two different natures, which is impossible.
This supports the broader theological argument against multiple gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا لما علمنا : أن طبيعة الماء توجب البرودة ، وأن طبيعة النار توجب التسخين، لاجرم استدللنا باختلاف هذين الأثرين ، على أن طبيعة الماء ، مخالفة لطبيعة النار . فلما دل اختلاف الآثار على اختلاف طبائع المؤثرات فبأن تدل على تغايرها أولى .
The third proof: Since we know that the nature (tabīʿa) of water necessitates coldness, and the nature of fire necessitates heating, we inevitably inferred from the difference of these two effects that the nature of water is different from the nature of fire. So when the difference of effects indicates the difference of the natures of the effectors (muʾthirāt), then it is all the more indicative of their distinctness.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, which draws an analogy from observable natural phenomena. The author observes that water's nature causes coldness and fire's nature causes heat. Because these effects are different, we conclude that the natures (طبائع) themselves are different. More generally, if differing effects prove differing causes, then it is even more evident that a single cause cannot produce two different effects—otherwise its nature would have to be both similar to two different things, which is impossible. The proof uses the principle that distinct effects require distinct causes.

Key Terms
طبيعة
أثر
مؤثر
تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that every difference in effect proves a difference in cause in all cases.
Correction: The argument applies when effects are essentially different, as in the example of water and fire. Not every accidental difference implies a difference in cause, but when effects are fundamentally opposed, their causes must be fundamentally distinct.

Checkpoint

A) Because the similarity of effects would indicate similarity of causes.
B) Because the difference of effects proves the difference of causes, and it is even clearer that one cause cannot produce two effects.
C) Because water and fire are opposites.
D) Because effects are more important than causes.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that distinct effects in nature require distinct causes, ruling out the possibility that one cause could produce essentially different effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The nature of water is fixed to cause cold, and fire to cause heat. Is your inner nature fixed on pleasing Allah, or do you waver between obedience and disobedience? What one effect should your faith (īmān) necessarily produce in your life?
?
If you justify your sins by saying 'I am weak' or 'I have good intentions,' are you not claiming that a single cause (yourself) can produce both righteous deeds and sinful acts? How does this passage compel you to seek a single, pure intention (niyyah) that consistently yields good effects?

Fourth Proof: A Single Cause Cannot Produce Two Effects
Argument that a cause must be similar to its effect, and a single cause cannot have two different effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single cause can only produce one specific effect, based on the principle of similarity and appropriateness between cause and effect.

Core Idea
A cause must be similar to its effect; one cause cannot be similar to two different things.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from the text, which builds on a fundamental principle of causality: between a cause and its effect, there must be a real connection of similarity and appropriateness. The author illustrates this with examples from knowledge and motion, then argues that if one cause produced two different effects, it would have to be similar to both, leading to a contradiction. This proof reinforces the unity of causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument. Notice the logical structure: first the principle, then examples, then the reductio.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the causal principle
Identify the key condition: a cause must have similarity (mushābaha) and appropriateness (munāsaba) with its effect. This is the foundation of the proof.
3
Examine the examples
Note how knowledge (ʿilm) is a cause for knowledge-possession (ʿālamiyya) but not for motility (mutaḥarrikiyya). Why? Because appropriateness exists in the first pair but not the second.
This illustrates the principle clearly.
4
Follow the reductio argument
Assume a single cause produces two different effects. Then it must be similar to both. But similarity to two different things implies the cause would have contradictory properties, which is impossible. Hence the assumption is false.
This is the core of the proof.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Recognize why this supports the idea that each cause has a single effect. This is a key argument for the unity of causation in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن العلة لا بد وأن تكون بينها وبين المعلول مشابهة ومناسبة . فإن العلم لا يكون علة للمتحركية ، والحركة لا تكون علة للعالمية . لأنه ليس بين العلم وبين المتحركية مناسبة . وأما العلم فإنه يوجب العالمية ، لأنه حصل بينهما مناسبة ومشابهة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو اقتضت العلة الواحدة معلولين مختلفين، لزم كون تلك العلة الواحدة متشابهة بالوجه الواحد ، لشيئين مختلفين، والمشابه للمختلفين ، مختلف . فيلزم كون ذلك الواحد مخالفاً لنفسه . وإنه محال .
The fourth proof: Indeed, between a cause (ʿilla) and its effect (maʿlūl) there must be similarity (mushābaha) and appropriateness (munāsaba). For knowledge (al-ʿilm) is not a cause for motility (al-mutaḥarrikiyya), and motion (al-ḥaraka) is not a cause for knowledge (al-ʿālimiyya), because there is no appropriateness between knowledge and motility. But knowledge necessitates knowledge-possession (al-ʿālamiyya) because there is appropriateness and similarity between them. If this is established, then we say: if one cause necessitated two different effects, then that one cause would be similar in one respect to two different things, and what is similar to two different things is itself different. Thus that one would be contradictory to itself, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, which is based on the principle that a cause must have a specific similarity and appropriateness (مشابهة ومناسبة) with its effect. The author gives an example: knowledge (العلم) cannot cause motility (المتحركية) because there is no appropriateness between them; rather, knowledge causes knowledge-possession (العالمية) because they are similar. If one cause were to produce two different effects, it would have to be similar in the same respect to both effects. But being similar to two different things makes the cause itself different (in that it would have contradictory properties), implying that the one cause would be contradictory to itself, which is impossible. Therefore, one cause can only have one effect.

معلول
مشابهة
مناسبة
علم
عالمية
متحركية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause can produce multiple different effects without any special connection.
Correction: The proof shows that a cause must have a specific similarity and appropriateness with its effect; producing two different effects would require the cause to have contradictory properties.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why can knowledge not be the cause of motility?
A) Because knowledge is abstract and motility is physical.
B) Because there is no appropriateness (مناسبة) between them.
C) Because knowledge is a cause of many things.
D) Because motility is a type of movement.

Key Takeaway
A single cause can only produce one specific effect because it must be similar to its effect, and similarity to two different effects is contradictory.

Fifth Proof: One Cause Cannot Produce Multiple Effects
Fifth proof using principle of indifference to show one cause cannot produce multiple effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof that a single necessitating cause cannot produce more than one effect because it would lead to an infinite and impossible number of effects.

Core Idea
One cause with multiple effects leads to an infinite regress of effects, which is impossible.

This lesson presents the fifth proof that a single necessitating cause cannot produce more than one effect. The argument is based on the principle of indifference: if a cause could produce multiple effects, there would be no reason for it to produce a specific finite number, so it would have to produce an infinite number, which is absurd. Therefore, a cause must produce exactly one effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the logical structure. Note the key terms: موجب, إيجاب, معلول.
2
Understand the principle of indifference
The proof uses the idea that if a cause has no preference, it cannot choose one number over another. Reflect on why this leads to infinite effects.
This is a common argument in kalam and philosophy.
3
Identify the conclusion
The proof concludes that the cause would necessitate infinite effects, which is impossible. Therefore, the original assumption (that a cause can produce more than one effect) is false.
4
Check your understanding with the quiz
Take the checkpoint quiz to ensure you grasp the main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الموجب الواحد ، إذا تعدى عن إيجاب المعلول الواحد ، لم يكن بأن يوجب عدداً ، أولى من أن يوجب عدداً آخر. فيلزم أن توجب تلك العلة الواحدة ، معلولات لا نهاية لها . وذلك محال .
The fifth proof: If one necessitator (mūjib) goes beyond necessitating one effect, it is not that it should necessitate one number rather than another number. Thus it would necessitate that that one cause necessitates infinite effects. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, which is based on the principle of indifference. If a single cause (الموجب الواحد) can produce more than one effect, then there is no reason why it would produce a specific number of effects (e.g., two) rather than any other number (e.g., three). By the principle that a cause with no preference would not choose one number over another, it would have to produce an infinite number of effects, which is impossible. Thus, the cause must produce exactly one effect.

Key Terms
موجب
إيجاب
معلول
لا نهاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that if a cause can produce multiple effects, it must be able to produce any number.
Correction: The argument is about the lack of sufficient reason for a particular finite number, not about the ability to produce any number.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument in the fifth proof?
A) One cause cannot produce two effects because effects must be infinite.
B) If a cause can produce more than one effect, there is no reason for it to stop at any finite number, leading to infinite effects.
C) A cause that produces multiple effects would be imperfect.
D) The number of effects must equal the number of causes.

Key Takeaway
A single necessitating cause cannot produce multiple distinct effects because it would have no reason to choose any finite number, leading to an impossible infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if your actions have no particular reason, simply moving from one thing to another without purpose? How does this proof challenge you to ensure that every effect you produce has a sufficient reason?
?
Reflect on a situation where you assumed one cause was responsible for multiple outcomes. How might this proof expose a lack of justification in your thinking?
?
If every cause must have a specific reason for its effect, what does that demand of you in your daily choices? Are you willing to examine the reasons behind your actions and eliminate arbitrary effects?

Four Refutations of the First Proof
Lists four counterexamples to the first proof that one cause cannot produce one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four refutations expose the flaws in the first proof by showing that its reasoning leads to absurdities when applied to examples like the center of a circle, unity, simple essence, and prime matter.

Core Idea
Four counterexamples reveal that the first proof's logic is self-defeating.

After presenting the first proof that one cause cannot produce more than one effect, the author now offers four refutations (نقوض) that challenge the proof's reasoning. These examples—the center of a circle, the number one, a simple essence, and prime matter—show that if the proof's logic were applied consistently, it would lead to unacceptable consequences, thereby invalidating the proof itself. As you read, notice how each refutation follows a similar pattern: it identifies a case where a single thing seems to produce multiple distinct effects, then shows that the proof's reasoning would imply a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the four refutations
Read the Arabic passage twice: once for overall structure, then again with the translation. Identify the four separate examples given.
2
Analyze the first refutation
Examine the example of the center of a circle being opposite to multiple points. Understand how the author argues that the concepts of these oppositions lead to a dilemma: either the point is composite or the concepts are effects, leading to an infinite regress.
3
Analyze the remaining three
Work through the second, third, and fourth refutations: unity, simple essence, and prime matter. Notice that each follows the same pattern: a single thing has multiple distinct 'effects' (concepts), and the proof's logic would force an absurd conclusion.
4
Understand the common pattern
Realize that all four refutations share a similar structure: they show that the first proof's reasoning, if applied beyond its original context, leads to contradictions or impossibilities. This strengthens the case that the proof itself is unsound.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider that these refutations do not deny the principle that one cause produces one effect; they merely show that the first proof's specific argument is flawed. This is a typical dialectical move in Islamic theology: refuting a proof doesn't necessarily refute the proposition it was meant to support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الحجة الأولى منقوضة بصور كثيرة :
فالنقض الأول : إن النقطة التي هي مركز الدائرة ، محاذية لجميع النقط المفترضة في الدائرة. فنقول : المفهوم من كون ذلك المركز محاذياً لهذه النقطة المفترضة في الدائرة غير المفهوم من كون ذلك المركز محاذياً للنقطة الأخرى. فهذان المفهومان، إن كانا مقومين الماهية النقطة لزم كونها مركبة، وإن كانا خارجين كانا لاحقين وممكنين ومعلولين، فيعود البحث في أن المفهوم من كون تلك النقطة مصدراً لأحدهما ، غير المفهوم من كونها مصدراً للثاني . ويعود الكلام بتمامه .
النقض الثاني : إن الوحدة إذا ضم إليها وحدة ، صارت مبدأ لهذين الاثنين . وإذا ضم إليهما وحدة أخرى صارت مبدأ للاثنين الثاني . فنقول : مفهوم كونها مبدأ لهذين الاثنين غير مفهوم كونها مبدأ للاثنين الثاني . ويعود التقسيم بتمامه فيه . فيلزم كون الوحدة منقسمة مركبة .
النقض الثالث : لا شك أن كل ماهية بسيطة، فإنه يسلب كل ما عداها عنها ، فالمفهوم من كونها بحيث يسلب عنها الألف مثلا ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونها بحيث يسلب عنها الباء. ويعود التقسيم المذكور فيه ، فيلزم : أن لا يسلب عن الواحد إلا الواحد . وإنه باطل .
النقض الرابع : المعهوم من كون الهيولى قابلة لهذه الصورة ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونها قابلة للصورة الثانية ، ويعود التقسيم بتمامه فيه . فيلزم : أن يقال : الهيولى لا يقبل إلا صورة واحدة .
Know that the first proof is refuted by many examples:
The first refutation: The point that is the center of a circle is opposite (muḥādhiya) to all the assumed points in the circle. So we say: the concept of that center being opposite to this assumed point in the circle is different from the concept of that center being opposite to another point. So these two concepts, if they are constitutive of the essence of the point, it would be composite; and if they are extraneous, they would be accidents, possibles, and effects, and the inquiry returns as to whether the concept of that point being a source for one of them is different from the concept of it being a source for the second. And the entire discussion returns.
The second refutation: When unity is combined with another unity, it becomes a principle for these two. And if another unity is added to them, it becomes a principle for the second two. So we say: the concept of it being a principle for these two is different from the concept of it being a principle for the second two. And the same division applies to it. So it would necessitate that unity is divisible and composite.
The third refutation: There is no doubt that every simple essence (māhiyya basīṭa) is such that everything other than it is negated from it. So the concept of it being such that 'alif' is negated from it is different from the concept of it being such that 'ba' is negated from it. And the aforementioned division applies to it, necessitating that only one thing be negated from the one. But that is false.
The fourth refutation: The concept of matter (hayūlā) being receptive to this form is different from the concept of it being receptive to the second form, and the same division applies to it. It would necessitate that it be said: matter accepts only one form.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents four refutations (نقوض) against the first proof (which argued that one cause cannot produce more than one effect). The author lists counterexamples where a single entity seems to produce multiple effects, but the refutations show that the reasoning of the first proof would lead to absurd conclusions if applied consistently.

The first refutation uses the example of a point at the center of a circle being opposite to every point on the circumference. The concept of its opposition to one point is different from its opposition to another. If these concepts were part of the point's essence, the point would be composite; if they are accidents, they are effects, and we ask whether the point as source of one concept differs from it as source of another. This leads to an infinite regress.

The second refutation concerns the number one (الوحدة). When added to another one, it becomes a principle for the two; adding a third makes it a principle for the second two. The concept of being a principle for the first two is different from being a principle for the second two, implying that unity is composite.

The third refutation involves a simple essence from which everything else is negated. The concept of negating 'alif' is different from negating 'ba', which would mean only one negation can apply, but this is false.

The fourth refutation involves prime matter (الهيولى) receiving different forms. The concepts of receptivity to different forms are distinct, which would absurdly imply matter can only receive one form.

These refutations aim to show that the first proof's logic, if applied universally, leads to absurdities, indicating a flaw in its reasoning.

Key Terms
نقض
حجة
نقطة
مركز
وحدة
ماهية بسيطة
هيولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutations disprove the principle that one cause produces one effect.
Correction: The refutations only show the first proof is flawed; the principle itself remains intact and is supported by other arguments.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the refutations presented?
A) To prove that one cause can produce many effects.
B) To show that the first proof is flawed because it leads to absurd conclusions when applied to other cases.
C) To argue that the center of a circle is composite.
D) To reject the principle of causality altogether.

Key Takeaway
The four refutations demonstrate that the first proof's reasoning, if applied consistently, leads to absurdities, thus invalidating that particular argument without necessarily rejecting the underlying causal principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The refutations show that even a seemingly solid proof can be dismantled. Are there beliefs in your faith that you hold without ever critically examining their logical foundations? Will you commit to seeking sound knowledge, even if it means admitting your reasoning is flawed?
?
The center of a circle is opposite to many points at once. Do you ever limit God's power in your mind, thinking He can only handle one thing at a time? How can this lesson deepen your trust in His ability to manage all affairs simultaneously?
?
The proof assumed one cause cannot produce one effect, but the refutations expose its faulty logic. Are you willing to submit your own intellectual pride to the clear texts of revelation, rather than relying solely on your own deductions about God?

Introduction to the Refutation of the One-Cause Principle
Author announces refutation of first proof of one-cause-one-effect principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author sets up his refutation of the one-cause-one-effect principle by announcing that the first proof is subject to many counterexamples.

Core Idea
A single refutation can dismantle an entire proof.

The author has just summarized the arguments of those who claim that one cause produces only one effect. Now he transitions to the critical phase: refutation. He begins by declaring that the first of those arguments is already broken by numerous counterexamples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2
Understand 'naqd' (refutation)
The term 'منقوضة' comes from 'نقض', meaning to refute by showing a counterexample. In kalam, a naqd is a powerful tool to invalidate a proof.
3
Why start with the first proof?
The author begins with the first proof likely because it is foundational. By refuting it, he undermines the whole argument. Consider the strategic placement.
4
Reflect on the method
The author uses 'بصور كثيرة' (many counterexamples). This indicates that the refutation is not just one but multiple, strengthening his critique.
We will see these counterexamples in subsequent lessons.

The Center Point Objection
First objection: center point of a circle with multiple alignments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of the center point of a circle challenges the principle that one cause produces only one effect, and why this objection leads to a regress.

Core Idea
One simple point can have multiple distinct relations, undermining the one-cause-one-effect principle.

In this lesson, we examine the first of several objections (naqd) raised against the claim that a single cause cannot produce multiple effects. The author uses a geometric example—the center point of a circle—to show that a simple entity can have multiple distinct relations. This concrete case tests the logical consistency of the causal principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's setup
Read the passage carefully. The center point of a circle is aligned to every point on the circumference. This single point has multiple alignments.
The word 'aligned' (محاذية) here indicates a relation.
2
Identify the two concepts
The concept of being aligned to one specific point is different from the concept of being aligned to another point. These are two distinct relational concepts.
The author calls them 'two concepts' (هذان المفهومان).
3
Analyze the two possibilities
The author examines two options: either these concepts are part of the essence of the point, or they are external. If part of essence, the point becomes composite. If external, they are accidents, possible, and caused, leading to a regress when asking about their source.
4
See the conclusion
Thus, the same reasoning used to attack multiple effects applies here. The objection shows that the one-cause-one-effect principle fails.
The argument returns to the original problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالنقض الأول : إن النقطة التي هي مركز الدائرة ، محاذية لجميع النقط المفترضة في الدائرة. فنقول المفهوم من كون ذلك المركز محاذياً لهذه النقطة المفترضة في الدائرة غير المفهوم من كون المركز محاذياً للنقطة الأخرى. فهذان المفهومان، إن كانا مقومين الماهية النقطة لزم كونها مركبة، وإن كانا خارجين كانا لاحقين وممكنين ومعلولين، فيعود البحث في أن المفهوم من كون تلك النقطة مصدراً لأحدهما ، غير المفهوم من كونها مصدراً للثاني . ويعود الكلام بتمامه
The first objection: The point that is the center of the circle is aligned to all the assumed points in the circle. We say: The concept of that center being aligned to this assumed point in the circle is other than the concept of the center being aligned to the other point. These two concepts, if they are constitutive of the essence of the point, then it would be composite; and if they are external, then they are accidents, possible, and effects, so the inquiry returns that the concept of that point being a source for one of them is other than the concept of it being a source for the second. And the entire argument returns.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (نقض) against the claim that one cause produces only one effect. The example is the center point of a circle: it has multiple alignments to different points on the circumference. The author argues that the concept of alignment to one point is distinct from alignment to another. If these concepts were part of the essence of the point, the point would be composite (contradicting its simplicity). If they are external, then they are accidental attributes that are possible and caused, leading to an infinite regress when asking about the source of these attributes. Thus, the same reasoning used to refute multiple effects from one cause applies here, showing the principle is false.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think the objection is about geometry, but it is about the logical implications of multiple relations from a simple entity.
Correction: The argument uses geometry as a concrete example to test the metaphysical principle of causality, not to discuss geometric properties themselves.

Checkpoint

C) The movement of a body.

Key Takeaway
The center point objection shows that a simple entity can have multiple distinct relational concepts, which challenges the one-cause-one-effect principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Does it reconcile the oneness of God with the diversity of the world, or does it raise deeper questions? Take a moment to write down one tension you still feel and commit to seeking an answer.
?
If you treat the concepts of alignment as accidental, you admit that the simple point causes multiple effects. But the objection then falls into a regress. This mirrors the Kalam argument that an infinite regress must stop at a necessary being. Have you ever felt an uneasy infinity when thinking about causes? How does this lesson make you more aware of the need for a foundational cause in your own reasoning?
?
Imagine you are explaining God's creation to a skeptic. They say: 'If God is one, how can He create many things?' Would you use this center point objection? What would you say? What does your comfort or discomfort with using this analogy reveal about your own understanding of God's attribute of causality?

Objection: Unit as Principle for Distinct Pairs
Second objection challenging the simplicity of the unit by showing it must be composite to be principle for distinct pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection argues that a single unit cannot be a principle for multiple distinct pairs without becoming composite.

Core Idea
A unit that is source for two different pairs must itself be divisible.

This lesson examines the second objection against the idea that a simple unit can be the principle of only one effect. The objector shows that when we consider a unit as the principle for one pair and then for another distinct pair, the unit would have to have multiple distinct attributes, thus being composite. This challenges the premise that a single cause produces a single effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical steps of the objection.
Notice the structure: premise, premise, conclusion.
2
Identify the logical structure
Break down the argument: first premise (unit + unit = principle for two), second premise (unit + another unit = principle for second two), then the key inference that the concepts differ, leading to the conclusion that the unit is divisible.
The conclusion is that the unit would be 'munqasima murakkaba' (divisible, composite).
3
Connect to the broader debate
Understand that this objection targets the principle that a single cause produces a single effect. The unit here is an analogue for a simple cause.
This objection shows that if a simple thing can be the source of multiple distinct effects, it must have parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنقض الثاني : إن الوحدة إذا ضم إليها وحدة ، صارت مبدأ لهذين الاثنين . وإذا ضم إليهما وحدة أخرى صارت مبدأ للاثنين الثاني . فنقول : مفهوم كونها مبدأ لهذين الاثنين غير مفهوم كونها مبدأ للاثنين الثاني ويعود التقسيم بتمامه فيه . فيلزم كون الوحدة منقسمة مركبة .
The second objection: When a unit is added to a unit, it becomes a principle for these two. And when another unit is added to them, it becomes a principle for the second two. We say: The concept of it being a principle for these two is other than the concept of it being a principle for the second two, and the entire division returns in it. So it follows that the unit is divisible and composite.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection. The author takes the concept of a unit (الوحدة). When one unit is added to another, it becomes the source of the number two. When another unit is added, it becomes the source of a different pair. The concept of being a source for the first pair is different from being a source for the second pair. This implies that the unit itself would have multiple distinct attributes, making it composite and divisible—contradicting its simplicity. Thus, if the one-cause principle were applied, the unit could only produce one effect, but here it produces multiple distinct effects, refuting the claim.
