The Third Objection: Simple Essence and Negations
The author argues that a simple essence negates multiple things, distinct concepts arise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection against the one-cause principle uses the example of a simple essence negating multiple things to show that the principle leads to an absurd conclusion.

Core Idea
A simple essence negates many things, so one cause cannot produce only one effect.

The author now presents the third objection to the one-cause principle. He considers a simple essence from which everything else is negated. The objection argues that such an essence would have multiple distinct negations, which would violate the principle that one cause produces only one effect. This forces the opponent to accept an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the structure of the argument. Notice how the author sets up the premise about a simple essence.
A simple essence (ماهية بسيطة) is one that has no parts.
2
Identify the core premise
The key premise: every simple essence negates everything other than itself. Consider how this leads to multiple distinct concepts of negation.
3
Follow the reduction to absurdity
The objection then applies the one-cause principle: if the essence is the cause of its own negations, it would have to produce only one negation. But it negates many things – a contradiction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the one-cause principle fails because it leads to the false claim that only one thing is negated from a simple essence.
This shows the principle cannot hold for simple essences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثالث : لا شك أن كل ماهية بسيطة، فإنه يسلب كل ما عداها عنها ، فالمفهوم من كونها بحيث يسلب عنها الألف مثلا ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونها بحيث يسلب عنها الباء. ويعود التقسيم المذكور فيه ، فيلزم : أن لا يسلب عن الواحد إلا الواحد . وإنه باطل .
The third objection: There is no doubt that every simple essence has everything other than it negated from it. So the concept of it being such that 'alif' is negated from it, for example, is different from the concept of it being such that 'ba' is negated from it. And the mentioned division returns in it, so it follows that only one thing should be negated from the one. And that is false.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. The author considers a simple essence (ماهية بسيطة) from which all other things are negated. For example, the concept of it not being A is distinct from the concept of it not being B. According to the one-cause principle, if the essence were the cause of these negations, it would need to produce multiple distinct effects (the negations). To avoid this, one would have to say that only one negation can be attributed to a simple essence, which is obviously false because a simple essence negates infinitely many things. Thus, the principle fails.

Key Terms
ماهية بسيطة
سلب
مفهوم
واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a simple essence, being one, can only have one negation.
Correction: In reality, a simple essence negates all things other than itself, resulting in many distinct negations.

Checkpoint

A) The essence would be composite.
B) Only one negation could be attributed to it.
C) The essence would cause all negations.
D) Negations are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The one-cause principle fails because a simple essence negates many things, not just one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you strive to let your heart be filled with a unified awareness of Him, beyond discrete thoughts?
?

?
The argument shows that logical reasoning about simple essences leads to a contradiction for the one-cause principle. Does that humble your trust in pure reason when speaking about the Divine? How will you balance rational inquiry with humble acceptance of revelation?

Fourth Objection: Prime Matter Receiving Multiple Forms
The author uses prime matter receiving multiple forms as a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of prime matter receiving different forms provides a counterexample to the one-cause principle.

Core Idea
If one cause can only have one effect, then prime matter could only receive one form – which is false.

The author now presents a specific counterexample from physics: prime matter (hyle) is said to be receptive to multiple distinct forms. The concept of its receptivity to one form is conceptually distinct from its receptivity to another. If the one-cause principle were true, then prime matter, as a single cause, could only produce one effect – i.e., be receptive to only one form. But in reality, prime matter receives many forms, so the principle fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: 'The concept of X is different from the concept of Y, so the division returns...'
2
Identify the concepts
Identify the two concepts: 'being receptive to this form' and 'being receptive to that form'. They are distinct.
3
Understand the logical move
The author says 'the entire division returns' meaning the same principle (one cause → one effect) applies here. Then the conclusion is that hyle would only accept one form.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Assess the counterexample
Ask yourself: Does this refute the one-cause principle? How does this example show the principle leads to a false consequence?
The conclusion contradicts observed reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الرابع : المعهوم من كون الهيولى قابلة لهذه الصورة ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونها قابلة للصورة الثانية ، ويعود التقسيم بتمامه فيه . فيلزم : أن يقال : الهيولى لا يقبل إلا صورة واحدة .
The fourth objection: The concept of hyle (prime matter) being receptive to this form is different from the concept of it being receptive to the second form, and the entire division returns in it. So it follows that it should be said: Hyle does not accept except one form.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection using the concept of hyle (الهيولى, prime matter). Prime matter is said to be receptive to various forms. The concept of its receptivity to form A is different from its receptivity to form B. If the one-cause principle were applied, hyle could only be receptive to one form, because multiple receptivities would require multiple effects from a single cause (the hyle). But in reality, hyle can receive many forms successively, so the principle is false.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة
قابلية
مفهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The one-cause principle is universally valid and applies to all causes.
Correction: This counterexample shows that one cause (prime matter) can have multiple effects (receptivity to multiple forms), so the principle is not universally true.

Checkpoint
What would the one-cause principle force us to say about hyle according to the fourth objection?
A) Hyle can receive all forms.
B) Hyle is simple and indivisible.
C) Hyle can only receive one form.
D) Hyle is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection demonstrates that prime matter's ability to receive multiple forms disproves the one-cause principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a false principle can limit what we think is possible. In your own life, have you silently accepted the principle that you can only be good at one thing, or that a single cause in your past determines your future? What is one such limiting belief you will actively reject this week, and how will you train yourself to be more 'receptive' to multiple forms of growth and grace?

The objection uses hyle's ability to receive multiple forms. Your heart and mind are even more receptive. What one new 'form' of knowledge or worship will you intentionally expose yourself to in the coming days?

Fifth Objection: A Body Can Have Multiple Accidents
The author observes a body can have successive states like motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical objection that a single body can undergo multiple accidents, and why this challenges the one-cause principle.

Core Idea
Observation shows a body can have successive accidents—so why limit it to one?

This lesson presents the fifth objection against the one-cause principle. The objector points to everyday experience: a body can be at rest and then move, or change color, etc. This seems to refute the idea that a body can only have one accident. We will examine the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Observe the examples
Read the passage and note the examples: rest-to-motion, black-to-white, dark-to-light, sour-to-sweet. They show change in a single body.
2
Distinguish two concepts of receptivity
The argument claims the receptivity to motion is a different concept from receptivity to rest. Understand why they must be distinct.
3
Follow the logical division
The two concepts are either both inside the essence, both outside, or one inside and one outside. Each option leads to a problem, concluding that a body would only accept one accident.
This is the absurd consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الخامس : إنا نشاهد أن الجسم الواحد ، قد يكون ساكناً . ثم يزول عنه السكون ، وتحدث فيه الحركة . وكذا القول في تبدل السواد بالبياض ، والظلمة بالنور ، والحموضة بالحلاوة . فنقول : المفهوم من كونه قابلاً للحركة ، غير المفهوم الذي به قابل للسكون . فهذان المفهومان . إما أن يكونا داخلين في الماهية ، أو خارجين عنها ، أو أحدهما داخلاً ، والآخر خارجاً . ونسوق التقسيم إلى آخره. فيلزم أن يقال : الجسم الواحد لا يقبل إلا عرضاً واحداً : وهو محال
The fifth objection: We observe that one body may be at rest. Then rest leaves it and motion occurs in it. Similarly with the change of blackness to whiteness, darkness to light, sourness to sweetness. We say: The concept of it being receptive to motion is other than the concept by which it is receptive to rest. These two concepts: either they are inside the essence, or outside it, or one inside and the other outside. And we carry the division to its end. So it would follow that it should be said: One body does not accept except one accident. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection based on empirical observation. A single body can have successive accidents: it can be at rest and then in motion, or change from black to white, etc. The concept of being receptive to motion is distinct from the concept of being receptive to rest. If these receptivities were part of the essence, the body would be composite; if external, they would be accidental attributes. Applying the one-cause principle would force us to say that a body can only have one accident, which is obviously false because bodies undergo multiple changes.

Key Terms
جسم
سكون
حركة
عرض
قابلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the fifth objection conclude would be the false consequence of the one-cause principle?
A) Body can only have one accident.
B) Body cannot change.
C) Motion is impossible.
D) Body is simple.

Key Takeaway
The one-cause principle leads to the absurd conclusion that a body can only have one accident, which is refuted by everyday observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You observe change constantly—a car moving, a light switching on. Does your automatic assumption about a single cause for each change align with what this lesson shows? What would it take for you to question that assumption deeply?
?

?
When you see a person change from sad to happy, do you assume that sadness and happiness cannot coexist because they require two different 'receptivities'? How does this lesson challenge the way you understand human change—and your own heart?

The Sixth Objection: The Cause's Essence and Infinite Regress
The author attacks the notion of a cause having a single effect by analyzing its essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that a cause can only have one effect leads to the same problem as multiple effects, namely infinite regress.

Core Idea
Even a single-effect cause leads to infinite regress when its essence and necessitation are distinguished.

This lesson presents a powerful objection that turns the opponents' argument against them. The author analyzes the very concept of a cause having a single effect and shows that it suffers from the same difficulty as multiple effects. This forces the opponent to abandon their line of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to understand the flow of the argument.
The objection is presented as a refutation of the claim that a cause necessitates only one effect.
2
Understand the distinction between essence and necessitation
Notice how the author distinguishes between the essence of the cause and its property of necessitating the effect. They are conceptually distinct because we can think of one without the other.
This distinction is key to the argument.
3
Follow the proof that infinite regress arises
The necessitation property is an attribute of the essence, hence possible. Since the essence is the necessitator, this leads to a regress. Trace this logic step by step.
The regress occurs because the attribute itself requires a cause.
4
See how this neutralizes the opponent's argument
The author concludes that the same problem exists for both single and multiple effects, so the opponent cannot use it as a proof against multiple effects.
This is a classic dialectical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض السادس : إن العلة التي توجب معلولاً واحداً فقط . نقول : المفهوم من ذات تلك العلة غير المفهوم من كونها موجبة لذلك المعلول . بدليل : أنه يصح تعقل كل واحد منهما مع الذهول عن الآخر ، فيلزم أن يكون كونها موجبة لذلك المعلول ، مغايراً لذات العلة . ثم إن كونها موجبة لذلك المعلول ، صفة من صفات تلك الذات، ولاحق من لواحقها ، فيكون ممكناً . وموجبة : هو تلك الذات. فيلزم التسلسل . فثبت : أن الإلزام الذي ذكرتموه على تقدير كون العلة الواحدة موجبة لمعلولين ، فهو بعينه قائم إذا لم يوجب إلا معلولاً واحداً . وإذا كان المحذور المذكور قائماً على التقديرين ، امتنع الاستدلال به على إبطال أحد التقديرين .
The sixth objection: Regarding the cause that necessitates only one effect. We say: The concept of the essence of that cause is other than the concept of it being necessitating that effect. Evidence: It is valid to conceive each one of them while being heedless of the other. So it follows that its being necessitating that effect is other than the essence of the cause. Then, its being necessitating that effect is an attribute of that essence and one of its accidents, so it is possible. And the necessitator is that essence. So an infinite regress follows. Thus it is established that the objection you mentioned on the supposition of one cause necessitating two effects is exactly the same when it necessitates only one effect. And since the mentioned difficulty exists on both suppositions, it is impossible to use it to invalidate one of them.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection, which is a powerful refutation. The author turns the argument against the proponents themselves. Even if a cause produces only one effect, we can distinguish between the essence of the cause and its property of necessitating that effect. These are distinct concepts because we can think of the essence without thinking of the effect. Therefore, the property of necessitating is an additional attribute, which itself requires a cause, leading to infinite regress. Thus, the same problem that the opponents use to reject multiple effects also applies to their own position. Therefore, they cannot use that problem as an argument against multiple effects.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
ذات
موجبة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause that produces only one effect avoids the problem of infinite regress that occurs when a cause produces multiple effects.
Correction: The sixth objection shows that even a single-effect cause involves a distinction between essence and necessitation, leading to infinite regress, so the problem is universal.

Checkpoint
What does the sixth objection show about a cause that necessitates only one effect?
A) It avoids infinite regress.
B) It also involves distinct concepts leading to regress.
C) It is simple and uncaused.
D) It is the same as multiple causes.

Key Takeaway
The objection that a cause can only have one effect fails because it leads to the same infinite regress it seeks to avoid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions and their causes, do you ever assume that a single intention is enough to bring about an action without considering additional conditions? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
The argument shows that distinguishing essence from attribute leads to regress. In your own understanding of God, do you separate His essence from His attributes? What is at stake if you cannot reconcile this distinction?
?
If you believe that God's attributes are eternal and not contingent, how do you respond to the charge that even a single divine action leads to regress? Is your response based on a true reading of the source or on a comfortable assumption?

The Seventh Objection: Infinite Regress from Inherence
The author presents the seventh objection: inherence causes regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the inherence of blackness in a substrate cannot be essential to blackness without causing an infinite regress.

Core Idea
“Inherence in a substrate is additional to essence, leading to regress.”

This objection challenges the idea that a single cause can explain the relationship between an accident and its substrate. By examining blackness, the author shows that the concept of inherence is distinct from the essence of blackness and, if taken as an additional attribute, it requires another inherence, setting off an infinite chain. This exposes a flaw in the one-cause principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: distinction, implication, consequence.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the concepts: 'blackness' (سواد), 'inherence' (حلول), 'substrate' (محل), and 'additional' (زائد). Understand how they relate.
Inherence is the attribute of being present in a place.
3
Trace the regress argument
Follow the reasoning: (1) Blackness is distinct from its inherence. (2) Therefore inherence is an additional attribute. (3) That additional attribute must itself inhere, leading to another inherence, and so on ad infinitum.
The conclusion 'يلزم التسلسل' means infinite regress is necessary.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does this regress matter? It shows that the one-cause principle cannot explain simple inherence without falling into an infinite chain of causes.
This connects to broader debates about causality and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض السابع : المفهوم من كون السواد سواداً ، غير المفهوم من كونه حالا في ذلك المحل فوجب أن يكون حلوله في المحل زائداً عليه . وذلك الزائد أيضاً حال فيه . فيلزم التسلسل
The seventh objection: The concept of blackness being blackness is other than the concept of it being present in that substrate. So it must be that its being present in the substrate is additional to it. And that additional (attribute) is also present in it. So an infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection, focusing on the relationship between an accident and its substrate. The concept of blackness as an essence is distinct from the concept of it inhering in a place. The inherence is an additional attribute of blackness. But for that additional attribute to inhere, it would require another inherence, leading to an infinite regress. This shows that the one-cause principle cannot account for the simple fact of inherence without regress.

Key Terms
سواد
حلول
محل
زائد
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the inherence of blackness in a substrate is part of the essence of blackness itself.
Correction: The passage shows that the concept of being black is distinct from the concept of being present in a substrate; therefore inherence is an additional attribute, not part of the essence.

Checkpoint
What does the seventh objection conclude about the inherence of blackness in a substrate?
A) It is essential to blackness.
B) It leads to an infinite regress.
C) It is impossible.
D) It is a simple relation.

Key Takeaway
The seventh objection demonstrates that inherence cannot be an essential attribute without triggering an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you hold any belief about yourself that you consider 'essential' to who you are, but have never examined whether it might actually be an accidental attribute that depends on something else? What would it cost you to investigate that chain of dependency?
?
When you find yourself defending a position, do you ever notice that your reasons themselves require further reasons, leading to an endless regress? Are you willing to stop the regress by accepting a first principle, or do you prefer to keep the chain going to avoid commitment?
?
If your faith rests on a chain of arguments, where does that chain end? Have you honestly confronted the possibility that your reasoning might regress infinitely, or have you assumed a foundation without proof?

Eighth Objection: Blackness vs. Its Origination
The author distinguishes blackness from its temporal occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of blackness is distinct from its temporal origination, and why collapsing them leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
Blackness is not the same as its being created; they are two distinct concepts.

This passage presents the eighth objection against the one-cause principle. The objector argues that the concept of blackness and the concept of its temporal occurrence are not identical. If they were, blackness would itself be a temporal event, which is impossible. This distinction challenges the idea that a single cause can produce a single effect containing multiple inseparable attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the concise logical structure.
2
Identify the two concepts
Pick out 'sawād' (blackness) and 'ḥudūth' (origination). Understand that they are presented as distinct concepts.
3
Understand the conditional argument
The argument says: if the concept of origination were identical to blackness, then blackness would be origination. Trace this conditional reasoning.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Recognize the absurd consequence
Realize that blackness is a color, not an event, so identifying it with origination is impossible. This refutes the one-cause principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثامن : المفهوم من كون السواد سواداً ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونه حادثاً في هذا الوقت فلو كان المفهوم من كونه حادثاً : نفس السواد للزم أن يكون السواد نفس الحدوث .
The eighth objection: The concept of blackness being blackness is different from the concept of it being originated (hādith) at this time. For if the concept of it being originated were the very blackness, then it would follow that blackness is the very origination.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth objection. It distinguishes between the essence of blackness and its temporal origination (being created at a specific time). These are two different concepts. If they were identical, then blackness would be identical to 'origination', which is absurd because blackness is a color, not a temporal event. Thus, the one-cause principle fails because it cannot allow multiple attributes without introducing composition or regress.

Key Terms
سواد
حادث
مفهوم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The attributes of a thing (like its color) are the same as the thing's existence or its temporal origin.
Correction: The objection shows that the concept of blackness is distinct from the concept of its occurrence at a particular time, so they are not identical.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence if the concept of blackness being originated were the same as blackness itself?
A) Blackness would be eternal.
B) Blackness would be identical to origination.
C) Blackness would be a substance.
D) There would be no problem.

Key Takeaway
The concept of an attribute is not identical to the concept of its temporal occurrence; otherwise, attributes would be events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a personal attribute you possess, like patience or anger. Are you aware that the attribute itself is distinct from its occurrence at a given moment? How does conflating the two lead you to treat temporary feelings as if they define you permanently?
?
You often think of your faith (iman) as something fixed, but this lesson teaches that attributes and their temporal arising are different. Is your faith today merely a repetition of past belief, or is it a fresh origination? What would change if you saw each moment of faith as distinct and new?
?
The author argues that blackness cannot be identical to its origination. How does this apply to your own existence? Are you guilty of thinking your actions and their moral weight are the same thing? How can you separate who you are from what you do in a given moment?

The Fifth Objection: Distinguishing Receptivity Concepts
Al-Razi's fifth objection using taksim on receptive concepts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses a taksim (division) about receptive concepts to force an absurd conclusion, refuting the opponent's position.

Core Idea
Different changes prove different receptive concepts; their classification leads to impossibility.

Al-Razi challenges an unnamed opponent by starting from everyday observations: a body at rest can become moving, black can become white. He argues that the very concept of being receptive to motion differs from being receptive to rest. Then he poses a three-way division about these concepts—are they part of the essence or not?—and pushes it to an absurd conclusion. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the empirical premise
Read the first few lines describing changes in a single body: rest to motion, black to white, etc. Notice these are undeniable facts.
2
Identify the conceptual distinction
Al-Razi claims the concept of being receptive to motion is different from that of being receptive to rest. Why? Because you can have one without the other.
Concepts are mental meanings, not physical things.
3
Understand the taksim (division)
He then divides into three cases: both concepts inside the essence, both outside, or one inside and one outside. Think about what each implies.
The full division is not spelled out but is carried to its end.
4
Grasp the forced conclusion
The outcome of the division is that a body can only accept one accident—an absurdity. Thus the opponent's original view must be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الخامس : إنا نشاهد أن الجسم الواحد ، قد يكون ساكناً . ثم يزول عنه السكون ، وتحدث فيه الحركة . وكذا القول في تبدل السواد بالبياض ، والظلمة بالنور ، والحموضة بالحلاوة . فنقول : المفهوم من كونه قابلاً للحركة ، غير المفهوم الذي به قابل للسكون . فهذان المفهومان . إما أن يكونا داخلين في الماهية ، أو خارجين عنها ، أو أحدهما داخلاً ، والآخر خارجاً . ونسوق التقسيم إلى آخره. فيلزم أن يقال : الجسم الواحد لا يقبل إلا عرضاً واحداً : وهو محال
The fifth objection: We observe that a single body may be at rest. Then rest departs from it and motion occurs in it. Similarly, the case in the change of blackness to whiteness, darkness to light, and sourness to sweetness. So we say: The concept of its being receptive to motion is other than the concept by which it is receptive to rest. So these two concepts. Either they are included in the essence, or external to it, or one of them is included and the other external. And we carry the division to its end. So it necessarily follows that one body accepts only one accident: and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection (naqd) against a certain position (likely the claim that simple entities cannot be composed). The objector begins by observing empirical facts: a single body can change from rest to motion, and similarly accidents like color, light, and taste change. From these changes, the objector argues that the concept of being receptive to motion is different from the concept of being receptive to rest. Then he poses a division: these two concepts are either part of the body's essence (quiddity) or external to it, or one is internal and the other external. The complete division (not fully spelled out here) leads to the conclusion that a single body could only accept one accident—which is absurd given our observations. Thus the original position is refuted.

Key Terms
نقض
مفهوم
قابل
ماهية
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objector is proving that bodies are composed of parts, but the argument is actually a reductio against a position that denies composition in certain entities.
Correction: The conclusion 'one body accepts only one accident' is intended to be absurd, not a claim the objector endorses. The argument refutes the opponent by showing their view leads to contradiction.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the fifth objection force upon the opponent?
A) That a body can accept multiple accidents simultaneously.
B) That a single body can only accept one accident.
C) That motion and rest are the same concept.
D) That the body's essence is simple and indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that distinguishing receptive concepts and applying a division forced the opponent into an absurdity, thereby refuting their position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi's argument shows that a body cannot be self-sufficient because it must be receptive to multiple accidents. When you experience changes in your own body—health, mood, strength—do you realize that you are utterly dependent on Allah's constant creation? How can you cultivate a moment-by-moment awareness of His power over your existence today?
?
The division forces an absurdity, revealing that assuming simplicity leads to contradiction. In your own life, do you ever assume you are a simple, unchanging self, forgetting that Allah recreates you in every instant? Will you commit to remembering that each state you experience is a new act of creation, deepening your gratitude and reliance on Him?
?
If a body can change from rest to motion only through a distinct receptivity, then every change you undergo is a sign of your need for a Creator. How will you respond today, when you notice any change in your circumstances, by turning immediately to Allah in acknowledgment of His sole power?

Seventh Objection: The Infinite Regress of Inherence
Al-Razi's seventh objection against distinguishing essence from inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi's seventh objection uses the concept of infinite regress to challenge the distinction between an accident's essence and its inherence in a subject.

Core Idea
If inherence is separate from the accident itself, an infinite chain of accidents follows.

This passage presents a sharp objection from al-Razi. He argues that if we separate the 'being black' from 'being in a subject', then the inherence itself becomes an additional accident, requiring another inherence, and so on. This leads to an absurd infinite regress, undermining the opponent's distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic and literal translation to grasp the concise argument.
Notice the brevity of the objection.
2
Identify the distinction
Al-Razi posits a distinction between the concept of blackness and its inherence. Find where this is stated.
3
Trace the regress
Follow the logical steps: from distinction to additional inherence, then to that additional also inhering, leading to infinite regress.
Understand why this regress is considered absurd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض السابع : المفهوم من كون السواد سواداً ، غير المفهوم من كونه حالا في ذلك المحل فوجب أن يكون حلوله في المحل زائداً عليه . وذلك الزائد أيضاً حال فيه . فيلزم التسلسل
The seventh objection: The concept of blackness being blackness is other than the concept of its being present (hāll) in that subject. So it is necessary that its inherence (ḥulūl) in the subject be additional to it. And that additional thing is also present in it. So an infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This short passage presents the seventh objection. The objector considers blackness (sawād) as an accident. The concept of blackness as blackness is distinct from the concept of its inherence in a subject (the body). Therefore, the inherence itself must be a separate accident added to blackness. But then that added accident also inheres in blackness, requiring another accident, and so on indefinitely. This regress is absurd, so the original distinction between essence and inherence is problematic for the opponent.

Key Terms
مفهوم
سواد
حال
محل
حلول
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this objection is a positive assertion that blackness actually has an infinite regress.
Correction: This is a reductio ad absurdum: the regress follows from the opponent's distinction, not from reality.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does al-Razi derive from distinguishing blackness from its inherence?
A) Blackness becomes permanent.
B) Blackness becomes a substance.
C) An infinite regress of accidents is required.
D) The subject becomes black.

Key Takeaway
Distinguishing an accident's essence from its inherence leads to an infinite regress of accidents, showing the distinction is untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, when followed to its logical conclusion, led to an absurdity you were unwilling to accept? How did you respond—did you abandon the premise or ignore the consequence? This lesson shows that even great scholars like al-Razi used logical consistency to refute flawed distinctions. Where in your own understanding of Islamic creed might you be making a similar error without realizing it? Will you courageously examine your assumptions today?
?
The objection focuses on the distinction between an accident's essence and its inherence. Consider your own beliefs about Allah's attributes: Do you ever treat His attributes as if they are separate 'things' added to His essence? If that led to an impossible regress (like multiple eternals), would you be willing to adjust your understanding based on the evidence? Or would you cling to a comfortable but flawed distinction? What is at stake for your ‘aqīdah?
?
The passage uses a simple example—blackness—to expose a logical problem. Can you identify a contrast between two concepts you previously thought were distinct, only to realize that the distinction leads to an infinite chain? Perhaps in your understanding of free will and predestination, or the relationship between Allah's will and His decree. This objection challenges you to think: Am I making a distinction that leads to an infinite regress? If so, what action will you take to resolve it spiritually and intellectually?

Sixth Objection: The Regress of Necessitation
Al-Razi argues that the necessitation attribute is distinct from the essence, leading to regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that the necessitation attribute of a cause is distinct from its essence, leading to an infinite regress that invalidates a key critique of plural effects.

Core Idea
The cause's necessitation cannot be identical to its essence, or infinite regress follows.

Al-Razi presents the sixth objection against a common causal principle. He turns the tables: even if a cause has only one effect, the concept of its necessitation is separate from its essence. This distinction implies necessitation is an attributive accident, raising the same regress problem the opponent sought to avoid. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice al-Razi begins by stating the sixth objection concerning a cause that necessitates only one effect.
2
Identify the key distinction
Al-Razi argues that the concept of the cause's essence is different from the concept of its being necessitating. Find the sentence where he makes this distinction and the supporting evidence.
The evidence is that each can be conceived without the other.
3
Follow the regress argument
He then argues that 'being necessitating' is an attribute, which is possible and requires another cause, leading to an infinite regress. Trace how this regress follows from the distinction.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: it shows the opponent's assumption leads to an absurd consequence.
4
Understand the conclusion
Finally, Al-Razi concludes that the same problem applies whether the cause has one effect or many. Therefore, the opponent's argument cannot disprove multiple effects. Reflect on how this refutes the opponent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض السادس : إن العلة التي توجب معلولاً واحداً فقط . نقول : المفهوم من ذات تلك العلة غير المفهوم من كونها موجبة لذلك المعلول . بدليل : أنه يصح تعقل كل واحد منهما مع الذهول عن الآخر ، فيلزم أن يكون كونها موجبة لذلك المعلول ، مغايراً لذات العلة . ثم إن كونها موجبة لذلك المعلول ، صفة من صفات تلك الذات، ولاحق من لواحقها ، فيكون ممكناً . وموجبة : هو تلك الذات. فيلزم التسلسل . فثبت : أن الإلزام الذي ذكرتموه على تقدير كون العلة الواحدة موجبة لمعلولين ، فهو بعينه قائم إذا لم يوجب إلا معلولاً واحداً . وإذا كان المحذور المذكور قائماً على التقديرين ، امتنع الاستدلال به على إبطال أحد التقديرين .
The sixth objection: The cause that necessitates only one effect. We say: The concept of the essence of that cause is other than the concept of its being necessitating of that effect. By evidence: it is valid to conceive each one of them while being heedless of the other, so it necessarily follows that its being necessitating of that effect is other than the essence of the cause. Then, its being necessitating of that effect is an attribute of those attributes of that essence, and a consequent of its concomitants, so it is possible. And 'necessitating' is that same essence. So an infinite regress follows. Thus it is established that the implication you mentioned on the supposition that one cause necessitates two effects is exactly the same as when it necessitates only one effect. And since the mentioned absurdity holds for both suppositions, it is impossible to use it as evidence for invalidating one of the two suppositions.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage contains the sixth objection. The opponent had probably argued that a cause cannot have multiple effects because that would require composition in the cause. The objector turns the argument around: even if a cause has only one effect, the concept of the cause's essence is different from the concept of its being necessitating of that effect (since each can be conceived without the other). This distinction implies that the attribute 'being necessitating' is distinct from the essence, and since attributes are possible (contingent) and attached to the essence, they require another cause, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore, the same problem applies whether the cause has one effect or many. Thus the opponent's argument cannot disprove multiple effects.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
موجبة
ذات
صفة
لاحق
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Razi is claiming that infinite regress actually occurs in causal chains.
Correction: Al-Razi is using reductio ad absurdum: he shows that the opponent's own assumptions lead to an absurd infinite regress, thereby refuting the opponent's argument. He does not affirm that such regress exists in reality.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi argue about the cause's necessitation attribute?
A) It is identical to the essence of the cause.
B) It is distinct from the essence and leads to infinite regress.
C) It is impossible for a cause to have any effect.
D) It is a necessary attribute that does not require a cause.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that the opponent's own reasoning about a single-effect cause leads to the same infinite regress, thus neutralizing their objection.

?
Al-Razi uses this reductio to neutralize an opponent's argument. How often do you examine your own theological reasoning for hidden assumptions? Can you identify a belief you hold that, upon applying similar logical scrutiny, might lead to an absurd conclusion you haven't considered?

The Eighth Objection: Infinite Regress from Blackness's Temporality
Al-Razi's eighth objection: distinguishing essence from temporality leads to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi argues that distinguishing blackness's essence from its temporality leads to an infinite regress of accidents.

Core Idea
If blackness and its temporality are distinct, then temporality becomes an additional accident causing regress.

Al-Razi's eighth objection targets the kalam distinction between an accident's essence and its temporal origination. He argues that if they are distinct, then the temporality itself becomes an accident that requires another temporality, leading to an infinite chain. This passage presents a concise logical dilemma that challenges the coherence of temporal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the logical flow. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
The argument is short but dense.
2
Identify the components of the argument
Break the argument into its premise, conditional, and conclusion. The premise states a distinction; the conditional draws an implication; the conclusion identifies a regress.
Use the commentary to help.
3
Understand the regress problem
Reflect on why an infinite regress is considered problematic in kalam. How does it undermine the original distinction?
This connects to broader debates on causality.
4
Connect to the seventh objection
Note that this objection is parallel to the seventh, but with blackness instead of another accident. Compare the structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض الثامن : المفهوم من كون السواد سواداً ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونه حادثاً في هذا الوقت . فلو كان المفهوم من كونه حادثاً : نفس السواد وجب أن يكون حدوثه في ذلك الوقت زائداً عليه . ثم ذلك الزائد أيضاً حادث في ذلك الوقت . فيلزم التسلسل
The eighth objection: The concept of blackness being blackness is different from the concept of its being temporally originated (ḥādith) at this time. For if the concept of its being originated were the blackness itself, then its origination at that time would be additional to it. Then that additional thing is also originated at that time. So an infinite regress follows.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This is the eighth objection, parallel to the seventh. Here the objector considers the temporality (ḥudūth) of blackness. The concept of blackness as blackness is not the same as the concept of its occurring at a specific time. If they were identical, then the origination at that time would be a separate accident added to blackness. But that added accident is also temporal, requiring another accident, and so on. This regress shows that distinguishing the essence from its temporal attribute is problematic.

Key Terms
مفهوم
سواد
حادث
زمان
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument shows that accidents cannot be originated (temporal).
Correction: Rather, it shows that distinguishing the essence of an accident from its temporality leads to a regress; it does not deny that accidents are originated.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence if the concept of blackness were identical to its being originated?
A) Blackness would be eternal.
B) Origination would be a separate accident, causing regress.
C) Blackness would not require a subject.
D) There would be no difference between blackness and whiteness.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's eighth objection uses logical regress to challenge the kalam distinction between an accident's essence and its temporal attribute.

Ninth Objection: Causal Chains and Multiple Concepts
Al-Razi objects that a first cause in a causal chain has multiple causal relations, each with a distinct concept, leading to composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the multiplicity of causal concepts in a chain to argue against the simplicity of a first cause.

Core Idea
Different causal relations imply different concepts, undermining causal simplicity.

Al-Razi presents a ninth objection against the notion of a simple first cause. He considers a causal chain where each cause produces an effect that becomes a cause for the next effect. The first cause stands in multiple relations to different effects, each relation having a distinct concept. By analyzing whether these concepts are internal or external to the essence, al-Razi shows that the opponent's position leads to composition or regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: Al-Razi first describes the causal chain, then asserts distinct concepts for each relation.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the distinct concepts
Al-Razi explicitly mentions three concepts: being a cause of A, being a cause of the cause of B, and being a cause of the cause of the cause of J. Find these in the text and note how they are said to be different (mughtayira).
3
Understand the division
After stating that the concepts are different, al-Razi says 'Either they are conceptual or external, and the division returns to its end.' This refers to a previous argument: if the concepts are in the essence, composition follows; if external, an infinite regress results.
The 'division' refers to a standard kalam argument about attributes being either identical to or distinct from the essence.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this objection challenges the opponent's claim of absolute simplicity. The first cause would have multiple distinct relations, each implying a different concept, which cannot be reduced to one simple essence.
This ties back to earlier objections about multiplicity of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض التاسع : العلة إذا أوجبت معلولاً ، وذلك المعلول أوجب معلولاً آخر وهكذا إلى آخر المراتب . فنقول : العلة الأولى يصدق عليها أنها علة للمعلول الأول ، وأنها علة المعلول الثاني. وهكذا إلى آخر المراتب . ولا شك أن هذه اللواحق الكثيرة ، لاحقة لذات العلة الأولى . فنقول : المفهوم من كونها علة للألف ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونها علة لعلة الباء ، ومن كونها علة لعلة علة الجيم . فهذه المفهومات المتغايرة . إما أن تكون مفهومة أو خارجة ، ويعود التقسيم إلى آخره .
The ninth objection: When a cause necessitates an effect, and that effect necessitates another effect, and so on to the end of the levels. We say: The first cause is truthfully described as being a cause of the first effect, and as being a cause of the second effect, and so on to the end of the levels. There is no doubt that these many consequents are consequent to the essence of the first cause. So we say: The concept of its being a cause of A is other than the concept of its being a cause of the cause of B, and from its being a cause of the cause of the cause of J. So these different concepts. Either they are conceptual or external, and the division returns to its end.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This ninth objection addresses a causal chain. The first cause is related to multiple effects via intermediaries. The objector argues that the concepts of being a cause of each effect (directly or indirectly) are distinct. For example, being the cause of the first effect is a different relation than being the cause of the second effect through an intermediate. These multiple concepts either exist in the mind or in reality, and the same division as before (internal/external to essence) leads to composition or regress. Thus, the opponent's claim of simplicity is undermined.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
مراتب
لاحق
مفهوم
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that causal chains pose no problem for a simple first cause because the cause is the same.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that being a cause of different effects, even through intermediaries, involves distinct relations and concepts, which imply multiplicity in the first cause.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say about the concepts of the first cause's causal relations?
A) They are all identical.
B) They are different and lead to a division about essence.
C) They are all external to the essence.
D) They are impossible.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's ninth objection shows that even in a causal chain, the first cause's multiple relations imply distinct concepts, challenging its simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi argues that every causal relation introduces a distinct concept in the first cause, challenging absolute simplicity. When you think about Allah's relationship with every atom in existence, do you feel the weight of this objection? How do you personally reconcile the multiplicity of His actions with His perfect oneness? Write a specific resolution you will hold in your heart.
?
In your daily life, you often attribute effects to immediate causes—your hard work led to success, your mistake led to failure. Where does the First Cause fit in your practical attribution? Is there a recent event where you relied on a secondary cause as if it were independent? Commit to one concrete change in how you mentally trace all outcomes back to Allah.
?
This objection is not merely theoretical; it touches the core of tawheed. If you cannot logically defend divine simplicity against al-Razi's argument, does that shake your certainty? What step will you take to deepen your understanding—seeking knowledge, reflection, or supplication—so that your belief in Allah's oneness is not just inherited but intellectually certain?

The Ninth Refutation: Conceptual Diversity in a Causal Chain
Razi presents the ninth refutation against the claim that a single cause necessitates simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that a single cause must be simple by showing that diverse concepts of causation do not imply composition in the cause.

Core Idea
Diverse concepts do not equal real composition.

In this passage, Razi presents the ninth refutation against an argument that a cause must be simple because it has one relation to its effect. He considers a chain of causes and effects and argues that the concepts of being the cause of each effect are different. However, this conceptual diversity does not necessitate any real multiplicity in the cause itself. This refutation is a key move in defending the unity of the First Cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text of the ninth refutation slowly, noticing the logical flow from the condition to the conclusion.
Pay attention to the terms 'علة' and 'مفهوم'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that a single cause has only one relation to its effect, implying simplicity. Razi challenges this by considering a chain of effects.
3
Understand Razi's argument
Razi says that the concept of being the cause of the first effect differs from the concept of being the cause of the second effect. These concepts are diverse. He then asks whether these concepts are internal (conceptual) or external (real).
This leads to a division that he does not complete here, but the implication is that they are merely conceptual.
4
Reflect on the implication
If conceptual diversity implied composition, then even simple things would be composite. Since that is absurd, the opponent's argument fails.
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض التاسع : العلة إذا أوجبت معلولاً ، وذلك المعلول أوجب معلولاً آخر وهكذا إلى آخر المراتب . فنقول : العلة الأولى يصدق عليها أنها علة للمعلول الأول ، وأنها علة المعلول الثاني. وهكذا إلى آخر المراتب . ولا شك أن هذه اللواحق الكثيرة ، لاحقة لذات العلة الأولى . فنقول : المفهوم من كونها علة للألف ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونها علة لعلة الباء ، ومن كونها علة لعلة علة الجيم . فهذه المفهومات المتغايرة . إما أن تكون مفهومة أو خارجة ، ويعود التقسيم إلى آخره .
النقض العاشر : المفهوم من قولنا : إنه تعالى موجود مع العقل الأول مغاير للمفهوم من قولنا : إنه موجود مع العقل الثاني ، ومع الفلك الأول والفلك الثاني . وهكذا الكلام في كونه تعالى موجود مع كل واحد من العقول والنفوس والأفلاك والأعراض والصور. ثم تذكر ذلك التقسيم إلى آخره . أو نقول : إنه تعالى قبل كل واحد من هذه العقول والأفلاك ، إما بالعلة أو بالرتبة . ولا شك أن المفهوم من كونه قبل هذا مغاير للمفهوم من كونه قبل ذلك . فيثبت : أن الاعتبار الذي ذكروه لو صح لزم البسائط بأسرها مركبة ، ولزم كون الباري تعالى مركباً ، ولزم نفي التأثير والمؤثر على الاطلاق . ولما كانت هذه الأشياء باطلة ، علمنا : أن ما ذكروه باطل والله أعلم .
The ninth refutation: If a cause necessitates an effect, and that effect necessitates another effect, and so on to the last stages, we say: the first cause is truly described as being the cause of the first effect, and as being the cause of the second effect, and so on to the last stages. There is no doubt that these many consequent attributes are attached to the essence of the first cause. We say: the concept of its being the cause of A is different from the concept of its being the cause of the cause of B, and from its being the cause of the cause of the cause of C. So these are diverse concepts. They are either conceptual or external, and the division returns to its end.
The tenth refutation: The concept of our saying 'He, exalted, exists with the first intellect' is different from the concept of our saying 'He exists with the second intellect', and with the first sphere and the second sphere. And similarly the discourse concerning His being existent with each of the intellects, souls, spheres, accidents, and forms. Then that division is mentioned to its end. Or we say: He, exalted, is before each of these intellects and spheres, either by causation or by rank. There is no doubt that the concept of His being before this is different from the concept of His being before that. So it is established that the consideration they mentioned, if sound, would necessitate that all simple things are composite, and that the Creator, exalted, is composite, and would necessitate the absolute negation of effect and efficient cause. And since these things are false, we know that what they mentioned is false, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage contains two refutations (naqd) against an opponent's argument that a single cause must be simple because it has a single relation to its effect. Razi argues that when a cause produces a chain of effects, the concepts of its being the cause of each effect are different. For example, 'being the cause of A' is a different concept from 'being the cause of the cause of B'. These diverse concepts do not imply composition in the cause, because they are mere conceptual distinctions, not real divisions. Similarly, in the tenth refutation, he applies the same reasoning to God's existence with multiple created things. The concept of God being with the first intellect is different from being with the second intellect. If such conceptual diversity implied composition, then even simple beings would be composite, and God would be composite, which is absurd. Therefore, the opponent's argument is invalid.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
مفهوم
النقض
بسائط
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That having multiple descriptions or concepts about something means that thing is composite.
Correction: Razi shows that concepts can be distinct without the subject being composite. For example, 'being the cause of A' and 'being the cause of B' are different concepts but refer to the same cause.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue about the concepts of being a cause in a causal chain?
A) They are identical
B) They are diverse but do not imply composition in the cause
C) They necessitate composition
D) They are external realities

Key Takeaway
The ninth refutation establishes that conceptual diversity does not entail real composition, preserving the simplicity of the First Cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah’s names like Ar-Rahman and Ar-Raheem, do you ever feel as if these are separate 'parts' of Him? How does Razi’s distinction between conceptual and real multiplicity challenge that feeling? What would it mean for your heart to truly believe that His essence is one and undivided despite these many descriptions? Will you pause now and say, 'Allah is one, and His names are not separate additions'?
?
Think of a time when you judged someone as 'complicated' because they had many roles or traits. How does this lesson’s insight—that many concepts can attach to a single simple essence—change the way you view the unity of Allah’s attributes? Are you willing to let go of any mental image of God as composite? Commit to repeating 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh' with the awareness that He is absolutely simple.
?
You believe that Allah creates, sustains, judges, and forgives. Do you ever unconsciously imagine these as different 'actions' that divide His attention? How does the argument here (that many descriptions do not imply composition) correct that assumption? What is one practical change you can make today in how you think about Allah’s oneness in your daily life?

The Tenth Refutation: God's Relations to Multiple Beings
Razi presents the tenth refutation against the claim that a single cause necessitates simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's tenth refutation, which applies the same principle of conceptual diversity to God's existence with multiple created beings, showing that it does not imply composition in God.

Core Idea
God's being-with each creation is conceptually distinct but not composition.

In this second refutation, Razi extends the argument from causation to the broader context of God's relationship with all created things. The concept of God's existence together with the first intellect is different from the concept of His existence with the second intellect, and so on. These conceptual distinctions do not imply any real multiplicity in God. Razi also considers the notion of priority (qabl) and shows that the same reasoning applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text of the tenth refutation, noting the examples of intellects and spheres.
Notice the repetition of the structure from the ninth refutation.
2
Identify the concepts
Razi lists concepts like 'God exists with the first intellect' vs. 'God exists with the second intellect'. These concepts are different.
3
Understand the alternative argument
Razi also considers the concept of priority: 'God is before this intellect' vs. 'God is before that sphere'. These also differ.
This covers both existential coexistence and temporal/causal priority.
4
Apply the same logic
If such conceptual diversity implied composition, then all simple things would be composite, and even God would be composite. Since that is false, the opponent's argument fails.
This is the same reasoning as the ninth refutation.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the opponent's consideration, if sound, would lead to absurdities, so it must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقض التاسع : العلة إذا أوجبت معلولاً ، وذلك المعلول أوجب معلولاً آخر وهكذا إلى آخر المراتب . فنقول : العلة الأولى يصدق عليها أنها علة للمعلول الأول ، وأنها علة المعلول الثاني. وهكذا إلى آخر المراتب . ولا شك أن هذه اللواحق الكثيرة ، لاحقة لذات العلة الأولى . فنقول : المفهوم من كونها علة للألف ، مغاير للمفهوم من كونها علة لعلة الباء ، ومن كونها علة لعلة علة الجيم . فهذه المفهومات المتغايرة . إما أن تكون مفهومة أو خارجة ، ويعود التقسيم إلى آخره .
النقض العاشر : المفهوم من قولنا : إنه تعالى موجود مع العقل الأول مغاير للمفهوم من قولنا : إنه موجود مع العقل الثاني ، ومع الفلك الأول والفلك الثاني . وهكذا الكلام في كونه تعالى موجود مع كل واحد من العقول والنفوس والأفلاك والأعراض والصور. ثم تذكر ذلك التقسيم إلى آخره . أو نقول : إنه تعالى قبل كل واحد من هذه العقول والأفلاك ، إما بالعلة أو بالرتبة . ولا شك أن المفهوم من كونه قبل هذا مغاير للمفهوم من كونه قبل ذلك . فيثبت : أن الاعتبار الذي ذكروه لو صح لزم البسائط بأسرها مركبة ، ولزم كون الباري تعالى مركباً ، ولزم نفي التأثير والمؤثر على الاطلاق . ولما كانت هذه الأشياء باطلة ، علمنا : أن ما ذكروه باطل والله أعلم .
The ninth refutation: If a cause necessitates an effect, and that effect necessitates another effect, and so on to the last stages, we say: the first cause is truly described as being the cause of the first effect, and as being the cause of the second effect, and so on to the last stages. There is no doubt that these many consequent attributes are attached to the essence of the first cause. We say: the concept of its being the cause of A is different from the concept of its being the cause of the cause of B, and from its being the cause of the cause of the cause of C. So these are diverse concepts. They are either conceptual or external, and the division returns to its end.
The tenth refutation: The concept of our saying 'He, exalted, exists with the first intellect' is different from the concept of our saying 'He exists with the second intellect', and with the first sphere and the second sphere. And similarly the discourse concerning His being existent with each of the intellects, souls, spheres, accidents, and forms. Then that division is mentioned to its end. Or we say: He, exalted, is before each of these intellects and spheres, either by causation or by rank. There is no doubt that the concept of His being before this is different from the concept of His being before that. So it is established that the consideration they mentioned, if sound, would necessitate that all simple things are composite, and that the Creator, exalted, is composite, and would necessitate the absolute negation of effect and efficient cause. And since these things are false, we know that what they mentioned is false, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage contains two refutations (naqd) against an opponent's argument that a single cause must be simple because it has a single relation to its effect. Razi argues that when a cause produces a chain of effects, the concepts of its being the cause of each effect are different. For example, 'being the cause of A' is a different concept from 'being the cause of the cause of B'. These diverse concepts do not imply composition in the cause, because they are mere conceptual distinctions, not real divisions. Similarly, in the tenth refutation, he applies the same reasoning to God's existence with multiple created things. The concept of God being with the first intellect is different from being with the second intellect. If such conceptual diversity implied composition, then even simple beings would be composite, and God would be composite, which is absurd. Therefore, the opponent's argument is invalid.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
مفهوم
النقض
بسائط
مركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's multiple relations to creatures imply composition in God.
Correction: Razi shows that the concepts of these relations are distinct but do not entail any real multiplicity in God's essence.

Checkpoint
What does Razi argue about the concept of God's existence with multiple intellects and spheres?
A) They are all the same concept
B) They differ but do not imply composition in God
C) They prove God is composite
D) They negate God's existence

Key Takeaway
The tenth refutation reinforces that conceptual diversity in God's relations does not entail composition, upholding tawhid.

When you imagine God's relationship with you, do you ever unconsciously think that a 'part' of God is focused on you? How does this lesson's truth—that conceptual distinctions do not imply composition—challenge that image, and what does it demand of your understanding of God's closeness?

This lesson reveals that false assumptions about God lead to absurd conclusions. Is there any assumption about God's attributes or actions you hold that, if examined, would collapse like the opponent's argument? What would it cost you to surrender that assumption and embrace God's simplicity?

The Logical Distinction Between 'Not A' and 'Not-A' in Causality
Razi refutes an argument against a single cause producing multiple effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'did not produce A' and 'produced what is not A' are logically distinct, and how this distinction resolves an objection against a single cause producing multiple effects.

Core Idea
Denying production of A is not the same as affirming production of something else.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi responds to an argument that a single cause cannot produce distinct effects without contradiction. He makes a crucial logical distinction that may seem subtle at first but has profound implications for causal reasoning. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for Razi's dense style. Then read the literal translation. Note the key phrases: 'صدر عنه ما ليس بالألف' and 'لم يصدر عنه الألف'.
The passage uses 'A' as a placeholder for any specific effect.
2
Identify the core distinction
Razi claims there is a logical difference between saying 'the cause did not produce A' and 'the cause produced what is not A'. The first negates production of A; the second affirms production of something else (not-A). Understand how this is not a trivial word game.
Think of an example: a teacher who did not give an A grade is not the same as a teacher who gave a B grade.
3
Examine the linguistic analogy
Razi gives parallels: 'by possibility not' vs 'not by possibility', and 'by necessity not' vs 'not by necessity'. These show that the placement of negation changes meaning. Apply the same logic to the causal case.
This illustrates a general principle: negation scope matters.
4
Follow the proof by contradiction
Razi argues that if the two statements were equivalent, absurd consequences would follow: a body could not have two different attributes, and an essence could not exist at two different times. Understand why these consequences are impossible.
The proof shows that the opponent's position leads to self-contradiction, so their objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : العلة الواحدة إذا صدر عنها الألف ، وصدر عنها الباء - ولا شك أن الباء غير الألف - فههنا يصدق أن يقال : إنه صدر عنه الألف ، وصدر عنه ما ليس بالألف . ولا يلزم أن يقال : صدر عنه الألف ، ولم يصدر عنه الألف . ألا ترى أنهم قالوا : فرق بين قولنا بالإمكان ليس ، وبين قولنا : ليس بالإمكان . وفرق بين قولنا : بالضرورة ليس ، وبين قولنا : ليس بالضرورة. فكذا ههنا : فرق بين قولنا : لم يصدر عنه الألف ، وبين قولنا : صدر عنه ما ليس بالألف . والدليل القاطع على هذا الفرق : أنه لولاه لوجب أن لا يقبل الجسم الواحد ، إلا صفة واحدة . لأنه لو قبل صفتين مختلفتين، لزم أن يصدق عليه : أنه قبل تلك الصفة ، وأنه ما قبلها ، وذلك محال . وأيضاً : لو صح ما قالوه ، لزم أن لا تحصل الذات الواحدة إلا وقتاً واحداً، إلا أنه لو حصل في هذا الوقت ، وحصل أيضاً في الوقت الثاني ، لوجب أن يصدق عليه : أنه حصل في هذا الوقت ، وأنه لم يحصل في هذا الوقت. ولما كان ذلك باطلا ، فكذا ما قالوه .
And the answer to the second argument: we say: when a single cause produces A and produces B – and there is no doubt that B is other than A – then here it is true to say: it produced A, and it produced what is not A. And it does not necessitate that one say: it produced A and it did not produce A. Do you not see that they said: there is a difference between our saying 'by possibility not' and our saying 'not by possibility'? And a difference between our saying 'by necessity not' and our saying 'not by necessity'? So likewise here: there is a difference between our saying 'it did not produce A' and our saying 'it produced what is not A'. The conclusive proof of this difference is: that if it were not for it, a single body would be incapable of receiving more than one attribute. For if it received two different attributes, it would necessitate that it be true of it: that it received that attribute, and that it did not receive it, and that is impossible. Also: if what they said were sound, it would necessitate that a single essence occur only at a single time, since if it occurred at this time and also occurred at the second time, it would necessitate that it be true of it: that it occurred at this time and that it did not occur at this time. And since that is false, so too is what they said.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is Razi's answer to a second argument (likely from an opponent claiming that a single cause cannot produce distinct effects without contradiction). Razi makes a crucial logical distinction: saying a cause produced 'not-A' (i.e., something other than A) is not the same as saying it did not produce A. The former affirms production of a different thing; the latter negates production of A. He illustrates with linguistic examples: 'by possibility not' differs from 'not by possibility'; similarly, 'did not produce A' differs from 'produced what is not A'. He provides a proof: if the two were equivalent, a single body could not have two different attributes, because having attribute X would entail not having X (contradiction). Also, a single essence could not exist at two times, because existing at time t1 would entail not existing at t1. Since these consequences are false, the distinction stands, and the opponent's argument fails.

Key Terms
العلة الواحدة (single cause)
صدر عنه (produced from it)
لم يصدر (did not produce)
ما ليس بالألف (what is not A)
الإمكان (possibility)
الضرورة (necessity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'did not produce A' and 'produced what is not A' mean the same thing, leading to the view that a single cause cannot produce A and B without contradiction.
Correction: Razi shows they are logically distinct: the former negates production of A, the latter affirms production of something else. They are not equivalent, so no contradiction arises.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what logical error does the opponent's argument commit?
A) They assume that producing not-A is the same as not producing A.
B) They deny that a single cause can be the source of multiple effects.
C) They confuse possibility with necessity.
D) They ignore the difference between 'by possibility not' and 'not by possibility'.

Key Takeaway
Razi's distinction between negating production of A and affirming production of not-A is a subtle but powerful logical tool that defends the coherence of a single cause producing multiple distinct effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In your daily thoughts, when do you slip into equating 'not X' with 'X not'? For example, do you sometimes think 'I did not succeed' means the same as 'I succeeded in not succeeding'? How can you train yourself to note the difference and avoid self-deception?
?
Razi’s argument shows that imprecision leads to absurdities. Is there an area of your faith where you have been imprecise in belief or practice? Commit to examining one such area today and clarify it using the logical tool from this lesson.

Different Effects Do Not Necessarily Imply Different Causes
Razi argues that difference in effects does not prove difference in causes if there is no observed failure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's criterion for when difference in effects indicates difference in causes and why mere difference is insufficient.

Core Idea
Difference in effects alone does not prove different causes; failure of effects is key.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi addresses a fundamental question in causality: if two agents produce different effects, does that necessarily mean they are different in essence? Razi argues that we cannot conclude that from mere difference; we need to observe a failure—one cause not producing the other's effect. This distinction is crucial for understanding how we infer the essences of causes from their effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand Razi's argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if this failure is not present...'
2
Identify the key condition
Razi states that we infer different essences when there is a failure of effect (e.g., fiery nature does not cool). Identify the condition he sets for inference.
The condition is the failure of one agent to produce the effect of the other.
3
Understand the negative case
Razi says that without such failure, mere difference in effects does not prove diversity of causes. Consider why this is important.
This refutes a common assumption that different effects always imply different causes.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Razi ends with a rhetorical question: 'And is the dispute anything other than about this?' Reflect on the significance.
He indicates that the core issue in the debate is precisely this condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الثالثة : إنه لما حصلت الطبيعة النارية ، ولم يوجد التبريد، وحصلت الطبيعة المائية ، ولم يوجد التسخين ، استدللنا بتخلف هذا الأثر عن ذلك المؤثر، على اختلاف ماهية المؤثرين . فأما لو لم يوجد هذا التخلف ، فلا نسلم أن مجرد اختلاف الأثرين يدل على تغاير المؤثرين . وهل النزاع إلا فيه ؟
And the answer to the third argument: When the fiery nature occurred and cooling was not present, and the watery nature occurred and heating was not present, we inferred by the failure of this effect from that cause the difference in the essences of the causes. But if this failure is not present, we do not concede that mere difference in the two effects indicates the diversity of the causes. And is the dispute anything other than about this?
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is Razi's answer to a third argument that likely claimed that different effects must come from different causes. Razi explains that from observation, when a fiery nature exists and cooling does not occur, and a watery nature exists and heating does not occur, we infer that the two natures have different essences because each fails to produce the other's effect. However, if there is no such failure (i.e., each produces its characteristic effect without failure), then mere difference in effects is not sufficient to prove that the causes are different in essence. He asks rhetorically: isn't the dispute precisely about this point? So he concedes that when effects are different and there is a failure of one cause to produce the other's effect, we can infer different essences, but when there is no failure, we cannot.

Key Terms
الطبيعة النارية (fiery nature)
الطبيعة المائية (watery nature)
تخلف الأثر (failure of effect)
ماهية المؤثر (essence of the agent)
تغاير المؤثرين (diversity of agents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Different effects always indicate different causes.
Correction: Razi explains that this inference requires observing a failure of effect; without failure, mere difference in effects is insufficient to prove diversity of causes.

Checkpoint
Under what condition does Razi accept that different effects indicate different causes?
A) Always, as long as the effects are different.
B) Never, because all causes are alike.
C) Only when one cause fails to produce the effect of the other.
D) Only when the causes are from different species.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that difference in effects alone does not prove diversity of causes; the critical evidence is the failure of one cause to produce the other's characteristic effect.
