Why Cause and Effect Cannot Share the Same Essence
Razi argues from rational principle to conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that a cause must be essentially distinct from its effect, based on the principle that things identical in essence must be equal in all necessary properties.

Core Idea
If cause and effect were identical in essence, neither would have priority or causality.

Razi presents a concise rational argument to refute the idea that a cause and its effect could be identical in essence. He invokes a fundamental principle: things that are completely similar in essence must be alike in all their necessary properties. From this principle, he derives a powerful conclusion about the necessary distinction between cause and effect. Let's read his words carefully and unpack the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the argument.
Notice the structure: principle, conditional, conclusion.
2
Identify the rational principle
The argument starts with a principle: things similar in the entirety of essence must be equal in all necessary properties. Pinpoint this in the text.
This is key to the entire deduction.
3
Apply the principle to cause and effect
Now follow the implied conditional: if cause and effect were identical in essence, they would be equal in priority and causality. Why does that lead to impossibility?
Consider what it means for something to be a cause.
4
Reach the conclusion
Razi concludes that cause and effect must differ in essence. Confirm that you see why this follows from the principle and the conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب من الحجة الرابعة : إن العلة يمتنع كونها متساوية للمعلول في الماهية ، لأنه ثبت في العقل : أن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، يجب استواؤها في جميع اللوازم ، فلو كانت العلة مماثلة للمعلول في تمام الماهية ، لم يكن أحدهما أولى بالتقدم من الآخر، ولا بالعلية من الآخر. فتعين أن العلة مغايرة للمعلول في الماهية.
And the answer from the fourth argument: It is impossible for the cause to be equal to the effect in essence, because it is established in reason that things that are similar in the entirety of essence must be equal in all necessary properties. So if the cause were similar to the effect in the entirety of essence, neither of them would be more deserving of priority over the other, nor of causality over the other. So it is determined that the cause is different from the effect in essence.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is Razi's answer to a fourth argument, presented as a proof from reason. He argues that a cause cannot share the same essence as its effect. The rational principle is: things that are completely identical in essence must be equal in all necessary properties (lawāzim). If a cause and effect were identical in essence, then neither would have priority over the other in terms of temporal priority or causal relationship. Since causality implies that the cause precedes the effect and is the source, they must differ in essence. Therefore, the cause and effect are essentially distinct.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)
ماهية (essence)
متماثلات (similar things)
تمام الماهية (entirety of essence)
لوازم (necessary properties)
علية (causality)
تقدم (priority)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cause and effect can be identical in essence as long as they differ in some accidental properties.
Correction: Razi shows that if they were identical in essence, they would be equal in all necessary properties, including priority and causality, which are essential to the definition of cause. Accidental differences do not affect necessary properties.

Checkpoint
Why, according to Razi, cannot a cause be identical in essence to its effect?
A) Because cause and effect always differ in time.
B) Because if they were identical, they would be equal in all necessary properties, so neither could be prior or causal.
C) Because effects are always material and causes are spiritual.
D) Because similarity in essence is impossible between two things.

Key Takeaway
Cause and effect must be essentially distinct because identity in essence would entail equality in all aspects, contradicting the causal relationship.

How will you repent from that mindset and affirm His utter otherness?

Refuting the Objection: Producing Two Different Effects
Answering the objection that a single cause cannot produce two different effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that a single cause cannot produce two different effects by distinguishing between 'did not produce A' and 'produced what is not A'.

Core Idea
A cause can produce two different effects without contradiction by careful logical distinction.

The opponent argues that if a single cause produces two different effects, then it both produced and did not produce the same effect, which is absurd. The author responds by showing that this reasoning confuses two different statements. He illustrates with simple examples and proves that the consequence would lead to impossible conclusions. This passage is a classic example of logical precision in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses the letters alif and ba as examples.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author distinguishes between 'it did not produce A' and 'it produced what is not A'. Find where he makes this distinction and understand why it is crucial.
These two statements are not logically equivalent.
3
Examine the analogies from logic
The author gives analogies from modal logic: 'by possibility it is not' vs 'it is not by possibility', and similarly for necessity. Reflect on how these illustrate the distinction.
The placement of the negation matters.
4
Understand the proof by reduction
The author argues that if the objection were valid, a body could only have one attribute and an essence could only exist at one time. See why these consequences are absurd.
This proves the distinction is correct.
5
Summarize the refutation
Write a short summary of why the objection fails, using the distinction you learned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : العلة الواحدة إذا صدر عنها الألف ، وصدر عنها الباء - ولا شك أن الباء غير الألف - فههنا يصدق أن يقال : إنه صدر عنه الألف ، وصدر عنه ما ليس بالألف . ولا يلزم أن يقال : صدر عنه الألف ، ولم يصدر عنه الألف . لأنه لا فرق بين أن يقال : صدر عنه ما ليس بألف ، وبين أن يقال : لم يصدر عنه الألف . ألا ترى أنهم قالوا : فرق بين قولنا بالإمكان ليس ، وبين قولنا : ليس بالإمكان . وفرق بين قولنا : بالضرورة ليس ، وبين قولنا : ليس بالضرورة. فكذا ههنا : فرق بين قولنا : لم يصدر عنه الألف ، وبين قولنا : صدر عنه ما ليس بالألف . والدليل القاطع على هذا الفرق : أنه لولاه لوجب أن لا يقبل الجسم الواحد ، إلا صفة واحدة . لأنه لو قبل صفتين مختلفتين، لزم أن يصدق عليه : أنه قبل تلك الصفة ، وأنه ما قبلها ، وذلك محال . وأيضاً : لو صح ما قالوه ، لزم أن لا تحصل الذات الواحدة إلا وقتاً واحداً، إلا أنه لو حصل في هذا الوقت ، وحصل أيضاً في الوقت الثاني ، لوجب أن يصدق عليه : أنه حصل في هذا الوقت ، وأنه لم يحصل في هذا الوقت. ولما كان ذلك باطلا ، فكذا ما قالوه
And the answer to the second argument: that we say: when a single cause produces 'alif' and produces 'ba' – and there is no doubt that 'ba' is other than 'alif' – then here it is true to say that it produced 'alif' and produced what is not 'alif'. And it does not follow that it is said: it produced 'alif' and it did not produce 'alif'. Because there is no difference between saying 'it produced what is not alif' and saying 'it did not produce alif'. Do you not see that they said: there is a difference between our saying 'by possibility it is not' and our saying 'it is not by possibility'? And there is a difference between our saying 'by necessity it is not' and our saying 'it is not by necessity'. So likewise here: there is a difference between our saying 'it did not produce alif' and our saying 'it produced what is not alif'. The decisive proof for this difference: that if it were not for it, then it would be necessary that a single body does not accept except one attribute. Because if it accepted two different attributes, then it would be true of it that it accepted that attribute and that it did not accept it, and that is impossible. And also: if what they said were sound, then it would be necessary that a single essence does not occur except in one time, except that if it occurred in this time, and also occurred in the second time, then it would be necessary to say of it: it occurred in this time, and it did not occur in this time. And since that is false, so is what they said.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection (hujja) that a single cause cannot produce two different effects because that would imply the cause both produced and did not produce the same thing. The author responds by distinguishing between 'it did not produce A' and 'it produced what is not A'. He illustrates with letters: a cause produces alif and ba. Since ba is not alif, it is true that it produced alif and produced what is not alif. It does not follow that it produced alif and did not produce alif, because 'produced what is not alif' is not equivalent to 'did not produce alif'. He supports this with examples from logic: the difference between 'by possibility it is not' and 'it is not by possibility' (modal distinctions). Then he gives a proof: if the objection were valid, then a body could only accept one attribute, because if it accepted two different attributes, it would be said to have accepted that attribute and not accepted it, which is impossible. Also, an essence could only occur at one time. Since these consequences are false, the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
علة
معلول
ماهية
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a cause produces two different effects, then it both produced and did not produce the same effect, which is a contradiction.
Correction: The author shows that 'it produced what is not A' is logically different from 'it did not produce A', so no contradiction arises.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that a single cause cannot produce two different effects?
A) By denying that a single cause can produce two effects.
B) By distinguishing between 'it did not produce A' and 'it produced what is not A'.
C) By arguing that all effects are identical in essence.
D) By relying on the principle of excluded middle.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it conflates two logically distinct statements; a single cause can produce different effects without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When Does Difference of Effects Prove Difference of Causes?
Answering the argument that difference of effects alone proves difference of causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand under what condition a difference in effects implies a difference in causes, and why mere difference is insufficient.

Core Idea
Failure of expected effect, not mere difference, proves causal distinction.

In the third argument, the opponent claimed that different effects must come from different causes. Sanusi responds by distinguishing between cases where an effect fails to occur and cases where it does occur. The key is that only the failure of an expected effect provides evidence for distinct essences. Let's examine his example and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the example
Find the example using fiery nature (fire) and watery nature (water). What effects are expected? What fails?
Fire expected to cool? No, fire heats; but the point is about cooling failure from fire.
3
Locate the condition
Identify the key condition under which the author accepts the inference: 'when the fiery nature occurred and cooling did not occur'.
4
Contrast with the opponent's claim
Compare with the opponent's assumption that mere difference of effects suffices. Why does the author reject that?
The passage says: 'if this failure had not occurred, then we do not concede that mere difference of effects indicates diversity.'
5
Reflect on the takeaway
Summarize in your own words when difference of effects does and does not indicate difference of causes.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الثالثة : إنه لما حصلت الطبيعة النارية ، ولم يوجد التبريد، وحصلت الطبيعة المائية ، ولم يوجد التسخين ، استدللنا بتخلف هذا الأثر عن ذلك المؤثر، على اختلاف ماهية المؤثرين . فأما لو لم يوجد هذا التخلف ، فلا نسلم أن مجرد اختلاف الأثرين يدل على تغاير المؤثرين . وهل النزاع إلا فيه ؟
And the answer to the third argument: that when the fiery nature occurred and cooling did not occur, and the watery nature occurred and heating did not occur, we inferred, by the failure of this effect from that cause, the difference in the essence of the causes. But if this failure had not occurred, then we do not concede that mere difference of effects indicates the diversity of the causes. And is the dispute except about that?
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the third argument. The author gives an example: when the nature of fire is present but cooling does not occur, and the nature of water is present but heating does not occur, we infer from the failure of the expected effect that the essences of the causes are different. But if there were no such failure, then mere difference of effects does not prove difference of causes. In other words, the argument only works if an effect fails to appear; otherwise, diverse effects can come from diverse causes.

Misconception: People often think that any difference between effects automatically means the causes are different.
Correction: The author explains that only when an expected effect fails to occur does the difference indicate a difference in the essences of the causes. Mere difference without failure is insufficient.

Checkpoint

B) Only when the expected effect fails to occur.
C) Only when the effects are opposites.
D) Only when the causes are observable.

Key Takeaway
The inference of causal difference requires a failure of the expected effect, not merely the presence of different effects.

Cause and Effect Are Distinct in Essence
Refuting the claim that cause and effect must be similar in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why cause and effect cannot be identical in essence according to rational proof.

Core Idea
Cause and effect must be distinct in essence, or causality collapses.

In this passage, the author refutes the claim that cause and effect must be similar in essence. He provides a rational argument: if they were identical, they would have all the same properties, and neither would have priority over the other. Thus, they must be different. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Identify the rational premise
The author states a principle: things that are similar in complete essence must be equal in all concomitants. Understand what this means.
A 'concomitant' is a necessary property or attribute.
3
Apply the premise to causality
Consider the hypothetical: if cause and effect were identical in essence, then they would have the same concomitants. Then neither would be more deserving of being the cause. Since that is absurd, they cannot be identical.
This shows the problem: cause and effect must be asymmetric.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب من الحجة الرابعة : إن العلة يمتنع كونها متساوية للمعلول في الماهية ، لأنه ثبت في العقل : أن المتماثلات في تمام الماهية ، يجب استواؤها في جميع اللوازم ، فلو كانت العلة مماثلة للمعلول في تمام الماهية ، لم يكن أحدهما بالعلية ، والآخر بالمعلولية أولى من العكس. فيثبت : أن قول من يقول : العلة مماثلة للمعلول : كلام باطل
And the answer to the fourth argument: that it is impossible for the cause to be equal to the effect in essence, because it is established in the intellect that things that are similar in complete essence must be equal in all concomitants. So if the cause were similar to the effect in complete essence, then one of them would not be more deserving of being the cause and the other the effect than the reverse. Thus it is established that the statement of whoever says that the cause is similar to the effect is false.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth argument, which likely claimed that cause and effect must be similar in essence. The author refutes this: if cause and effect were identical in essence, they would have all the same concomitants, so neither would be more deserving of being the cause rather than the effect. Since that is absurd, cause and effect must be distinct in essence.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that for a cause to produce an effect, they must be similar in essence (e.g., like produces like).
Correction: This passage shows that if cause and effect were identical in essence, they would have all the same properties, and causality would be impossible because neither would have priority as cause. Therefore, cause and effect must be distinct in essence.

Checkpoint

C) Because effects are less perfect than causes.
D) Because we can never know essences.

Key Takeaway
Cause and effect differ in essence; otherwise causality is impossible.

Refutation of Cause-Effect Similarity and Answer to Fifth Argument
Refutes cause-effect similarity and analyzes an argument about preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that cause is similar to effect and analyzes the fifth argument’s ambiguity about preference.

Core Idea
Cause and effect are not similar; ambiguity in 'preference' leads only to uncertainty.

This passage addresses two related points: first, it refutes the erroneous idea that a cause must resemble its effect, establishing that causality implies priority, not similarity. Second, it clarifies a logical argument that could be misinterpreted, showing that ambiguous statements yield only suspension of judgment, not certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation to grasp the two distinct sections.
2
Identify the refutation of similarity
Notice how the author asserts that the causal relationship makes one term prior, making similarity false.
3
Analyze the answer to the fifth argument
Examine the distinction between objective preference in reality and subjective preference in thought.
4
Reflect on the distinction's importance
Consider why this distinction matters for logical reasoning and avoiding false conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعلية ، والآخر بالمعلولية أولى من العكس. فيثبت : أن قول من يقول : العلة مماثلة للمعلول : (١) كلام باطل • والجواب عن الحجة الخامسة : إنا بينا في علم المنطق : « إن قول القائل : إنه ليس عدد أولى من عدد : إذا أريد به عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر ، فهذا ممنوع ، وإن أريد به عدم الأولوية في الذهن والخاطر ، فهذا لا يفيد إلا التوقف وعدم الجزم ، [ والله أعلم (۲) ] .
With causality, and the other by effectuality is more appropriate than the opposite. So it is established that the statement of one who says: The cause is similar to the effect: (1) is false speech. And the answer to the fifth argument: We have explained in the science of logic: 'Indeed, the statement of one who says: It is not that a number is preferable to a number: if by it is meant the absence of preference in actuality, then this is forbidden; and if by it is meant the absence of preference in the mind and thought, then this yields only suspension and lack of certainty, [and God knows best (2)].'
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage contains two related points. First, it refutes the claim that the cause is similar to the effect by stating that the relationship of causality and effectuality is such that one term (the cause) is prior to the other (the effect), making the opposite (similarity) untenable. The author asserts that the statement 'the cause is similar to the effect' is false. Second, it provides an answer to a fifth argument. The author refers to what was previously explained in logic: the ambiguous statement 'it is not that a number is preferable to a number' is analyzed by considering what is meant. If it means there is no objective preference in reality, that claim is rejected (mumtani'). If it means no preference in the mind or thought, then it only results in suspension of judgment and lack of certainty. The passage ends with 'and God knows best'.

Correction: The passage refutes the specific claim that the cause is comparable or identical to its effect in nature, not that they have any common properties.

Proof that the First Emanation is a Single Incorporeal Substance
Proof that the first emanation is a single, non-corporeal substance by elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the first emanation from God must be a single, non-corporeal substance.

Core Idea
The first emanation cannot be a body or an accident; it is a simple, incorporeal substance.

This passage presents a foundational argument in Islamic philosophy about the first created being. Starting from the principle 'from the One only one proceeds', the author systematically eliminates possibilities to show that the first emanation must be a single substance that is neither a body nor a part of a body, leaving only an incorporeal substance like an intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the logical sequence: the author uses the principle of 'from the one only one' and then classifies possible entities.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion is implied but not stated.
2
Understand the initial principle
The argument begins by affirming that God is absolutely one, and from the one only one can proceed. This sets the condition that the first emanation must be a single entity.
This principle is key in Neoplatonic and Islamic philosophical cosmology.
3
Follow the elimination of accident
The author argues the first emanation cannot be an accident because an accident depends on a substance for existence. If the first emanation were an accident, it would cause substances, leading to circularity (the accident being prior to substance while substance is prior to accident). This is impossible.
Circularity (دور) is a logical contradiction.
4
Follow the elimination of body and parts
Having established it is a substance, the author examines whether it is a body, a part of a body, or neither. A body is composed of hyle and form, so it cannot be first because its parts must precede it. A part like hyle is passive, while the first emanation is an active cause. Thus, it must be neither body nor part, hence incorporeal.
The argument ends before explicitly stating the positive conclusion, but it is implied.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the first emanation must be an incorporeal substance (often identified with the First Intellect). Write down the three main eliminations: not accident, not body, not part of body.
This argument is a standard proof in Islamic philosophy for the non-material nature of the first created intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : ثبت : أنه تعالى فرد منزه عن جميع جهات الكثرة ، وثبت : أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، فوجب القطع بأن الصادر الأول (۱) عن الله تعالى شيء واحد. فنقول : ثبت أن كل ممكن فهو إما جوهر وإما عرض ، فذلك الصادر الأول إما جوهر وإما عرض . لا جائز أن يكون عرضاً ، لأن العرض محتاج إلى الجوهر ، ومتأخر بالرتبة عن الجوهر . فلو كان الصادر الأول عرضاً ، لكان علة للجواهر ، فحينئذ يكون العرض سابقاً على الجوهر ، مع أن الجوهر كان سابقاً على العرض، فيلزم الدور، وهو محال . فيثبت : أن الصادر الأول جوهر . فنقول : ذلك الجوهر إما أن يكون جسماً ، أو جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم ، أو لا جسماً ولا جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم . والأول باطل لأن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. فتكوين الجسم لا يمكن إلا بتقدم تكوين أجزائه . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون الصادر الأول جسمها . ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول جزءاً من أجزاء ماهية الجسم ، لأن ذلك الجزء إما الهيولي أو الصورة، ولا جائز أن يكون الصادر الأول : هو الهيولى ، لأن الصادر الأول يكون علة لما بعده ، فلو كان الصادر الأول هيولى ، لزم كون الهيولى علة مؤثرة ، موجدة لسائر الأشياء. لكن الهيولي موجود قابل ، فيلزم أن
They said: It is established that He, exalted, is one, transcending all aspects of multiplicity, and it is established that from the one only one proceeds, so it is necessary to decide that the first emanation from God, exalted, is one thing. We say: It is established that every possible entity is either a substance or an accident, so that first emanation is either a substance or an accident. It is not permissible for it to be an accident, because an accident is in need of a substance and is posterior in rank to the substance. If the first emanation were an accident, it would be a cause of substances, and then the accident would be prior to the substance, while the substance was prior to the accident, leading to circularity, which is absurd. Thus it is established that the first emanation is a substance. We say: That substance is either a body, or a part of the parts of the essence of body, or neither a body nor a part of the parts of the essence of body. The first is false because a body is composed of hyle and form. The formation of a body is impossible except by the prior formation of its parts. If this is so, it is impossible for the first emanation to be a body. Nor is it permissible for the first emanation to be a part of the parts of the essence of body, because that part is either the hyle or the form, and it is not permissible for the first emanation to be the hyle, because the first emanation is a cause of what follows it; if the first emanation were hyle, then hyle would be an efficient cause, bringing into existence other things. But hyle is a passive recipient, so it would entail that...
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the first emanation from God (الصادر الأول) is a single, non-corporeal substance. The author begins by citing the established principle that God is absolutely one and transcendent, and from the one only one can proceed. Therefore, the first emanation must be one thing. Next, he classifies all possible entities into either substance (جوهر) or accident (عرض). The first emanation must be one of these. He argues it cannot be an accident because an accident depends on a substance and is posterior to it. If the first emanation were an accident, it would be the cause of substances, making the accident prior to the substance, which contradicts the natural priority of substance over accident. This leads to a circularity (دور), which is impossible. Thus the first emanation must be a substance. Then he examines this substance: it must be either a body (جسم), a part of the essence of a body (like hyle or form), or neither. It cannot be a body because a body is composed of hyle and form, and composition requires priority of parts, which would contradict the first emanation being the first. It cannot be a part of the essence of body, such as hyle, because hyle is a passive recipient, while the first emanation is an active cause. The argument is cut off before concluding that it must be a non-corporeal substance (i.e., an intellect or soul).

Key Terms
الصادر الأول (the first emanation)
جوهر (substance)
عرض (accident)
جسم (body)
دور (circularity)
هيولى (hyle)
صورة (form)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the first emanation is a physical body like the heavens or a celestial sphere.
Correction: The argument shows that the first emanation cannot be a body because a body is composed of parts and thus not simple. It must be an incorporeal substance, typically identified with the First Intellect.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the first emanation be an accident according to the argument?
A) Because accidents are impermanent.
B) Because an accident depends on a substance and would cause circular priority.
C) Because accidents are multiple.
D) Because accidents are created.

Key Takeaway
The first emanation must be a single incorporeal substance because it cannot be an accident (due to circular priority) nor a body (due to composition) nor a part of a body (due to passivity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If from the One only one can proceed, what does that say about the absolute uniqueness of God's creative act? In what ways do you subtly treat God's power as if it were divided or multiple in your daily life?
?
The proof eliminates accident, body, and part of body for the first emanation. Can you identify any of your own mental images of creation that unintentionally attribute composite or physical properties to God's first act? How can you purify your belief in accordance with this proof?
?
The first emanation is a simple substance, not dependent on anything else. Contrast that with your own existence—utterly dependent on God. Are there areas where you live as if you are an independent 'substance,' forgetting that you are a contingent being? What step can you take today to consciously reaffirm your dependence on Allah?

First Objection: The Weakness of the Body Argument
First objection critiques the body composition argument and offers a proof via imaginary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first objection challenges the original proof that the first effect is not a body by criticizing its reliance on the matter-form composition theory, and how an alternative proof using imaginary division is proposed but noted to be incompatible with the philosophers' principles.

Core Idea
A correct conclusion can rest on a weak argument.

In this passage, a theologian raises a critical objection to the earlier proof that the first emanation cannot be a body. He argues that the proof is built on shaky grounds—the idea that a body is composed of prime matter and form—which he claims is a weak theory. He then provides what he considers a stronger proof based on imaginary division, but notes that even this proof does not align with the philosophers' own principles. This objection teaches us the importance of sound argumentation, even when the conclusion is correct.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection's target
Notice that the objector does not deny the conclusion (that the first effect is not a body); he only attacks the method used to prove it. Read the first line carefully.
The objector says 'You have only refuted... by building on...' implying the method is flawed.
2
Follow the alternative proof
The objector offers a new proof: every body is subject to imaginary division, so it is composed of parts. From here he presents two scenarios for causation, both leading to contradictions.
Pay attention to the two 'if we say' branches and their implications.
3
Evaluate the objector's final verdict
He says the alternative proof is 'good' but does not conform to the philosophers' principles. Consider why that might be a problem for the audience.
The objector seems to accept the conclusion but questions whether the proof works within the philosophers' own framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول : إنكم إنما أبطلتم كون المعلول الأول جسماً، بالبناء على أن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. وسنبين أن كلام القوم في تقرير هذا الأصل في غاية الضعف، وحينئذ يبطل هذا الكلام . ثم نقول : الأولى أن يقال : الدليل على أن المعلول الأول ليس هو الجسم : أن كل جسم فإنه يقبل القسمة الوهمية ، وكل ما كان قابلاً للقسمة الوهمية ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون في نفسه مؤلفاً من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، فإن القسمة ليست عبارة عن إحداث الإثنينية والتعدد بل القسمة عبارة عن تفريق المتجاورين. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن قلنا : إنه تعالى علة جميع تلك الأجزاء ، فقد صدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد ، وهو محال . وإن قلنا : إنه تعالى علة الجزء واحد منها ، ثم ذلك الجزء علة للجزء الثاني ، إلى آخر الأجزاء . فههنا يلزم منه مجالان : الأول : إنه يلزم في جميع ذوات العالم أن يكون كل واحد منها بالنسبة إلى الآخر . إما أن يكون علة له ، أو معلولاً له . وهو باطل . والثاني : وهو أن الأجزاء متماثلة في تمام الماهية ، فيمتنع كون بعضها علة للبعض . وبهذا الطريق يثبت : أن المعلول الأول يمتنع أن يكون جسماً . وهذا الدليل جيد ، إلا أنه لا يلائم أصول الفلاسفة .
The first question: You have only refuted that the first effect (al-ma'lul al-awwal) is a body by building on the premise that the body is composed of prime matter and form. And we will show that the people's discourse in establishing this principle is extremely weak, and then this speech is invalidated. Then we say: It is better to say: The proof that the first effect is not the body is that every body is subject to imaginary division (al-qisma al-wahmiyya), and whatever is subject to imaginary division must in itself be composed of parts and segments, because division is not the creation of duality and multiplicity but rather the separation of contiguous things. When this is established, we say: If we say that He, exalted, is the cause of all those parts, then from the one more than one has issued, which is impossible. And if we say that He, exalted, is the cause of one part of them, and then that part is the cause of the second part, up to the rest of the parts, then two implications follow: First, it would follow that among all the essences (dhawat) of the world, each one in relation to the other is either a cause or an effect. And this is false. Second, that the parts are identical in complete quiddity (al-mahiyya), so it is impossible for some to be the cause of others. And by this way it is established that the first effect cannot be a body. And this proof is good, except that it does not conform to the principles of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (السؤال الأول) to the earlier proof that the first emanation is not a body. The objector argues that the original proof relied on the theory that a body is composed of prime matter (hayula) and form (sura). The objector claims this theory is weak and thus the proof collapses. He then offers an alternative proof: every body is subject to imaginary division, meaning it can be mentally partitioned into parts. This shows the body is composed of parts. If God were the cause of all these parts directly, then from the One (who is simple) many effects would issue, which is impossible (since from one only one can proceed). If God were the cause of only one part, and that part causes the next, and so on, then we would have a chain where each part is either cause or effect of another, which is false because it would require that all things in the world are related as cause-effect, and also the parts are identical in essence, so one cannot be the cause of another. Thus the first effect cannot be a body. The objector notes that this proof is valid but does not align with the philosophers' own principles. The objection thus challenges the foundation of the original argument and offers a different reasoning to the same conclusion, but with the implication that the original was flawed.

Key Terms
معلول أول
جسم
قسمة وهمية
أجزاء
ماهية
علة
فلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector fully rejects the conclusion that the first emanation is not a body.
Correction: The objector actually accepts the conclusion; he only criticizes the method used to prove it and offers an alternative proof.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, why does the original argument fail?
A) It assumes the first emanation is a body.
B) It relies on the weak premise that the body is composed of matter and form.
C) It uses imaginary division incorrectly.
D) It contradicts the hadith about the intellect.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that even a correct conclusion can be supported by a flawed argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a truth about God using an argument you later realized was weak? How did that affect your confidence? What would it take for you to build a more solid foundation for your beliefs?
?
The objector replaced a flawed argument with one based on logical implications. In your own spiritual life, do you rely on weak justifications for your practices or beliefs? Will you seek stronger proofs, even if it means confronting uncomfortable implications?

Second Objection: The First Emanation Could Be Prime Matter
Objection: Why cannot the first effect be prime matter?

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection challenges the impossibility of prime matter being the first emanation by undermining the proof based on the principle 'from the one only one proceeds'.

Core Idea
If the key proof fails, prime matter remains a candidate for the first emanation.

After granting that the first effect is not a body, the objector now asks: why can't it be prime matter? The earlier proof argued that prime matter cannot be both active and receptive. But this objection reveals that the proof relies on a principle that has already been refuted. The objector skillfully turns the tables, showing that the original argument's foundation is shaky.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection in Arabic and translation
First, read the full Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Note the concessive 'سلمنا' and the question 'فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هو الهيولى؟'.
The objector does not actually accept that the first effect is not a body; he grants it for argument's sake.
2
Identify the core challenge
Focus on the phrase 'لا نسلم أن هذا محال' (we do not concede that this is impossible). The objector is rejecting the impossibility of a thing being both receptive and active.
3
Trace the logical dependence
The objector explains that the original proof that something cannot be both active and receptive is based on the principle 'من الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد' (from one only one proceeds). He then asserts that this principle has already been refuted ('سلف إبطاله').
Understanding this dependency is key to seeing why the objection works.
4
Reflect on the dialectical strategy
Consider why the objector first concedes that the first effect is not a body. This allows him to focus the debate on prime matter. He then attacks the proof's foundation rather than the conclusion directly.
This is a common dialectical move in kalam: conceding a point to expose a flaw elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أن المعلول الأول ليس جسماً . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هو الهيولى ؟ أما قوله : ( يلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد ، قابلا وفاعلا وهو محال ، فنقول : لا نسلم أن هذا محال . فإن الدليل الذي تعوّلون عليه في إبطال هذه القاعدة هو قولكم : ( لو كان الشيء قابلاً وفاعلا معاً ، لزم يصدر عن الشيء الواحد ، أثران : الفعل والقبول . وهو محال ، إلا أن هذا الكلام مبني على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد . وقد سلف إبطاله
The second question: We grant that the first effect is not a body. So why is it not permissible for it to be prime matter (al-hayula)? As for his saying: 'It would follow that one thing is receptive and active simultaneously, and that is impossible,' we say: We do not concede that this is impossible. For the proof that you rely on to refute this rule is your saying: 'If a thing were receptive and active together, then from one thing two effects would proceed: action and reception. And that is impossible.' Except that this statement is built on the principle that from the one only one can proceed. And its refutation has already preceded.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (السؤال الثاني). The objector first concedes that the first effect is not a body (perhaps accepting the alternative proof from the first objection). He then asks: why can't it be prime matter (hayula)? The original proof had argued that prime matter cannot be the first emanation because that would require a single thing (the prime matter) to be both receptive and active simultaneously, which is impossible. The objector challenges this impossibility. He points out that the original proof's rejection of something being both active and receptive was based on the principle 'from the one only one can proceed' (al-wahid la yasduru 'anhu illa al-wahid). But that principle itself has already been refuted (presumably earlier in the text). Therefore, the impossibility is not established, and prime matter remains a possible candidate for the first emanation. This objection thus undercuts a key premise in the argument for the intellect. The passage ends abruptly; the full response to this objection likely follows on later pages.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The objector only grants it for the sake of argument ('سلمنا') to proceed to the main point about prime matter.

Checkpoint
On what basis does the second objection reject the claim that prime matter cannot be the first effect?
A) Prime matter is not a body, so it is allowed.
B) The impossibility of something being both active and receptive relies on the principle 'from one only one proceeds,' which has been refuted.
C) The first effect must be a body, so prime matter is impossible.
D) The proof that prime matter is impossible is found in revelation.

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that the proof against prime matter being the first effect is only as strong as the principle 'from the one only one proceeds,' which has been refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector does not dismiss the opponent’s argument outright but traces it back to a refuted principle. In your own life, when you hold a strong belief, have you ever examined whether it rests on a principle you have not truly verified? Which belief might you need to re-examine today?
?
The principle 'from the one only one proceeds' might seem abstract, but consider: do you ever limit God’s power by assuming that a single cause can only produce a single effect, perhaps in how you view prayer, salah, or dua? How could this lesson change how you trust in the boundless creativity of Allah?
?
The objector concedes a premise for the sake of argument, but does not abandon his overall position. In your disagreements with others—about faith, life, or relationships—do you listen to their points and build bridges, or do you reject everything at once? What step can you take this week to engage with an opposing view charitably without compromising truth?

Proof that the First Effect Cannot Be a Body
Al-Razi critiques and replaces the philosophers' proof with a stronger argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's alternative proof that the first effect cannot be a body, based on the impossibility of a body's composition leading to multiple effects from a single cause or an infinite chain.

Core Idea
A body's divisibility proves it cannot be the first effect of the One.

Al-Razi presents a powerful argument against the philosophers' claim that the first effect is a body. He first critiques their reliance on matter-form composition, then offers his own proof based on the divisibility of bodies. This proof avoids their weak premises and shows the impossibility of a body being the first effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: critique followed by a new proof.
Notice how al-Razi begins by dismissing the philosophers' principle.
2
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two parts: (1) critique of the philosophers' argument, (2) al-Razi's alternative proof. Separate them in your mind.
3
Understand the alternative proof
Follow the logic: every body is divisible -> composed of parts -> either God causes all parts (impossible) or causes one part leading to a chain (two problems).
The two problems are key: all things would be causes/effects, and identical quiddities cannot cause each other.
4
Reflect on al-Razi's final remark
Al-Razi says the proof is good but does not conform to the philosophers' principles. Think about why this is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول: إنكم إنما أبطلتم كون المعلول الأول جسماً، بالبناء على أن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة. وسنبين أن كلام القوم في تقرير هذا الأصل في غاية الضعف، وحينئذ يبطل هذا الكلام. ثم نقول: الأولى أن يقال: الدليل على أن المعلول الأول ليس هو الجسم: أن كل جسم فإنه يقبل القسمة الوهمية، وكل ما كان قابلاً للقسمة الوهمية، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون في نفسه مؤلفاً من الأجزاء والأبعاض، فإن القسمة ليست عبارة عن إحداث الإثنينية والتعدد بل القسمة عبارة عن تفريق المتجاورين. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن قلنا: إنه تعالى علة الجميع تلك الأجزاء، فقد صدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد، وهو محال. وإن قلنا: إنه تعالى علة الجزء واحد منها، ثم ذلك الجزء علة للجزء الثاني، إلى آخر الأجزاء. فههنا يلزم منه مجالان: الأول: إنه يلزم في جميع ذوات العالم أن يكون كل واحد منها بالنسبة إلى الآخر إما أن يكون علة له، أو معلولاً له. وهو باطل. والثاني: وهو أن الأجزاء متماثلة في تمام الماهية، فيمتنع كون بعضها علة للبعض. وبهذا الطريق يثبت: أن المعلول الأول يمتنع أن يكون جسماً. وهذا الدليل جيد، إلا أنه لا يلائم أصول الفلاسفة.
The first question: You have only invalidated that the first effect is a body, by building on the premise that the body is composed of matter and form. And we will show that the speech of the people in establishing this principle is extremely weak, and then this speech is invalidated. Then we say: It is better to say: The evidence that the first effect is not the body is that every body is divisible in estimation (al-qismah al-wahmiyyah), and everything that is divisible in estimation necessarily is in itself composed of parts and limbs, for division is not the expression of producing duality and multiplicity but division is the expression of separating what is adjacent. If this is established, we say: If we say that He, exalted, is the cause of all those parts, then from the One more than one has proceeded, and that is impossible. And if we say that He, exalted, is the cause of one part of them, then that part is the cause of the second part, up to the last of the parts. Then two aspects follow: The first: that it follows in all substances of the world that each one of them, in relation to the other, is either a cause of it or an effect of it. And that is false. And the second: that the parts are identical in complete quiddity (al-mahiyyah), so it is impossible for some of them to be a cause of others. And by this way it is established that the first effect is impossible to be a body. And this evidence is good, except that it does not conform to the principles of the philosophers.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is the first objection and answer in a discussion about the nature of the first effect. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, begins by stating that the philosophers (the 'opponents') have argued that the first effect cannot be a body because a body is composed of matter and form. He then criticizes their argument, saying he will show that their principle is weak. Instead, he offers an alternative proof: every body is divisible in estimation (i.e., imaginable as divided into parts). Anything that is so divisible must be composed of parts. Division is not the creation of duality but the separation of adjacent parts. Now, if the first effect were a body composed of parts, then either God is directly the cause of all those parts, which would mean multiple effects proceed from the One—impossible—or God causes only one part, and that part causes the next, etc. This leads to two problems: (1) every entity in the world would be either a cause or effect of another, which is false; (2) the parts are identical in quiddity, so one cannot be the cause of the other. Thus the first effect cannot be a body. Al-Razi notes that this proof is good but does not fit the philosophers' own principles.

Key Terms
المعلول الأول (al-ma'lul al-awwal)
القسمة الوهمية (al-qismah al-wahmiyyah)
الهيولى والصورة (al-hayula wa al-surah)
الماهية (al-mahiyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi completely rejects the philosophers' proof without offering his own.
Correction: Al-Razi critiques the philosophers' weak premise but provides a superior independent proof based on divisibility.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's proof, why can't the first effect be a body?
A) Because God cannot create a body.
B) Because a body is divisible, leading to impossible consequences from a single cause.
C) Because the philosophers proved it with weak arguments.
D) Because the first effect must be simple and indivisible.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that the first effect cannot be a body because its divisibility leads to either multiple effects from a single cause or an impossible causal chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi critiques even the most revered philosophers. What cherished belief or opinion do you hold that you have never seriously questioned? Will you examine its foundations today, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The proof shows that absurd consequences follow from assuming a composite first effect. Do you ever accept a belief without following its logical implications? Where in your life might you be tolerating a contradiction that leads to deeper error?
?
If the first effect cannot be a body, then the cause of all existence is utterly unlike anything we perceive. Does your daily life reflect this truth, or do you act as if God is like other causes you know? What one change can you make today to align your actions with God's absolute uniqueness?

Al-Razi's Refutation of the Objection on Matter and Recipient-Agent Duality
Al-Razi refutes the second objection about the first effect being matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi refutes the objection that the first effect cannot be matter by showing that his opponents' own theology accepts a single entity being both receptive and active.

Core Idea
Al-Razi turns his opponents' principles against them to prove a thing can be both receptive and active.

In this passage, Al-Razi tackles a subtle objection about the first effect. The opponent argues that if the first effect were matter, it would have to be both receptive and active, which they consider impossible. Al-Razi systematically dismantles this argument, first by questioning the underlying premise, then by showing that their own theological commitments already accept such duality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The opponent grants the first effect is not a body but suggests it could be matter, claiming this is impossible because it makes one thing both receptive and active.
This is a common kalām move: pushing the opponent's position to a supposed absurdity.
2
Follow Al-Razi's initial response
Al-Razi first rejects the impossibility claim, pointing out that the opponent's proof relies on the principle 'from one only one' which he says has already been refuted. He then even if accepting that principle, questions why reception is considered an effect.
Al-Razi is careful not to commit to the opposite, but rather shows inconsistency.
3
Explore the three counterexamples
Al-Razi presents three examples from their own theology: God's knowledge, the attribute of agency, and the determination of the Necessary Existent, each showing a single thing as both receptive and active.
These examples are designed to be irrefutable to the opponent.
4
Reflect on the implications
These counterexamples undermine the opponent's objection, supporting the possibility that the first effect could be matter without logical contradiction.

السؤال الثاني: سلمنا أن المعلول الأول ليس جسماً. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون هو الهيولى؟ أما قوله: (يلزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد، قابلا وفاعلا وهو محال) فنقول: لا نسلم أن هذا محال. فإن الدليل الذي تعوّلون عليه في إبطال هذه القاعدة هو قولكم: (لو كان الشيء قابلاً وفاعلا معاً، لزم يصدر عن الشيء الواحد، أثران: الفعل والقبول. وهو محال، إلا أن هذا الكلام مبني على أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد. وقد سلف إبطاله. سلمنا: أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد. فلم قلتم: إن القبول أثر؟ والدليل عليه: أنه لو كان القبول أثراً، لكان القابل مؤثراً، فحينئذ يصدق أن القابل فاعل. وهو عندكم محال. سلمنا ما ذكرتم من الدليل. إلا أنه منقوض على أصولكم بصور: الأولى: إنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات. والعلم عبارة عن حضور صورة المعلوم في ذات العالم. فصور المعلومات مرتسمة في ذات الله تعالى، فذاته قابلة لتلك الصور، والمؤثر فيها هو ذاته. فالشيء الواحد قابل وفاعل معاً، وذلك ينقض هذا الكلام. الثانية: إن كون المؤثر مؤثراً في الأثر صفة مغايرة لذات المؤثر. لأن ذات المؤثر جوهر قائم بالنفس، والمفهوم من كونه مؤثراً في الغير حالة نسبية إضافية. فأحدهما يغاير الآخر، فهذه المؤثرية صفة زائدة على الذات، قائمة بتلك الذات. والموجب لتلك المؤثرية هو تلك الذات، فالشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى تلك المؤثرية فاعلة لها، وقابلة لها. والثالثة: إن ذات واجب الوجود من حيث إنه هو واجب الوجود لذاته: متعينة متشخصة، وماهية وجوب الوجود مغايرة لذلك التعين، وإلا لكان كل من عرف الذات الواجبة لذاتها، عرف أنها ليست إلا ذلك المتعين، فوجب أن لا نحتاج في العلم بأن واجب الوجود واحد، إلى دليل منفصل. ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا: أن المفهوم من كونه واجب الوجود لذاته، غير المفهوم من ذلك التعين ومن ذلك التشخص. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إنه لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك التعين مقتضياً لوجوب الوجود بالذات، لما أن واجب الوجود لذاته، يمتنع أن يكون واجب الوجود لغيره، فلم يبق لا أن يقال: المفهوم من كونه واجب الوجود لذاته، هو الذي يوجب ذلك.
The second question: We grant that the first effect is not a body. So why may it not be matter? As for his saying: 'It necessitates that one thing be receptive and active, and that is impossible,' we say: We do not accept that this is impossible. For the evidence that you rely on in invalidating this rule is your saying: 'If a thing were receptive and active together, then two effects would issue from one thing: action and reception, and that is impossible,' except that this speech is based on the principle that from the one only one proceeds, and its refutation has already preceded. We grant that from the one only one proceeds. So why do you say that reception is an effect? The evidence for it is that if reception were an effect, then the receptive would be an agent, and then it would be true that the receptive is an agent, and that is impossible according to you. We grant what you have mentioned of the evidence. Except that it is contradicted by your own principles in [several] forms: The first: He, exalted, is knower of all things known. And knowledge is the presence of the form of the known in the essence of the knower. So the forms of the known things are imprinted in the essence of God, exalted, so His essence is receptive of those forms, and the agent in them is His essence. So one thing is receptive and active together, and that contradicts this speech. The second: That the agent's being an agent in the effect is an attribute distinct from the essence of the agent. For the essence of the agent is a self-subsistent substance, and the concept of its being an agent in another is a relational, relative state. So one differs from the other, so this 'acting' (al-mu'thiriyyah) is an attribute additional to the essence, subsisting in that essence. And the necessitator of that acting is that essence. So one thing, in relation to that acting, is active of it and receptive of it. The third: That the essence of the Necessary Existent inasmuch as it is necessary existent in itself is determined and particularized, and the quiddity of necessary existence is different from that determination. Otherwise, everyone who knew the necessary essence in itself would know that it is nothing but that determinate thing, and therefore we would not need, in knowing that the Necessary Existent is one, a separate proof. And since it is not so, we know that the concept of its being necessary existent in itself is other than the concept of that determination and that particularization. If this is established, we say: It is not permissible for that determination to be a requirer of necessary existence in essence, because the necessary existent in essence is impossible to be necessary existent through another. So nothing remains except that it be said: The concept of its being necessary existent in itself is what necessitates that.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is the second objection-and-answer in a series. The opponent grants that the first effect is not a body but asks why it cannot be matter (hayula). The opponent argues that if it were matter, then one thing (the first effect) would be both receptive (qabil) of form and active (fa'il) in receiving it, which they deem impossible. Al-Razi responds by deconstructing their argument. First, he notes that their proof against a thing being both receivable and active is based on the principle 'from one only one proceeds,' which he claims has already been refuted. Then, even if he accepts that principle, he questions why reception (qabul) is considered an effect? If it were an effect, the receptive would be an agent, which they reject. Al-Razi then says he accepts their evidence but shows it is contradicted by their own principles in three forms (suwar): (1) God's knowledge: God knows all things, and knowledge is the presence of the forms of the known in the knower. So God's essence is receptive of those forms, and God's essence is also the agent that produces them. Thus one thing (God) is both receptive and active. (2) The attribute of being an agent (mu'thiriyya) is an additional attribute distinct from the essence. The essence is both the cause of that attribute (active) and the subject in which it inheres (receptive). (3) The essence of the Necessary Existent is determined (mut'ayyan) and that determination is distinct from the quiddity of necessary existence. The essence is both that which necessitates determination (active) and that which is characterized by it (receptive). Each of these examples shows that according to their own principles, a single thing can be both receptive and active, thus refuting their objection.

Key Terms
الهيولى (al-hayula)
القابل (al-qabil)
الفاعل (al-fa'il)
الأثر (al-athar)
المؤثرية (al-mu'thiriyyah)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)
التعين (al-ta'ayyun)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi denies the principle 'from one only one' outright.
Correction: Al-Razi only questions its application here and provides counterexamples that show it does not preclude a thing being both receptive and active.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the counterexamples al-Razi uses to show that a single thing can be both receptive and active?
A) God's knowledge involves reception of forms in His essence.
B) The attribute of being an agent (mu'thiriyya) is both caused by and subsists in the essence.
C) The determination of the Necessary Existent is distinct from its quiddity, and the essence necessitates it.
D) The first effect as matter must be both receptive and active of form.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's refutation shows that the objection is invalid because the opponent's own theology already accepts a single thing being both receptive and active.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself rejecting a truth about God based on your own limited reasoning, like the opponent who thought one thing cannot be both active and receptive? How can al-Razi's method of turning your own principles against you lead you to examine your assumptions more deeply?
?
Al-Razi shows that God's essence is receptive to the forms of known things—yet He is also the agent of that knowledge. How does this dual aspect of God's nature affect your understanding of His omniscience? Does it humble you to realize that even in receiving, God is sovereign?
?
In your own life, you are sometimes active (initiating) and sometimes receptive (learning, growing). Is there a contradiction? How does al-Razi's argument encourage you to embrace both roles without seeing them as opposed, but as complementary aspects of a single reality?

Six Counterexamples Against the Mutual Exclusivity of Receiver and Agent
Refutation with six counterexamples against the opponent's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses six counterexamples to refute the opponent's claim that a single thing cannot be both receiver and agent.

Core Idea
One thing can be both receiver and agent; the opponent's own principles prove it.

The author presents a powerful refutation by showing that even on the opponent's own assumptions, a single entity can act as both receiver and agent. Through six distinct cases—from divine knowledge to numbers to prime matter—he demonstrates that the distinction between receiver and agent is not absolute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage to grasp the overall structure
Identify the opponent's proof, the author's concession, and then the six counterexamples.
The passage is dense; read through once before analyzing details.
2
Focus on each counterexample
For each example, identify the single thing that is both receiver and agent. Note how each works within the opponent's own framework.
The first example uses God's knowledge; the second uses the attribute of agency; the third uses necessary existence; the fourth uses numbers; the fifth uses prime matter; the sixth (partial) uses the preserving power.
3
Notice the common pattern
In each case, the same entity both necessitates an attribute and receives it. This shows that the concepts are not mutually exclusive.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this refutation is effective against the opponent's position. How does it undermine their argument?
The opponent claimed such a combination is impossible, but the author shows it occurs in many familiar cases.

الخامسة : إن الهيولى قابلة للصورة. فإما أن يكون كونها قابلة للصورة : عين تلك الذات، أو أمر زائد عليه . والأول باطل . لأن ذات القابل جوهر قائم بالنفس ، والقابلية صفة نسبية ، فوجب التغاير . وإذا ثبت التغاير . فنقول : هذا القبول الا يجوز [ أن يكون عدمياً (4) ] لأنه نقيض اللاقبول ، وهو عدم ، ونقيض العدم : وجود . فيثبت : أن القبول صفة موجودة ، مغايرة لتلك الذات. فنقول : المقتضي لهذه الصفة، إما تلك الذات أو غيرها ، والثاني (٥) باطل ، لأنه لو كان حصول تلك القابلية ، لأجل سبب منفصل . لوجب كون تلك الذات، قابلة لتلك القابلية ، فتكون كل قابلية مسبوقة . بقابلية أخرى. إلى غير النهاية. وهو محال . فيثبت : أن المقتضي لتلك القابلية هو تلك الذات ، والموصوف بها أيضاً تلك الذات . فالذات الواحدة قابلة وفاعلة معاً . وهو المطلوب
السادسة : إن القوة الحافظة التي تحفظ مثل المحسوسات ، ورسومها وأشباحها . هل قبلت تلك الرسوم والأشباح، أم لم تقبلها ؟ فإن لم تقبلها ،
Effect? The proof for it is: if acceptance were an effect, then the receiver would be an agent, in which case it would be true that the receiver is an agent. And according to you, that is impossible. We concede the proof you have mentioned. However, it is refuted according to your principles by [the following] cases:
First: He, exalted, is knower of all known things. Knowledge is the presence of the form of the known in the essence of the knower. So the forms of known things are imprinted in the essence of God, exalted, so His essence is a receiver for those forms, and the agent in them is His essence. So the single thing is receiver and agent together, and that refutes this statement.
Second: The fact that the agent is an agent in the effect is an attribute distinct from the essence of the agent. Because the essence of the agent is a self-subsistent substance, and the concept of its being an agent in another is a relational additive state. So one differs from the other. So this agency is an attribute superadded to the essence, subsisting in that essence. And the necessitator of that agency is that essence. So the single thing with respect to that agency is its agent and its receiver.
Third: The essence of the Necessary Existence, inasmuch as it is necessary of existence in itself, is determined and individuated, and the quiddity of necessity of existence is different from that determination. Otherwise, whoever knew the Necessary Essence in itself would know that it is nothing but that determined one, so it would be necessary that we would not need a separate proof to know that the Necessary Existence is one. And since it is not so, we know that the concept of its being necessary of existence in itself is different from the concept of that determination and that individuation. If this is established, we say: it is not permissible that that determination be a requisite of necessity of existence in itself, because the Necessary Existence in itself cannot be necessary of existence through another. So nothing remains but to say: the concept of its being necessary of existence in itself is what necessitates that determination. So on this, the agent in the occurrence of that determination and the receiver of it are one thing.
And the fourth: Three necessitates oddness, and four necessitates evenness. The proof for it is: if the necessitator of this oddness and evenness were something else, then when that thing ceases, it would be necessary that three would no longer be odd and four even. And that is impossible. So it is established that the necessitator of oddness is three's being three, and the necessitator of evenness is four's being four. Then the described by this oddness is three, and by this evenness is four. So the single thing is agent and receiver together. And that is the desired.
The fifth: Hyle is receptive to form. So either its being receptive to form is identical to that essence, or something superadded to it. The first is false, because the essence of the receiver is a self-subsistent substance, and receptivity is a relative attribute, so difference is necessary. And if difference is established, we say: this acceptance cannot be non-existent, because it is the opposite of non-acceptance, which is non-existence, and the opposite of non-existence is existence. So it is established that acceptance is an existent attribute, distinct from that essence. We say: the necessitator of this attribute is either that essence or something else. The second is false, because if the occurrence of that receptivity were due to a separate cause, then it would be necessary that that essence be receptive to that receptivity, and so every receptivity would be preceded by another receptivity ad infinitum. That is impossible. So it is established that the necessitator of that receptivity is that essence, and the described by it is also that essence. So the single essence is receiver and agent together. That is the desired.
The sixth: The preserving power that preserves perceptibles, their forms and likenesses: did it accept those forms and likenesses or did it not accept them? If it did not accept them,
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument that a thing cannot be both a receiver (قابل) and an agent (فاعل) simultaneously. The author first presents the opponent's proof: if acceptance (القبول) were an effect (أثر), then the receiver would be an agent, which the opponent claims is impossible. The author then concedes the opponent's proof but claims it is refuted by six counterexamples from the opponent's own principles.

The six counterexamples are:
1. God knows all things. Knowledge is the presence of the form of the known in the knower. Since God's essence is the knower, the forms of all known things are in His essence. Thus His essence is a receiver of those forms, and He is also the agent that produces them. So the same thing (God's essence) is both receiver and agent.
2. The attribute of being an agent (المؤثرية) is distinct from the essence of the agent. The essence is a substance, while agency is a relational attribute. The essence itself necessitates this agency, so the essence is both the agent (producing the agency) and the receiver (receiving the agency). Thus one thing is both agent and receiver.
3. The Necessary Existence (واجب الوجود) has a determination (تعين) that is distinct from its quiddity of necessary existence. The concept of necessary existence does not include determination, but the necessary existence itself necessitates that determination. So the necessary existence is both the agent (necessitating the determination) and the receiver (receiving the determination).
4. The number three necessitates oddness, and four necessitates evenness. The same thing (three or four) is both the agent that necessitates the property and the receiver that possesses it. So here a number is both agent and receiver.
5. Hyle (prime matter) is receptive to form. Its receptivity is an attribute distinct from its essence. The essence of hyle necessitates that receptivity. Thus hyle is both agent (necessitating its own receptivity) and receiver (receiving the receptivity). This avoids an infinite regress.
6. The preserving power (القوة الحافظة) preserves perceptibles and their forms. It must have accepted those forms; otherwise it could not preserve them. So it is both receiver (of the forms) and agent (preserving them).

Each example shows that a single thing can be both receiver and agent, refuting the opponent's claim. The author concludes with 'وهو المطلوب' (that is the desired).

Key Terms
القبول (acceptance)
القابل (receiver)
المؤثر (agent)
الأثر (effect)
المؤثرية (agency)
التعين (determination)
واجب الوجود (Necessary Existence)
الهيولى (hyle)
القوة الحافظة (preserving power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A thing cannot be both a receiver and an agent; they are mutually exclusive.
Correction: The author shows that in many cases—divine knowledge, agency attributes, necessary existence, numbers, prime matter, and preserving power—the same entity can be both receiver and agent.

Checkpoint
What is the author's overall argument in this passage?
A) That acceptance is impossible.
B) That a single thing cannot be both receiver and agent.
C) That a single thing can be both receiver and agent, refuting the opponent.
D) That only God can be both receiver and agent.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's claim that a thing cannot be both receiver and agent is refuted by six clear examples from divine attributes, agency, necessity, numbers, prime matter, and preserving power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that God's essence is both receiver and agent of all forms of knowledge, how does this affect your perception of His absolute simplicity? Are you comfortable with the idea that God 'receives' anything? Examine your own assumptions: do you secretly believe that God's knowledge is passive or that He is not intimately aware of every detail? Commit this week to praising God as al-'Alim (the All-Knowing) with the conviction that His knowledge is both active and receptive.
?
The fifth counterexample shows that hyle's essence necessitates its own receptivity, avoiding infinite regress. In your life, do you ever attribute your own responsiveness or receptivity to something outside yourself (e.g., circumstances, people) instead of owning it as part of your created nature? What would it mean to accept that your own essence, as created by God, has inherent capacities that you must activate? Reflect on a specific area where you can take more responsibility for your spiritual receptivity.
?
The fourth counterexample demonstrates that numbers themselves necessitate their properties (three requires oddness). This points to a deep reality: that created things have intrinsic natures that are not arbitrary. Does your daily life reflect a recognition that actions have inherent moral qualities (e.g., justice is good in itself), or do you sometimes treat moral laws as merely external commands? Challenge yourself to identify one action this week that you will perform solely because it is intrinsically good, not because of external reward or punishment.

Causality and Created Things

The Fifth Proof: Hyle and the Reception of Form
Al-Razi proves that matter's receptivity is a real attribute caused by itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that matter's receptivity to form is a real attribute caused by matter itself, making matter both recipient and agent.

Core Idea
One essence can be both active and passive: matter causes its own receptivity.

This passage presents a crisp philosophical proof by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. He examines a fundamental feature of matter—its ability to receive form—and asks what accounts for this ability. Through a series of logical disjunctions, he eliminates external causation and shows that the same essence must be both the cause and the subject of its own receptivity. This conclusion is striking because it blurs the sharp distinction between agent and patient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: if-then, either-or, etc. Al-Razi uses very precise kalam terminology.
2
Identify the three key premises
Locate these premises in the text: (1) Receptivity is not identical to the essence, (2) Receptivity is an existent attribute, (3) The cause of receptivity cannot be external.
Premise (3) uses the infinite regress argument.
3
Work through the infinite regress
Suppose an external cause for receptivity. That would require the essence to be receptive to that cause's effect, implying a prior receptivity, leading to infinite regress. See why Al-Razi finds this impossible.
This is a classic kalam reductio ad absurdum.
4
State the conclusion in your own words
The only option left: the essence itself is the cause of its own receptivity. Thus the same essence is both qābil (recipient) and fā‘il (agent). Explain this in simple terms.
This conclusion is significant for discussions on causality and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : إن الهيولى قابلة للصورة. فإما أن يكون كونها قابلة للصورة عين تلك الذات، أو أمر زائد عليه. والأول باطل. لأن ذات القابل جوهر قائم بالنفس، والقابلية صفة نسبية، فوجب التغاير. وإذا ثبت التغاير. فنقول: هذا القبول لا يجوز أن يكون عدمياً لأنه نقيض اللاقبول، وهو عدم، ونقيض العدم وجود. فيثبت: أن القبول صفة موجودة، مغايرة لتلك الذات. فنقول: المقتضي لهذه الصفة إما تلك الذات أو غيرها، والثاني باطل، لأنه لو كان حصول تلك القابلية لأجل سبب منفصل لوجب كون تلك الذات قابلة لتلك القابلية، فتكون كل قابلية مسبوقة بقابلية أخرى إلى غير النهاية. وهو محال. فيثبت: أن المقتضي لتلك القابلية هو تلك الذات، والموصوف بها أيضاً تلك الذات. فالذات الواحدة قابلة وفاعلة معاً. وهو المطلوب.
The fifth: Indeed hyle is receptive to form. Either its being receptive to form is identical to that essence or an additional matter. The first is false, because the essence of the recipient is a self-subsistent substance, while receptivity is a relative attribute, so difference is necessary. And when difference is established, we say: this receptivity cannot be non-existent because it is the opposite of non-receptivity, which is non-existence, and the opposite of non-existence is existence. So it is established that receptivity is an existent attribute, different from that essence. Then we say: the necessitator of this attribute is either that essence or something else. The second is false, because if that receptivity were due to a separate cause, then that essence would have to be receptive to that receptivity, leading to an infinite regress of receptivities, which is impossible. So it is established that the necessitator of that receptivity is that essence, and the described by it is also that essence. So the single essence is recipient and agent together. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) that the same essence can be both agent and recipient. Al-Razi examines hyle (matter, الهيولى) which receives form. He first argues that the receptivity (qābiliyya) is not identical to the essence of matter because receptivity is a relative attribute, while the essence is a substance. So they are distinct. Then he argues that receptivity is not a mere negation but an existent attribute, because it is the opposite of non-receptivity which is non-existence. Then he asks what causes this receptivity. If it were caused by something external, that would imply an infinite regress (tasalsul) of receptivities, which is impossible. Therefore, the receptivity must be caused by the essence itself. Thus, the same essence is both the cause (agent) of its own receptivity and the recipient of that attribute. Hence, one thing is both fā‘il and qābil.

Key Terms
هيولى
قابلية
صفة نسبية
تسلسل
فاعل
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi's proof implies that matter creates itself.
Correction: Al-Razi does not claim that matter creates its own existence; only that the attribute of receptivity is caused by the essence itself. The essence does not create its own substance, but rather its attribute of being receptive.

Checkpoint
What problem does Al-Razi identify if receptivity were caused by an external cause?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress of receptivities.
B) Matter would have multiple attributes.
C) The essence would be indistinguishable from its attributes.
D) Form would not be received.

Key Takeaway
The same essence can be both the cause and the subject of its own attribute, as demonstrated by matter's receptivity to form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If matter's own essence necessitates its receptivity, what does that say about your own essence? Are you waiting for external circumstances to change you, or can you actively necessitate change in yourself starting now?
?
This proof shows that a single essence can be both active (causing) and passive (receiving). Where in your life do you only see yourself as a passive recipient? What would it look like for you to be the active cause of your own transformation in that area?
?
Al-Razi argues that an external cause would lead to an infinite regress—an impossible chain. Do you rely on external validations that keep you dependent? What would it cost you to break that chain and take responsibility for your own growth?
