The Ninth Proof: First Intellect as Both Recipient and Agent
Al-Razi uses the emanationist model to show the first intellect is both recipient and agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the emanationist framework to argue that the first intellect is both recipient and agent, and why this argument is dialectical rather than a commitment to that ontology.

Core Idea
A single entity can be both recipient and agent when it is possible in itself.

This lesson examines Al-Razi's ninth proof, which draws on the Neoplatonic emanation model to demonstrate that the first intellect is both a recipient of existence from the First Cause and an agent that brings about the second intellect. As you read, notice how Al-Razi uses the concept of 'possible in itself' to establish both roles without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi frames the argument as a rhetorical question addressed to proponents of emanation.
2
Identify the key terms
Look at the Arabic terms for 'possible in itself' (ممكن لذاته), 'agent' (فاعل), and 'recipient' (قابل). Understand how these terms relate to each other in the argument.
The term 'علة أولى' refers to the First Cause (God).
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the argument: Since the first intellect is possible in itself, it must receive existence from another (the First Cause), making it a recipient. Then it acts as a cause for the second intellect, making it an agent.
Note that this reasoning assumes the emanationist framework, but the logical point about possibility and causality stands regardless.
4
Reflect on the dialectical nature
Consider that Al-Razi is not necessarily endorsing emanationism; he is using it as a dialectical tool to show consistency within that system. This is a common method in kalam.
This proof is a direct response to those who accept the emanationist model, not a standalone proof of the first intellect's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسعة : أليس أن عندكم العقل الأول علة للعقل الثاني؟ فنقول : العقل الأول ممكن لذاته، فيكون فاعلاً قابلاً للوجود من العلة الأولى. ثم إنه مؤثر في وجود المعلول الثاني، فهو فاعل. فالشيء الواحد قابل وفاعل معاً.
The ninth: Is it not that according to you the first intellect is cause of the second intellect? So we say: the first intellect is possible in itself, so it is agent and recipient of existence from the First Cause. Then it is effective in the existence of the second effect, so it is agent. So the single thing is recipient and agent together.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the emanationist framework (often associated with Avicenna). Al-Razi addresses those who hold that the first intellect (al-‘aql al-awwal) is the cause of the second intellect (al-‘aql al-thānī). He notes that the first intellect is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātihī), meaning it requires a cause for its existence. Therefore, it is both an agent (fā‘il) in that it actively causes the second intellect, and a recipient (qābil) in that it receives existence from the First Cause (al-‘illa al-ūlā). Thus, the same entity (the first intellect) is both qābil and fā‘il.

Key Terms
عقل أول
عقل ثاني
ممكن لذاته
علة أولى
فاعل
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi is endorsing the Neoplatonic emanationist cosmology as true.
Correction: This is a dialectical argument: Al-Razi uses the emanationist framework to draw a logical conclusion about causality, not to affirm the reality of the emanation schema itself.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's argument, why is the first intellect both agent and recipient?
A) Because it is eternal.
B) Because it is possible in itself and receives existence from the First Cause while causing the second intellect.
C) Because it is immaterial.
D) Because it is self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
In this proof, Al-Razi shows that a single entity can be both a recipient (from a higher cause) and an agent (to a lower effect) when it is possible in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that you are a recipient of every blessing from God? Or do you acknowledge your dependence while still taking responsibility for your actions? How can you balance being a 'recipient' and an 'agent' in your daily life starting today?
?
In what areas of your life are you passive, waiting for things to happen, when you could be an agent of change? And where are you overly active, ignoring your need for God's help? Reflect on one concrete step you can take right now.
?
The first intellect, despite being a cause for the second intellect, is still possible in itself and dependent on the First Cause. How does this challenge your pride or sense of self-reliance? Will you humbly embrace your dependence on God while actively striving to fulfill your purpose?

Ninth Argument: The First Intellect as Both Receptive and Agent
Ninth argument: first intellect as both receptive and agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first intellect can be both receptive of existence from the First Cause and an agent causing the second intellect, and why this does not involve contradiction.

Core Idea
The first intellect is both receptive and agent without contradiction.

This lesson examines the ninth argument from the text, which addresses a potential objection: how can the first intellect be both a recipient of existence and an active cause? The reasoning shows that these roles coexist without conflict. Let's read the passage and unpack its logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical question at the beginning.
2
Identify the key terms

These terms are central to the argument.
3
Understand the argument structure
The passage starts with a rhetorical question, then gives reasoning: the first intellect is possible in itself, so it is agent and receptive. Then it is effective for the second effect, so it is agent. The conclusion combines both roles.
Follow the logical flow from question to conclusion.
4
Reflect on the doctrine
Consider how this argument fits into the broader theory of emanation and causality. Why is it important that the same entity can be both receptive and agent?
This argument defends the coherence of the emanation scheme.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسعة : أليس أن عندكم العقل الأول علة للعقل الثاني ؟ فنقول : العقل الأول ممكن لذاته ، فيكون فاعلاً قابلاً للوجود من العلة الأولى . ثم إنه مؤثر في وجود المعلول الثاني ، فهو فاعل . فالشيء واحد قابل وفاعل معاً .

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth argument. It references the doctrine of the First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal) as the cause of the Second Intellect (al-ʿaql al-thānī). The first intellect is possible (mumkin) in itself, meaning it receives existence from the First Cause (al-ʿilla al-ūlā). In that respect, it is receptive (qābil) of existence. At the same time, it is effective (muʾaththir) in bringing about the existence of its effect (the second intellect), making it agent (fāʿil). Thus the same thing (the first intellect) is both qābil and fāʿil together.

Correction: The first intellect is possible in itself, so it receives existence (receptive) while simultaneously being a cause for the second intellect (agent). These roles are not contradictory.

Twelfth Argument: A Single Thing Can Be Both Receptive and Agent
The twelfth argument with example and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the twelfth argument uses the example of putrefaction causing fever to demonstrate that a single contingent thing can be both receptive to existence from another and an agent of change in another.

Core Idea
A single thing can be both receptive and agent.

This argument builds on the previous eleven forms to directly counter the claim that a thing cannot be both receptive and agent. The author presents a straightforward example from natural causality—putrefaction causing fever—to show that in the world of contingent things, the same entity can receive existence from a prior cause and simultaneously act as a cause for something else. This refutes a key premise of the opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Pay attention to the structure: statement, example, reasoning, conclusion.
2
Identify key terms
Note the terms: ممكن (contingent), علة (cause), عفونة (putrefaction), حمى (fever), قابل (receptive), فاعل (agent). Understand their roles.
The terms 'qabil' and 'fa'il' are central to the argument.
3
Understand the logic
Why does the contingency of the cause make it receptive? Why is it also active? Trace the reasoning.
The cause is contingent, so it receives existence from something else (receptive). It is a cause of change, so it is an agent.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Relate this argument to the opponents' claim that a single thing cannot be both receptive and agent. How does this example refute it?
This is the twelfth of twelve forms that collectively disprove the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية عشر : لا نزاع في أن في الممكنات ما يكون علة لغيره . مثل : إن العفونة توجب الحمّى . فالسبب ممكن ، فيكون قابلا للوجود من غيره وهو علة تغيّره ، فيكون فاعلاً لغيره . فالشيء الواحد قابل وفاعل معاً . فهذه اثنتي عشرة صورة تدل على فساد قولهم : « إن الشيء الواحد يمتنع أن يكون قابلا وفاعلا معاً » .
The twelfth: There is no dispute that among contingent things there is that which is a cause for something else. For example, putrefaction causes fever. So the cause is contingent, so it is receptive of existence from something else, and it is a cause of its change, so it is agent for something else. So the single thing is receptive and agent together. So these are twelve forms that indicate the corruption of their statement: "It is impossible for the single thing to be receptive and agent together."
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth argument and a concluding summary. The argument states that among contingent (mumkināt) things, some are causes for others. Example: putrefaction (ʿufūna) causes fever (ḥummā). The cause (putrefaction) is itself contingent, so it receives existence from something else (prior cause) — thus it is receptive (qābil). At the same time, it is the cause of a change (fever) in something else — thus it is agent (fāʿil). Therefore, the same thing is both qābil and fāʿil. Then the author concludes: these twelve forms (ṣuwar) demonstrate the corruption (fasād) of the opponents' claim that a single thing cannot simultaneously be receptive and agent. This refutes the key premise of those who argue against such coexistence.

Key Terms
ممكن
علة
عفونة
حمى
قابل
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A cause is purely active and never passive.
Correction: This argument shows that a cause is passive (receptive) in relation to its own cause and active in relation to its effect.

Checkpoint

A) That putrefaction is only receptive to existence
B) That fever is only an effect
C) That putrefaction is both receptive (to existence from its own cause) and agent (causing fever)
D) That putrefaction and fever are the same thing

Key Takeaway
The twelfth argument shows that a single contingent thing can be both receptive to existence from another and an agent of change in another, using the example of putrefaction causing fever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience illness like fever, which has a physical cause. But that cause itself is contingent and sustained by Allah. Does this humble you when you think you control your health? How would you react differently if you truly felt that every cause is a sign of Allah's power?
?
You are both a cause for others (through your words and actions) and an effect of countless influences. Are you aware that both your agency and your receptivity come from Allah? Examine a habit where you take full credit for an action—can you see its dependence on Allah's permission?
?
This argument shows that a single thing can be both passive and active. In your relationship with Allah, are you more focused on being passive (submitting) or active (obeying)? Can you think of a moment where you need to be both at once, like accepting His decree while striving to change your situation?

Three Proofs that One Thing can be both Receptive and Agent
Three numbered arguments proving one thing can be both receptacle and agent simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how three rational arguments demonstrate that a single entity can simultaneously possess both receptivity and agency, refuting the claim that these are mutually exclusive.

Core Idea
Agency and receptivity can coexist in one essence, as shown by three decisive proofs.

The claim that one thing cannot be both an agent and a receptacle is a logical error rooted in a misunderstanding of attributes and causation. In this passage, the author presents three numbered arguments—the tenth, eleventh, and twelfth—each from a different angle to prove that agency and receptivity can reside in the same subject. By examining agency as an attribute, necessitation between quiddities, and causal relationships among contingent things, we will see why the opponent's position is untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the three arguments unfold in sequence.
2
Understand the tenth argument
Notice how the author uses the fact that agency is an attribute of the agent: the agent's essence causes this attribute and is receptive to it, making the agent also a receptacle. Similarly for receptivity.
This argument shows the symmetry between agency and receptivity.
3
Examine the eleventh argument
The necessity (luzum) between quiddities is distinct from both essences; the same essence is both the cause of that necessity and the subject described by it.
This argument uses the relationship between quiddities.
4
Consider the twelfth argument
A contingent cause (like putrefaction causing fever) is receptive of existence from another and also an agent of change to another, so it is both.
This argument relies on observable causal relations.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that these twelve images refute the opponent's statement. Ask yourself: why is it important to show that one thing can be both receptive and agent?
This ties into broader theological discussions about divine attributes and causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشرة : قد دللنا على أن الفاعلية صفة مغايرة للمفعول ، وهي قائمة بذات الفاعل، فتلك الذات مؤثرة في حصول تلك الفاعلية وقابلة لها ، فالفاعل قابل . وقد دللنا على أن القابلية صفة قائمة بذات القابل . والمؤثر في حصولها تلك الذات ، والقابل فاعل . فعلى كلا التقديرين : الشيء الواحد قابل وفاعل معاً

الحادية عشر : أليس أن بعض الماهيات لازمة للبعض ؟ فذلك اللزوم مغاير لذات الملزوم ولذات اللازم ثم المؤثر في ذلك الاستلزام هو تلك الذات ، والموصوف به أيضاً تلك الذات، فقد عاد الحديث من أن الواحد قابل وفاعل معاً .

الثانية عشر : لا نزاع في أن في الممكنات ما يكون علة لغيره . مثل : إن العفونة توجب الحمّى . فالسبب ممكن ، فيكون قابلا للوجود من غيره وهو علة تغيّره ، فيكون فاعلاً لغيره . فالشيء الواحد قابل وفاعل معاً .

فهذه اثنتي عشرة صورة تدل على فساد قولهم : « إن الشيء الواحد يمتنع أن يكون قابلا وفاعلا معاً .
The tenth: We have proven that agency (al-fa'iliyya) is an attribute distinct from the effect (al-maf'ul), and it subsists in the essence of the agent, so that essence is effective in the occurrence of that agency and is receptive to it, so the agent is receptive. And we have proven that receptivity (al-qabiliyya) is an attribute subsisting in the essence of the receptacle, and the one effective in its occurrence is that essence, and the receptacle is an agent. So from both considerations: the one thing is both receptive and agent together.

The eleventh: Is it not that some quiddities (al-mahiyyat) are necessary (lazima) for others? That necessity (al-luzum) is other than the essence of the necessary (al-malzum) and the essence of the necessary thing (al-lazim), then the one effective in that necessitation is that essence, and the described by it is also that essence, so the discussion returns to that the one is receptive and agent together.

The twelfth: There is no dispute that among contingent things there are those that are causes (illa) of others, like putrefaction causes fever. So the cause is contingent, so it is receptive of existence from another, and it is a cause of change in another, so it is an agent of another. So the one thing is both receptive and agent together.

So these are twelve images indicating the corruption of their statement: 'It is impossible for one thing to be both receptive and agent together.'
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents three of a series of twelve arguments (the tenth, eleventh, and twelfth) that demonstrate the falsity of the claim that a single thing cannot be both receptive (qabil) and agent (fa'il) simultaneously. The author proceeds in a numbered order. In the tenth argument, he builds on prior proofs: agency is an attribute distinct from the effect and subsists in the agent's essence. That essence is effective in producing agency and is itself receptive to it, so the agent is also a receptacle. Similarly, receptivity is an attribute of the receptacle's essence, and that essence is the effective cause of that receptivity, so the receptacle is also an agent. Thus, from both perspectives, one thing is both receptacle and agent. In the eleventh argument, he considers the relation of necessitation between quiddities: if one quiddity necessarily accompanies another (luzum), that necessity is distinct from both essences. The essence itself is the cause of that necessitation and also the subject described by it, so again the one essence is both receptive (receiving the attribute of necessitation) and active (causing it). In the twelfth argument, he notes that contingent causes, such as putrefaction causing fever, are themselves made to exist by another cause (so they are receptive) while also effecting change in another (so they are agents). Thus, a single thing can combine receptivity and agency. The author concludes that these twelve proofs refute the opponents' claim that it is impossible for one thing to be both receptive and agent.

Key Terms
فاعلية
قابلية
ماهية
لزوم
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Agency and receptivity cannot both be true of the same thing.
Correction: The three arguments demonstrate that the same entity can be both an agent and a receptacle, as shown by the nature of attributes, necessitation, and causal relations.

Checkpoint
What do the three arguments collectively prove?
A) That agency and receptivity are identical attributes.
B) That a single thing can be both receptive and agent together.
C) That prime matter is eternal and uncreated.
D) That forms are independent of matter.

Key Takeaway
A single entity can simultaneously be both receptive and agent, as demonstrated by three independent rational arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are both receptive to Allah's guidance and an agent in your faith journey. Which of these two aspects do you neglect more, and what one concrete step can you take today to restore the balance?
?
If the same essence can be both effective and affected, what does that imply about your own role in your spiritual growth? Are you willing to be both the one who seeks change and the one who is changed by Allah?
?
The twelfth proof uses a simple example: putrefaction causes fever. In your life, where do you see yourself as a contingent cause—shaped by others yet shaping others? How does acknowledging this dual role affect your responsibility before Allah?

Refutation of the Claim that Prime Matters are Equal
Refutation of the second statement that prime matters are equal and effects similar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that prime matters are equal by exposing an internal contradiction in the opponent's position.

Core Idea
Opponents contradict themselves by claiming both equality and distinctness of prime matters.

This passage is a refutation of the second statement made by an opponent. The opponent asserted that prime matters (hayulayat) are all equal, therefore their effects must be similar. The author responds by pointing out a stunning inconsistency: the opponents themselves hold that each celestial sphere has a prime matter distinct in quiddity (mahiyya) from others. The refutation is brief and devastating, relying solely on the opponent's own doctrinal commitments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's claim is stated, then the author's response begins with 'we say'.
The author uses strong language: 'This is among the wonders.'
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent claims: (1) prime matters are equal, (2) therefore their effects must be similar. Note that the conclusion is presented as necessary (wajaba).
3
Understand the author's objection
The author points out that the opponents themselves have said that the prime matter of each sphere differs in quiddity from that of another sphere. This directly contradicts the claim of equality.
The author asks rhetorically: 'How have you abandoned this doctrine in this context?'
4
Reflect on the refutation strategy
This is a classic argument from internal contradiction. The author does not need to prove anything new; he simply shows the opponent's inconsistency.
The phrase 'And God knows best' signals the end of the answer.

أما قوله ثانياً : ( الهيوليات متساوية ، فوجب أن يتشابه الأثر ) فنقول : هذا من العجائب . فإنكم أنتم الذين قلتم : إن هيولى كل فلك ، مخالفة بالماهية لهيولى الفلك الآخر . فكيف تركتم هذا المذهب في هذا المقام ؟ والله أعلم .
As for his second statement: 'Prime matters (al-hayulayat) are equal, so the effect (al-athar) must be similar', we say: This is among the wonders. For you yourselves said that the prime matter (hayula) of each sphere is different in quiddity (mukhalifa bi-l-mahiyya) from the prime matter of the other sphere. So how have you abandoned this doctrine in this context? And God knows best.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's second point. The opponent claimed that prime matters (hayulayat) are all equal, and therefore their effects should be similar. The author responds by pointing out the contradiction: the opponents themselves hold that the prime matter of each celestial sphere is distinct in quiddity from that of another sphere. This is a standard position in Aristotelian cosmology. The author asks how they can abandon their own doctrine in this context. The refutation is brief and relies on exposing internal inconsistency in the opponent's system. The phrase 'And God knows best' concludes the answer.

Can the First Emanation Be a Form?
Q&A defending the form as possible first emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility that the first emanation could be a form, addressing objections about independence and circularity.

Core Idea
The form can cause matter without circularity, so it can be first.

This passage tackles a specific theological puzzle: if the first created being is a form, it must cause matter, but forms are usually dependent on matter. The author systematically refutes objections and offers two solutions to the circularity problem, showing that the form can be both cause and inherent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
Read the opening: the third question asks why the first emanation cannot be a form. Understand the context.
This is a dialectical exchange, typical of kalam.
2
Analyze the first objection and refutation
The objector says if form causes matter, it would be independent, but the author refutes using a dilemma about taqwim (constitution).
Focus on the two alternatives: either form does not constitute matter (then it's just an accident) or it does (then it must do so without matter's participation).
3
Examine the circularity objection
The theologians argue that since form inheres in matter, if it also causes matter, there is circularity. Understand why this is problematic.
Circularity is impossible in causal chains.
4
Understand the two resolutions
The author offers two answers: (1) form causes matter, then matter makes the form inherent; (2) form causes both matter and its own inherence. See how each avoids circularity.
Both solutions preserve the form's causal role without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يكون الصادر الأول هو الصورة ؟ قوله : : إن على هذا التقدير تكون الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى ، وحينئذ لا يكون تأثيرها في وجود الهيولى ، بشركة من الهيولى ، فتكون الصورة في فعلها غنية عن الهيولى ، وكل ما كان غنياً في فعله عن الهيولى ، كان غنياً في ذاته عن الهيولى : فنقول : هذا الكلام : باطل . وذلك لأن هذه الصورة. هل لها تأثير في تقويم الهيولى أم لا ؟ فإن لم يكن لها تأثير في تقويم الهيولى ، فحينئذ لا يبقى فرق بين العرض وبين الصورة ، فلا تكون الصورة قسماً مغايراً للعرض. وإن كان لها تأثير في تقويم الهيولى ، فتأثيرها في تقويم الهيولى ، إن كان بشركة من الهيولى ، لزم تقدم الهيولى على نفسها ، لأن شرط التأثير أن يتقدم بالرتبة على الأثر . فيثبت : أن تأثير الصورة في تقويم هيولاها ، ليس بشركة من تلك الهيولى. ثم إن هذا لا يقدح في كون تلك الصورة: صورة هيولانية . فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر ههنا كذلك ؟ وأيضاً : إن المتكلمين لما قالوا : الصورة لما كانت حالة في الهيولى ، كانت مفتقرة إلى الهيولى ، افتقار الحال إلى المحل فلو كانت علة التقويم الهيولى ، لزم افتقار الهيولى إليها ، فيلزم الدور ، وهو محال ، فهذا يدل على أن القول بالصورة محال . ثم إنا تكلفنا جواباً عن هذا الكلام : فقلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الصورة توجب وجود الهيولى، ثم إنها لا تؤثر في ذات الصورة ، بل تؤثر في جعل تلك الصورة حالة فيها ، وعلى هذا التقدير فالدور ساقط . أو يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الصورة توجب الهيولى ، ثم إنها توجب لنفسها كونها حالة في تلك الهيولى إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الصادر الأول صورة
The third question: Why is it not permissible that the first emanation (al-sadir al-awwal) is the form (al-sura)? His statement: On this estimation, the form would be a cause (illa) of the existence of the prime matter (al-hayula), and then its effect in the existence of the prime matter would not be with participation of the prime matter, so the form in its action would be independent of the prime matter, and everything independent in its action from the prime matter is independent in its essence from the prime matter. So we say: This speech is false. Because this form: does it have an effect in constituting (taqwim) the prime matter or not? If it has no effect in constituting the prime matter, then there is no difference between an accident (al-'arad) and the form, so the form would not be a category distinct from the accident. And if it has an effect in constituting the prime matter, then its effect in constituting the prime matter, if it is with participation of the prime matter, then the priority of the prime matter over itself would be necessary, because the condition of effect is that it is prior in rank to the effect. So it is established that the effect of the form in constituting its prime matter is not with participation of that prime matter. Then this does not detract from the form being a material form (sura hayulaniyya). So why is it not permissible that the matter here is likewise? Also: The mutakallimun (theologians) said: Since the form is inherent (halla) in the prime matter, it is in need of the prime matter, the need of the inherent for the locus (al-mahal). So if it were the cause of the constitution of the prime matter, then the need of the prime matter for it would be necessary, so circularity (al-dawr) would be necessary, and that is impossible. So this indicates that the statement about the form is impossible. Then we took the trouble to give an answer to this speech: We said: Why is it not permissible to say that the form causes the existence of the prime matter, and then it does not affect the essence of the form, but rather affects making that form inherent in it, and on this estimation the circularity drops. Or it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that the form causes the prime matter, and then it causes itself to be inherent in that prime matter? If you know this, we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the first emanation is a form?
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer unit. The third question asks whether the first emanation (the first created being) could be a form rather than an intellect or something else. The objector argues that if the form causes prime matter to exist, then the form would be independent of matter in its action, and hence independent in essence, which is problematic because forms are traditionally considered dependent on matter. The author refutes this by analyzing whether the form constitutes (yuqawwim) prime matter or not. If it does not, then form is no different from an accident, which is unacceptable. If it does constitute matter, then its influence cannot be with matter's participation (shirka), because that would require matter to precede itself — an absurdity. Thus, the form's effect is without matter's participation, and this does not negate it being a material form. Then the author considers another objection from the mutakallimun: if form is inherent in matter, it needs matter, but if it also causes matter, there is a circular dependency. The author offers two resolutions: either (1) the form causes matter's existence, but then matter makes the form inherent in it, breaking the cycle; or (2) the form causes both matter and its own inherence in matter. He concludes that it is permissible to say the first emanation is a form. The passage ends mid-sentence at the page break.

هيولى
علة
تقويم
دور
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The form cannot be the first emanation because it would cause a circular dependency between form and matter.
Correction: The author shows that circularity is avoided if the form causes matter's existence and then matter causes the form's inherence, or if the form causes both, breaking the cycle.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the circularity objection regarding form causing matter?
A) He denies that form causes matter at all.
B) He asserts that form causes matter and then matter causes the form to be inherent.
C) He claims that form is eternal and uncreated.
D) He relies on divine intervention to break the cycle.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that the first emanation can be a form because it can cause matter without circularity, either by matter conferring inherence or the form causing both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just analyzed whether the first emanation can be a form without needing matter beforehand. But in your own life, how often do you assume that you must have material means or preconditions before you can act virtuously? Where in your daily routine are you waiting for circumstances to be 'perfect' before obeying Allah? Identify one specific action you have been postponing due to lack of a 'material cause' and commit to doing it by next Friday, relying solely on Allah's help.
?
The objection of circularity seemed to trap the form in an impossible loop, but the author broke it by showing that the form can cause matter and then matter makes the form inherent. Think of a sinful habit or spiritual weakness that feels like a circular trap: you know it's wrong, but you feel you cannot escape without first changing something outside yourself. What is that 'circular dependency' in your heart? What is the first internal step you can take today—without waiting for external change—to break that cycle?
?
The author argues that the form's independence in causing matter does not negate its being a material form. You might think that relying on Allah makes you weak or passive. But the form is independent in its action while still being inherently tied to matter. In contrast, you might be independent in worldly matters but neglect your dependence on Allah. Is there an area of your life where you act as if you are fully independent of Allah? How can you demonstrate your need for Him in that area this week?

Resolving the Circularity Between Form and Prime Matter
Objection and response on form-matter causality; circularity resolved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the possibility that form causes prime matter without circularity, and how this applies to the first emanation.

Core Idea
Form can cause matter and still inhere in it without circularity.

This passage tackles a logical puzzle: if form inheres in matter, how can it also cause matter's existence? The objector sees a vicious circle. The author responds by examining the type of causality involved and offers two solutions that break the circle. This discussion is crucial for understanding the relationship between form and matter in Islamic philosophy and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the author's initial refutation
Read the first part where the objector claims that if form causes matter, form would be independent of matter. Then read the author's quick refutation clarifying that form's influence is not with matter's participation.
Notice the logical move: if form had no role, it would collapse into accident.
2
Examine the second objection from the theologians
Read the objection based on inherence: since form inheres in matter, if it caused matter, circularity would result. Understand why this seems problematic.
This is the core challenge.
3
Understand the two answers the author gives
The author proposes two ways to avoid circularity: (1) form causes matter's existence but then makes itself inhere without affecting its own essence, or (2) form causes matter and then necessitates its own inherence. Both prevent a logical loop.
These solutions preserve form's causal role and its dependence on matter for inherence.
4
Apply to the first emanation
Read the concluding sentence where the author suggests that the first emanation could be a corporeal form, using this logic. Reflect on how this argument supports that view.
This connects the abstract discussion to cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: إن على هذا التقدير تكون الصورة علة لوجود الهيولى، وحينئذ لا يكون تأثيرها في وجود الهيولى بشركة من الهيولى، فتكون الصورة في فعلها غنية عن الهيولى، وكل ما كان غنياً في فعله عن الهيولى كان غنياً في ذاته عن الهيولى. فنقول: هذا الكلام باطل. وذلك لأن هذه الصورة هل لها تأثير في تقويم الهيولى أم لا؟ فإن لم يكن لها تأثير في تقويم الهيولى فحينئذ لا يبقى فرق بين العرض وبين الصورة، فلا تكون الصورة قسماً مغايراً للعرض. وإن كان لها تأثير في تقويم الهيولى فتأثيرها في تقويم الهيولى إن كان بشركة من الهيولى لزم تقدم الهيولى على نفسها، لأن شرط التأثير أن يتقدم بالرتبة على الأثر. فيثبت أن تأثير الصورة في تقويم هيولاها ليس بشركة من تلك الهيولى. ثم إن هذا لا يقدح في كون تلك الصورة صورة هيولانية. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر ههنا كذلك؟ وأيضاً: إن المتكلمين لما قالوا: الصورة لما كانت حالة في الهيولى كانت مفتقرة إلى الهيولى افتقار الحال إلى المحل، فلو كانت علة التقويم الهيولى لزم افتقار الهيولى إليها فيلزم الدور وهو محال، فهذا يدل على أن القول بالصورة محال. ثم إنا تكلفنا جواباً عن هذا الكلام فقلنا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن الصورة توجب وجود الهيولى، ثم إنها لا تؤثر في ذات الصورة بل تؤثر في جعل تلك الصورة حالة فيها، وعلى هذا التقدير فالدور ساقط. أو يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: الصورة توجب الهيولى، ثم إنها توجب لنفسها كونها حالة في تلك الهيولى. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: الصادر الأول صورة جسمانية، ويكون الحال واقعاً على هذا الوجه؟
He said: On this supposition, the form (ṣūra) is the cause of the existence of prime matter (hayūlā), and then its influence does not occur in the existence of prime matter with the participation of prime matter, so the form is independent of prime matter in its act, and whatever is independent in its act of prime matter is independent in its essence of prime matter. We say: This statement is false. That is because this form: does it have an influence in constituting (taqwīm) prime matter or not? If it has no influence in constituting prime matter, then no difference remains between accident (ʿaraḍ) and form, so form would not be a category distinct from accident. And if it has an influence in constituting prime matter, then its influence in constituting prime matter, if it is with the participation of prime matter, would necessitate the priority of prime matter over itself, because the condition of influence is that it precedes the effect in rank. Thus it is established that the influence of form in constituting its matter is not with the participation of that matter. Then this does not impair that form being a material form. So why is it not permissible that the matter be such here? Also, when the theologians (mutakallimūn) said: Since the form inheres in prime matter, it is in need of prime matter as the state's need of its locus, so if it were the cause of the constitution of prime matter, then prime matter would be in need of it, leading to circularity (dawr), which is impossible—this indicates that the assertion of form is impossible. Then we undertook to answer this statement and said: Why is it not permissible to say that the form necessitates the existence of prime matter, and then it does not affect the essence of the form, but rather affects making that form a state in it, and on this supposition circularity is avoided? Or it may be said: Why is it not permissible to say that the form necessitates prime matter, and then it necessitates for itself its being a state in that matter? Once you understand this, we say: Why is it not permissible to say that the first emanation (al-ṣādir al-awwal) is a corporeal form, and that the state occurs in this manner?
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response discussion on whether form is the cause of prime matter. The objector claims that if form causes matter, then form would be independent of matter, but the author refutes this by examining whether form has a role in constituting matter. If it does not, then form collapses into accident; if it does, its influence cannot be with the participation of matter else matter would precede itself. Therefore, the influence is without participation, and this does not contradict form being material. Another objection is raised from theologians: form inheres in matter, so if it caused matter, circularity would result. The author answers by offering two ways: either form causes matter's existence without affecting its own essence, or form causes matter and then causes itself to inhere. Both avoid circularity. The conclusion is that the first emanation could be a corporeal form.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author shows that form's causality in giving existence to matter does not entail independence; form can cause matter without circularity by distinguishing between causing existence and causing inherence.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the circularity (dawr) that arises if form is the cause of prime matter?
A) By denying that form has any causal role in matter.
B) By asserting that form causes matter without needing matter, so no circle.
C) By allowing that form causes matter's existence but then causes itself to inhere in that matter, or that it causes matter and then inheres without affecting its own essence.
D) By claiming that matter causes form instead.

Key Takeaway
Form can be the cause of prime matter's existence without circularity by separating the act of giving existence from the act of inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How will you embrace your total need of Him today without diminishing your role as a responsible agent?
?
The author resolves circularity by distinguishing between causing existence and causing inherence. Where in your life have you confused similar distinctions—for example, confusing what gives you your value with who sustains it? What would it look like to separate those honestly, and what might you have to give up?
?
If the first emanation is a corporeal form, then matter is not a corruption but a meaningful part of creation. How does this challenge any hidden contempt you may hold for your own body, your physical needs, or the material world? Will you commit to seeing the physical as a gift from the Creator, not a prison?

Why the First Emanation Cannot Be a Soul: Objection and Response
Objection that the first emanation cannot be a soul, and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument against the objection that the first emanation cannot be a soul due to the soul's need for a physical instrument.

Core Idea
The soul's direct action on its instrument is rational, so the first emanation could be a soul.

The fourth question challenges the claim that the first emanation is incorporeal. An objector argues that a soul acts only through a physical instrument. In this passage, the author dismantles this objection by examining how the soul engages with its instrument, revealing two possibilities and their consequences. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the full context.
Pay attention to the logical structure: question, objection, response.
2
Identify the objection
Locate the objector's claim that the first emanation cannot be a soul because a soul affects only through a physical instrument.
3
Analyze the two scenarios
The author presents two cases: if the soul uses another instrument (leads to regress) or if it acts directly (no problem). Understand each.
These are critical for the rebuttal.
4
Understand why the objection fails
Neither scenario makes the first emanation impossible; thus the objection is invalid.
The author concludes that the soul could be the first emanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: لم لا يجوز أن يكون الصادر الأول نفساً؟ قوله: و لأن النفس هو الذي لا يؤثر في الأثر إلا بآلة جسمانية. قلنا: هذا الكلام خطا. لأن تأثيرها في تلك الآلة الجسمانية إن كان بآلة أخرى جسمانية لزم التسلسل، أو يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه وهو محال. وإن كان تأثيرها في تلك الآلة لا بواسطة آلة أخرى مع أن هذا لا يقدح في كونها نفساً. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في سائر الصور كذلك؟
The fourth question: Why is it not permissible that the first emanation be a soul? He said: Because the soul (nafs) is that which does not produce an effect except by means of a physical instrument (āla jismāniyya). We said: This statement is incorrect. Because its influence upon that physical instrument, if it is by means of another physical instrument, would necessitate infinite regress (tasalsul), or it would necessitate the priority of a thing over itself, which is impossible. And if its influence upon that instrument is without the mediation of another instrument, then this does not impair its being a soul. So why is it not permissible that the case be similar in all other forms?
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth question and its answer. The objector claims that the first emanation cannot be a soul because a soul only acts through a physical instrument. The author responds that this statement is wrong. He argues by analyzing how the soul acts upon its instrument: if it uses another physical instrument, that leads to either infinite regress or a thing preceding itself, both impossible. If the soul acts upon the instrument directly without another instrument, then this does not negate its being a soul. Thus, the objection fails, and the first emanation could be a soul just as other forms (like the soul) are possible.

Key Terms
نفس
آلة جسمانية
تسلسل
تقدم الشيء على نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul's need for a physical instrument means it cannot be the first emanation because it would require a physical intermediary.
Correction: The soul's need for an instrument does not disqualify it, because the soul can act directly on the instrument without requiring another instrument, which is logically coherent and does not contradict its nature.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the objection that the soul needs a physical instrument fail to disprove that the first emanation could be a soul?
A) Because the soul does not actually need an instrument.
B) Because the soul can act directly on the instrument without needing another instrument, which does not contradict its being a soul.
C) Because the soul uses a spiritual instrument, not a physical one.
D) Because the first emanation is beyond such categories.

Key Takeaway
The soul's need for an instrument does not disqualify it from being the first emanation, because direct action on the instrument is conceivable and does not contradict the definition of a soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that the soul can act directly without requiring a physical intermediary. Is there a spiritual practice you have been postponing because you think you need a specific 'instrument' (place, time, teacher) to do it? What is one step you can take today to act directly with your soul?
?
The objector assumed the soul needed an instrument, but the response proved otherwise. In your own journey of faith, what assumptions have you accepted without questioning that might be limiting your understanding of God's power or your relationship with Him? Can you identify one such assumption and challenge it today?
?
The author asks why the same logic cannot apply in other cases. Are you applying inconsistent reasoning in your life—allowing something for yourself but rejecting it for others, or accepting a claim in one area but denying it in another? What is one area where you need to be more consistent in your application of truth?

Why the First Emanation Cannot Be a Soul: Objection and Refutation
Objection and response: Why the first emanation cannot be a soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument against the first emanation being a soul and the refutation based on the problem of infinite regress and self-priority.

Core Idea
The soul's use of a corporeal instrument does not disprove it being the first emanation.

In this passage, the author engages with an objection that the first emanation cannot be a soul because a soul requires a corporeal instrument to act. The response cleverly shows that this line of reasoning either leads to an infinite regress or is self-defeating, thereby preserving the possibility that the first emanation could be a soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a question, an opponent's claim, then a rebuttal starting with 'قلنا'.
The passage is a typical kalam objection-response format.
2
Understand the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that a soul cannot be the first emanation because it only acts through a corporeal instrument. Identify the claim in the text.
3
Analyze the two possibilities
The author considers two ways the soul might influence its instrument: through another instrument (leading to infinite regress) or directly (which does not negate its being a soul).
This is the core logical move.
4
Recognize the logical consequences
The first possibility leads to either an infinite regress (tasalsul) or self-priority (tqaddum al-shay' 'ala nafsihi), both impossible. The second possibility does not refute the soul's nature.
5
Conclude the argument
Since the opponent's reasoning fails in both cases, the objection is invalid. The same logic applies to other similar cases.
Reflect on how this argument strengthens the possibility of the first emanation being a soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: لم لا يجوز أن يكون الصادر الأول نفساً؟ قوله: ولأن النفس هو الذي لا يؤثر في الأثر إلا بآلة جسمانية، قلنا: هذا الكلام خطأ. لأن تأثيرها في تلك الآلة الجسمانية. إن كان بآلة أخرى جسمانية لزم التسلسل، أو يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه وهو محال. وإن كان تأثيرها في تلك الآلة لا بواسطة آلة أخرى، مع أن هذا لا يقدح في كونها نفساً. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في سائر الصور كذلك؟
The fourth question: Why is it not permissible that the first emanation be a soul? His statement: Because the soul is that which does not affect the effect except through a corporeal instrument. We said: This speech is mistaken. Because its influence on that corporeal instrument, if it were through another corporeal instrument, then an infinite regress would follow, or the priority of a thing over itself would follow, and that is impossible. And if its influence on that instrument is without mediation of another instrument, then this does not impair its being a soul. So why is it not permissible that the case be likewise in other instances?
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a kalam objection and response. The opponent argues that the first emanation cannot be a soul (nafs) because a soul only acts through a corporeal instrument (āla jismāniyya). The responder (author) rebuts this as mistaken. The argument proceeds: If the soul's influence on that corporeal instrument itself requires another corporeal instrument, an infinite regress (tasalsul) would occur, or the thing would be prior to itself – both impossible. If, instead, the soul influences the first instrument without a mediating instrument, then the fact that the soul uses an instrument does not negate its being a soul. The responder concludes that the same reasoning applies to all similar cases, so the objection fails.

Key Terms
الصادر الأول
النفس
آلة جسمانية
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection stands because the soul's use of an instrument proves it cannot be the first emanation.
Correction: The responder shows that the use of an instrument does not necessarily imply an infinite regress or negate the soul's nature, so the objection is not valid.

Checkpoint
How does the responder refute the claim that a soul cannot be the first emanation because it requires a corporeal instrument?
A) By arguing that the soul does not need any instrument at all.
B) By showing that the soul's use of an instrument does not entail an infinite regress or self-priority.
C) By stating that the first emanation is not a soul but an intellect.
D) By claiming that corporeal instruments are not necessary for the soul.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the soul requires a corporeal instrument does not preclude it from being the first emanation, as the instrument itself does not necessitate an infinite regress or self-priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an objection to a belief you hold, do you examine its logical structure as thoroughly as this passage does, or do you dismiss it emotionally? Commit to analyzing one current doubt with the same method: identify the claim, and test the reasoning with the two-option refutation.
?
This passage shows that the soul can act directly without an instrument. In your spiritual life, do you often feel dependent on external tools (e.g., specific rituals, places, or people) to connect with God, or do you actively cultivate direct, unmediated sincerity? Today, try to perform a single inward act of devotion—like a thought of gratitude or repentance—without any outward action, and notice whether you feel it is equally real.

The Philosophers' View on Multiplicity from the First Emanation
Explains and critiques the philosophers' emanation theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument for how multiplicity arises from the First Principle and why a direct emanation of many things from the One is impossible, as well as the proposed solution involving the possible essence.

Core Idea
Multiplicity enters the universe through the first effect's own possibility, not from the One directly.

This passage tackles a classic challenge in philosophical theology: if God is absolutely simple and one, how can a world of multiplicity emanate from Him? The philosophers' response is presented in its strongest form, then scrutinized for logical consistency. You will see how the argument leads to the conclusion that the first effect must combine its own possibility with existence from its cause, thereby generating multiplicity without compromising divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the English translation. Notice the flow of the argument from problem to solution.
The passage is cut off at the end; focus on the logical structure presented.
2
Follow the logical dilemma
Identify the two scenarios: either the first emanation produces only one thing (leading to infinite causal chains) or it produces many things. The author argues the first is false, and the second leads to a dilemma about multiplicity being necessary or possible.
Pay attention to the refutation using the two grains of wheat.
3
Understand the solution based on possible essence
The solution is that the first effect has possibility from itself and existence from its cause; combining these yields multiplicity. This avoids the One directly producing many.
This distinction is key to preserving divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح مذهب هؤلاء الفلاسفة في أن فلك المصادر الأول. كيف يصدر عنه الكثرة الحاصلة في الممكنات؟
اعلم أنا نعبر عن كلام القوم بالوجه الأحسن، فنقول : الصادر الأول . إما أن يقال : إنه لا يصدر عنه أيضاً ، إلا الواحد، فيكون الكلام في الثاني ، كما في الأول . فيلزم أن لا يوجد موجودان في الممكنات ، إلا ويكون أحدهما علة للثاني ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . فإن هذه الحبة من الحنطة ، ليست علة لتلك الحبة الأخرى ، ولا معلولة لها ، فإن وجب الاعتراف بأنه لا بد وأن يصدر عن المعلول الأول أكثر من الواحد ، وكل ما صدر عنه أكثر من الواحد ، ففيه كثرة . ينتج : أنه حصل في ذات المعلول الأول : كثرة . فتلك الكثرة إن كانت واجبة لذاته، كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد - وإن كانت ممكنة فهي صادرة عن واجب الوجود، فالواحد قد صدر عنه أكثر من الواحد ، وهو محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : الماهية الممكنة . لها من ذاتها : الإمكان ولها من علتها : الوجود . فإذا ضمّ ماله من ذاته إلى ماله من غيره ، حصلت كثرة يمكن جعلها مبدأ للمعلولات الكثيرة . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المعلول الأول حصل له الإمكان بذاته ، والوجود من غيره . والشيء ما
Explanation of the doctrine of these philosophers concerning that [؟] the first sources. How does the multiplicity obtained in possible things emanate from it? Know that we express the speech of the people in the best manner, and we say: The first emanation. Either it is said that nothing else emanates from it except the one, so the discussion about the second is like that about the first. Then it would follow that no two existents exist among the possible things unless one of them is a cause of the other, and it is known that this is false. For this grain of wheat is not a cause of that other grain, nor is it an effect of it. So it is necessary to acknowledge that it is unavoidable that from the first effect emanates more than one, and everything from which more than one emanates has multiplicity in it. It follows that multiplicity occurred in the essence of the first effect. Then that multiplicity: if it were necessary in itself, then the Necessary Existent would be more than one; and if it were possible, then it emanates from the Necessary Existent, so the One would have emanated more than one, which is impossible. So nothing remains except to say: The possible essence has from itself possibility and from its cause existence. So when what it has from itself is combined with what it has from another, a multiplicity results which can be made a principle for many effects. If you understand this, we say: The first effect obtained possibility by itself and existence from another. And the thing [what...]
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a critique and refinement of the philosophers' doctrine regarding how multiplicity arises from the First Principle. It begins by stating the topic: explaining the philosophers' view on how the multiplicity found in possible things emanates from the first emanation. The author then presents the argument in the best possible form. The first scenario considered is that the first emanation itself only produces one effect, which would require an infinite chain of causes and effects, implying that any two existents are related as cause and effect. This is refuted by a counterexample: two grains of wheat are neither causes nor effects of each other. Therefore, it is necessary that the first effect emanates more than one. But if the first effect contains multiplicity, that multiplicity is either necessary or possible. If necessary, then there would be more than one necessary being. If possible, it must come from the Necessary Existent, meaning the One directly produced multiplicity, which is impossible. Thus, the only remaining solution is to consider the possible essence: it has possibility from itself and existence from its cause. When these two aspects—its own possibility and the existence it receives from its cause—are combined, a multiplicity arises that can serve as the source for many effects. The passage concludes by reiterating that the first effect has possibility in itself and existence from another, setting up the foundation for subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
الصادر الأول
المعلول الأول
الكثرة
الماهية الممكنة
الإمكان
الوجود
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the One directly produces multiplicity, it compromises divine simplicity.
Correction: The solution uses the distinction between essence and existence within the first effect: the first effect's own possibility (from itself) and the existence it receives (from its cause) combine to produce multiplicity without the One directly causing many.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason that multiplicity arises in the first effect?
A) Because the first effect is composed of matter and form.
B) Because the first effect has possibility from itself and existence from its cause.
C) Because the first effect is an intellect that contains many forms.
D) Because the first effect is caused by many causes.

Key Takeaway
The first effect's combination of inherent possibility and granted existence is the key to understanding multiplicity while preserving divine unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that your very existence is a combination of nothingness (from yourself) and existence (from God). Do you truly feel that you are nothing without Him, or do you live as if you have an independent self? Pause right now and say in your heart: 'I have nothing from myself, all is from You.' Will you repeat this daily?
?
When you look at the 'multiplicity' in your life—your successes, failures, relationships, trials—do you trace them back to secondary causes (like your effort, luck, or people) or do you see them as directly from Allah, without any real causal power in creation? What would change in your reliance on Allah if you really internalized that only He gives existence?
?
If the first effect itself is contingent and receives existence from the One, then everything else receives existence through that first effect. How does this hierarchy of dependence make you reflect on your own position? Are you content to be a passive receiver, or do you seek to claim a false independence? Will you acknowledge your absolute need for Allah every moment today?

Causality and Created Things

The Philosophers' Problem of Multiplicity and Its Solution
Author explains the solution to the multiplicity problem using three aspects of the first effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers explained the emergence of multiplicity from a single first cause without compromising divine unity.

Core Idea
Multiplicity arises not directly from the One but from the aspects of the first effect.

The philosophers faced a logical puzzle: if only one proceeds from God (the First Cause), how can there be multiple creatures? The solution lies in analyzing the first effect's own aspects. This passage presents the core argument and the three-aspect solution that became foundational in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the initial problem
Read the Arabic text from 'اعلم أنا نعبر' to 'وهو محال' and its translation. Notice how the author sets up the dilemma: if only one proceeds from each cause, then all existents would be causally linked, which is false.
Pay attention to the grain of wheat example – it illustrates a non-causal relationship between two similar things.
2
Identify the proposed solution
Read the next part starting from 'فلم يبق إلا أن يقال' to 'عللا لموجودات ثلاثة'. The solution involves recognizing three aspects in the first effect: possibility, existence, and necessity-by-another.
These three aspects become the principles for matter, form, and the second intellect.
3
Connect the three aspects to the cosmos
Notice how the author assigns each aspect as a cause for a different component of the outermost sphere: possibility -> matter, existence -> form, necessity-by-another -> the moving intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح مذهب هؤلاء الفلاسفة في أن فلك المصادر الأول. كيف تصدر عنه الكثرة المحاصلة في الممكنات؟ اعلم أنا نعبر عن كلام القوم بالوجه الأحسن، فنقول : الصادر الأول . إما أن يقال : إنه لا يصدر عنه أيضاً ، إلا الواحد، فيكون الكلام في الثاني ، كما في الأول . فيلزم أن لا يوجد موجودان في الممكنات ، إلا ويكون أحدهما علة للثاني ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . فإن هذه الحبة من الحنطة ، ليست علة لتلك الحبة الأخرى ، ولا معلولة لها ، فإن وجب الاعتراف بأنه لا بد وأن يصدر عن المعلول الأول أكثر من الواحد ، وكل ما صدر عنه أكثر من الواحد ، ففيه كثرة . ينتج : أنه حصل في ذات المعلول الأول : كثرة . فتلك الكثرة إن كانت واجبة لذاته، كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد - وإن كانت ممكنة فهي صادرة عن واجب الوجود، فالواحد قد صدر عنه أكثر من الواحد ، وهو محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : الماهية الممكنة . لها من ذاتها : الإمكان ولها من علتها : الوجود . فإذا ضمّ ماله من ذاته إلى ماله من غيره ، حصلت كثرة يمكن جعلها مبدأ للمعلولات الكثيرة . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المعلول الأول حصل له الإمكان بذاته ، والوجود من غيره . والشيء ما لم يجب لم يوجد ، فقد حصل ههنا أمور ثلاثة : الإمكان والوجود والوجوب بالغير . فوجب جعل هذه الاعتبارات الثلاثة عللا لموجودات ثلاثة ، ويجب جعل الأشرف علة للأشرف ، والأخس علة للأخس ، فالإمكان علة لهيولى الفلك الأقصى والوجود علة لصورته، والوجوب بالغير، علة للفعل الثاني الذي هو العقل المحرك للفلك الأقصى وبهذا الطريق، فإنه صدر عن كل عقل : عقل وفلك . حتى ينتهي إلى الفلك الأخير الذي هو العقل الفعال . واعلم أن الشيخ الرئيس قد أدرج في أثناء هذا الكلام نوعاً آخر من التقرير . فقال : إن العقل الأول يعقل ماهية نفسه، ويعقل وجود نفسه ، ويعقل وجوبه بالأول . ويعقل الأول فجعل عقله لماهية نفسه : علة لهيولى الفلك الأقصى، وجعل عقله لوجود نفسه علة لصورة الفلك الأقصى. وجعل عقله لوجوبه بالأول علة لنفس الفلك الأقصى . وجعل عقله بالأول ، علة لعقل الفلك الأقصى . لما ثبت : أنه يجب جعل الأشرف علة للأشرف فهذا تفصيل أقوالهم في هذا الباب
Explanation of the doctrine of these philosophers concerning the sphere of the first sources. How does multiplicity that occurs in possibles proceed from it? Know that we express the words of the people in the best manner, and we say: The first effect. It may be said that only one proceeds from it as well, so the discussion about the second is like that about the first. Then it would follow that no two existents exist among the possibles unless one of them is a cause of the other, and it is known that this is false. For this grain of wheat is not a cause of that other grain, nor an effect of it. So if it is necessary to acknowledge that more than one must proceed from the first effect, and everything from which more than one proceeds has multiplicity, then it follows that multiplicity occurred in the essence of the first effect. If that multiplicity is necessary for its essence, then the Necessary Existent would be more than one; and if it is possible, then it proceeds from the Necessary Existent, so the One has produced more than one, which is impossible. So nothing remains except to say: The possible quiddity has possibility from itself and existence from its cause. So when what it has from itself is joined to what it has from others, a multiplicity occurs that can be made a principle for many effects. If you understand this, we say: The first effect acquired possibility from itself and existence from another. And a thing is not existent unless it is necessary [by another]. So three things have occurred here: possibility, existence, and necessity-by-another. So it is necessary to make these three considerations causes for three existents, and it is necessary to make the nobler a cause for the nobler, and the baser a cause for the baser. So possibility is a cause for the matter of the outermost sphere, and existence is a cause for its form, and necessity-by-another is a cause for the second act which is the intellect moving the outermost sphere. And in this way, from every intellect proceeds an intellect and a sphere, until it ends at the last sphere which is the active intellect. And know that the Chief Master (Ibn Sina) inserted another type of exposition in this discussion. He said: The first intellect intellects the quiddity of itself, and intellects the existence of itself, and intellects its necessity by the First, and intellects the First. So he made its intellection of its own quiddity a cause for the matter of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of its own existence a cause for the form of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of its necessity by the First a cause for the soul of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of the First a cause for the intellect of the outermost sphere. Since it is established that the nobler must be made a cause for the nobler. This is the detailed account of their statements in this chapter.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the philosophers' doctrine of emanation, specifically how multiplicity arises from the First Cause. The author begins by explaining the problem: if only one proceeds from the First, then the same applies to the second, leading to an infinite regress where every two existents are cause and effect, which is absurd (e.g., two grains of wheat not causally related). So more than one must proceed from the first effect, but that introduces multiplicity in the first effect. If that multiplicity is necessary, then the Necessary would be multiple; if possible, then the One produced multiplicity directly, which is impossible. The solution: the possible quiddity has possibility from itself and existence from its cause. Combining these yields multiplicity that can serve as basis for many effects. Thus the first effect has three aspects: possibility (self), existence (from cause), and necessity-by-another (from cause). These three aspects become causes for three things: matter, form, and the second act (intellect) of the outermost sphere. Then the author presents Ibn Sina's version: the first intellect's intellections of its quiddity, existence, necessity, and the First produce respectively the matter, form, soul, and intellect of the outermost sphere. The passage concludes that this is their detailed account.

Key Terms
الصادر الأول
الإمكان
الوجود
الوجوب بالغير
الهيولى
الصورة
العقل المحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers denied any multiplicity in the first effect, but they explain it via aspects of its quiddity.
Correction: The philosophers do not deny multiplicity; they attribute it to the possible quiddity's own aspects, not to the First Cause directly.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what prevents the first effect from being a simple one without multiplicity?
A) Its essence is multiple.
B) It must produce more than one, and that requires multiplicity in it.
C) The First Cause created it with multiplicity.
D) The grain of wheat example proves it.

Key Takeaway
The three aspects of the first effect (possibility, existence, necessity-by-another) explain how multiplicity arises without compromising divine unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the multiplicity around you—the countless people, events, and phenomena—do you ever wonder how they all trace back to the One? How does this lesson's explanation of the three aspects of the first effect challenge you to see every created thing as a bundle of dependence (possibility, existence, necessity-by-another) rather than as self-sufficient? Will you now pause before each thing and recall that its very existence is a borrowed gift?
?
The philosophers say the first effect has necessity-by-another from God. In your own life, you experience moments of being 'necessitated' by circumstances—your job, your health, your relationships. Do you live as though you are necessary by yourself or necessary by Another? What would change in your daily prayer and reliance if you truly internalised that every 'must' in your life is only because He makes it so?
?
This passage dissects the first effect into three aspects. But you too are a possible being: you have possibility in yourself, existence from God, and a borrowed necessity. When was the last time you felt the weight of your own contingency—that at any moment you could cease to be if He withdrew His sustaining existence? Does that awareness grip you with awe, or have you grown comfortable with your borrowed being?

Ibn Sina's Four Intellection Scheme
Ibn Sina's detailed account of the first intellect's intellections generating the outermost sphere's components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's version of emanation, where the first intellect's four intellections generate the matter, form, soul, and intellect of the outermost sphere.

Core Idea
The first intellect's self-awareness and knowledge of the First produce the components of the cosmos.

Ibn Sina refined the emanation scheme by focusing on the intellect's own cognitive acts. Instead of abstract aspects, he used the first intellect's intellections of its quiddity, existence, necessity, and the First as causes for the four elements of the outermost sphere. This passage presents his elegant formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's exposition
Read the Arabic from 'واعلم أن الشيخ الرئيس' to the end and its translation. Notice how he lists four intellections of the first intellect.
Each intellection corresponds to a different component: quiddity, existence, necessity, and the First.
2
Match intellections to cosmic components
Identify which intellection causes which: quiddity -> matter, existence -> form, necessity -> soul, knowledge of First -> intellect.
The principle 'nobler causes nobler' is applied here.
3
Reflect on the overall chain
Understand that this pattern repeats for each subsequent intellect until the Active Intellect.
This is the standard emanation scheme in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح مذهب هؤلاء الفلاسفة في أن فلك المصادر الأول. كيف تصدر عنه الكثرة المحاصلة في الممكنات؟ اعلم أنا نعبر عن كلام القوم بالوجه الأحسن، فنقول : الصادر الأول . إما أن يقال : إنه لا يصدر عنه أيضاً ، إلا الواحد، فيكون الكلام في الثاني ، كما في الأول . فيلزم أن لا يوجد موجودان في الممكنات ، إلا ويكون أحدهما علة للثاني ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . فإن هذه الحبة من الحنطة ، ليست علة لتلك الحبة الأخرى ، ولا معلولة لها ، فإن وجب الاعتراف بأنه لا بد وأن يصدر عن المعلول الأول أكثر من الواحد ، وكل ما صدر عنه أكثر من الواحد ، ففيه كثرة . ينتج : أنه حصل في ذات المعلول الأول : كثرة . فتلك الكثرة إن كانت واجبة لذاته، كان واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد - وإن كانت ممكنة فهي صادرة عن واجب الوجود، فالواحد قد صدر عنه أكثر من الواحد ، وهو محال . فلم يبق إلا أن يقال : الماهية الممكنة . لها من ذاتها : الإمكان ولها من علتها : الوجود . فإذا ضمّ ماله من ذاته إلى ماله من غيره ، حصلت كثرة يمكن جعلها مبدأ للمعلولات الكثيرة . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : المعلول الأول حصل له الإمكان بذاته ، والوجود من غيره . والشيء ما لم يجب لم يوجد ، فقد حصل ههنا أمور ثلاثة : الإمكان والوجود والوجوب بالغير . فوجب جعل هذه الاعتبارات الثلاثة عللا لموجودات ثلاثة ، ويجب جعل الأشرف علة للأشرف ، والأخس علة للأخس ، فالإمكان علة لهيولى الفلك الأقصى والوجود علة لصورته، والوجوب بالغير، علة للفعل الثاني الذي هو العقل المحرك للفلك الأقصى وبهذا الطريق، فإنه صدر عن كل عقل : عقل وفلك . حتى ينتهي إلى الفلك الأخير الذي هو العقل الفعال . واعلم أن الشيخ الرئيس قد أدرج في أثناء هذا الكلام نوعاً آخر من التقرير . فقال : إن العقل الأول يعقل ماهية نفسه، ويعقل وجود نفسه ، ويعقل وجوبه بالأول . ويعقل الأول فجعل عقله لماهية نفسه : علة لهيولى الفلك الأقصى، وجعل عقله لوجود نفسه علة لصورة الفلك الأقصى. وجعل عقله لوجوبه بالأول علة لنفس الفلك الأقصى . وجعل عقله بالأول ، علة لعقل الفلك الأقصى . لما ثبت : أنه يجب جعل الأشرف علة للأشرف فهذا تفصيل أقوالهم في هذا الباب
Explanation of the doctrine of these philosophers concerning the sphere of the first sources. How does multiplicity that occurs in possibles proceed from it? Know that we express the words of the people in the best manner, and we say: The first effect. It may be said that only one proceeds from it as well, so the discussion about the second is like that about the first. Then it would follow that no two existents exist among the possibles unless one of them is a cause of the other, and it is known that this is false. For this grain of wheat is not a cause of that other grain, nor an effect of it. So if it is necessary to acknowledge that more than one must proceed from the first effect, and everything from which more than one proceeds has multiplicity, then it follows that multiplicity occurred in the essence of the first effect. If that multiplicity is necessary for its essence, then the Necessary Existent would be more than one; and if it is possible, then it proceeds from the Necessary Existent, so the One has produced more than one, which is impossible. So nothing remains except to say: The possible quiddity has possibility from itself and existence from its cause. So when what it has from itself is joined to what it has from others, a multiplicity occurs that can be made a principle for many effects. If you understand this, we say: The first effect acquired possibility from itself and existence from another. And a thing is not existent unless it is necessary [by another]. So three things have occurred here: possibility, existence, and necessity-by-another. So it is necessary to make these three considerations causes for three existents, and it is necessary to make the nobler a cause for the nobler, and the baser a cause for the baser. So possibility is a cause for the matter of the outermost sphere, and existence is a cause for its form, and necessity-by-another is a cause for the second act which is the intellect moving the outermost sphere. And in this way, from every intellect proceeds an intellect and a sphere, until it ends at the last sphere which is the active intellect. And know that the Chief Master (Ibn Sina) inserted another type of exposition in this discussion. He said: The first intellect intellects the quiddity of itself, and intellects the existence of itself, and intellects its necessity by the First, and intellects the First. So he made its intellection of its own quiddity a cause for the matter of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of its own existence a cause for the form of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of its necessity by the First a cause for the soul of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of the First a cause for the intellect of the outermost sphere. Since it is established that the nobler must be made a cause for the nobler. This is the detailed account of their statements in this chapter.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the philosophers' doctrine of emanation, specifically how multiplicity arises from the First Cause. The author begins by explaining the problem: if only one proceeds from the First, then the same applies to the second, leading to an infinite regress where every two existents are cause and effect, which is absurd (e.g., two grains of wheat not causally related). So more than one must proceed from the first effect, but that introduces multiplicity in the first effect. If that multiplicity is necessary, then the Necessary would be multiple; if possible, then the One produced multiplicity directly, which is impossible. The solution: the possible quiddity has possibility from itself and existence from its cause. Combining these yields multiplicity that can serve as basis for many effects. Thus the first effect has three aspects: possibility (self), existence (from cause), and necessity-by-another (from cause). These three aspects become causes for three things: matter, form, and the second act (intellect) of the outermost sphere. Then the author presents Ibn Sina's version: the first intellect's intellections of its quiddity, existence, necessity, and the First produce respectively the matter, form, soul, and intellect of the outermost sphere. The passage concludes that this is their detailed account.

الهيولى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the first intellect only has one intellection, but Ibn Sina describes four.
Correction: Ibn Sina explicitly states the first intellect intellects four things: its quiddity, existence, necessity, and the First.

Checkpoint
