B) The form of the outermost sphere
C) The soul of the outermost sphere
D) The intellect of the outermost sphere

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's emanation scheme uses the first intellect's four intellections to generate the matter, form, soul, and intellect of the outermost sphere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first intellect's intellection of its necessity by the First causes the soul of the sphere. Do you truly recognize your own utter dependence on God in every moment, or do you often feel self-sufficient? What would change if you lived with constant awareness of your contingency?
?
Ibn Sina describes the nobler causing the nobler. In your own life, are you striving for the noblest things—knowledge of God, virtue, truth—or are you content with base matters? Examine your daily priorities: do they reflect a pursuit of the higher?
?
The first intellect's intellection of the First produces the intellect of the sphere. In what ways does your knowledge of God shape your intellect and actions? Are you actively seeking to know God more deeply, or is your knowledge superficial? Commit to one specific step to deepen your knowledge of Allah today.

First Objection: The Dilemma of One or Many Effects
First objection against necessary causation using a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the philosophers' doctrine of necessary emanation: the dilemma that if God were a necessitating cause, His effect must be either one or many, both of which are impossible.

Core Idea
If God were a necessitating cause, His effect would be either one or many—both impossible.

This lesson presents the first objection from the Mutakallimun who affirm a free agent. They argue that if God were a necessitating cause (موجب), then His effect must be either one or multiple. Through a logical demonstration, they show both possibilities lead to absurdity, thereby refuting necessary emanation and supporting the doctrine of a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical structure: a dilemma is presented, then one horn is refuted.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; we only get part of the objection.
2
Identify the dilemma
The objection states: if the First were a necessitating cause, its effect (the first created thing) must be either one or more than one. Both are claimed to be false.
The text says 'والقسمان باطلان'—both divisions are false.
3
Understand the argument against one effect
The passage explains why one effect is impossible: if the effect is one, then its effect would also be one, leading to an infinite chain where every two existents are cause and effect. This is absurd, e.g., two grains of dust would be causally related.
This argument is standard in Kalam to prevent infinite regress of causes.
4
Notice the transition to a question
After stating the impossibility of one effect, the text begins a question to the philosophers: 'Why not...' This likely addresses a potential defense from the philosophers.
The passage ends here; the full objection may continue in subsequent pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه من وجوه : الأول : إنا نقلنا عن القائلين بالفاعل المختار ، أنهم قالوا : لو كان موجباً ، لكان : معلوله إما أن يكون واحداً ، وإما أن يكون أكثر من واحد . والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بكونه علة موجبة . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون معلوله شيئاً واحداً لأنه إما أن يكون معلول ذلك المعلول : أيضاً واحداً . فيلزم أن لا يوجد في جميع الممكنات شيئان ، إلا وأحدهما علة للآخر ، ومعلول له . وهو محال . ثم أوردنا عليه سؤال للفلاسفة : وهو أنه : لم لا
And the objection against it [the philosophers' doctrine] is from several aspects: The first: We have reported from the proponents of the free agent that they said: If He were a necessitating cause, then His effect would either be one or more than one. Both divisions are false, so the statement that He is a necessitating cause is false. And we only said that it is impossible for His effect to be one because then the effect of that effect would also be one. So it would follow that there are no two things among all possibles except that one is a cause of the other and an effect of it. And that is impossible. Then we raised against it a question to the philosophers, which is: Why not...
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the philosophers' emanation doctrine, from the perspective of the Mutakallimun who affirm a free agent. The objection is structured as a dilemma: if the First were a necessitating cause (موجب), its effect must be either one or many. Both are impossible. The argument for the impossibility of one effect is that it would lead to an infinite regress where every two existents are cause and effect, which is absurd (counterexample: two grains). Therefore the First cannot be a necessitating cause; He must be a free agent (فاعل مختار) who chooses to create. The text then begins a question to the philosophers: 'Why not...' but is cut off. The author likely continues the objection by anticipating a possible response.

علة موجبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection only applies to the philosophers and is not a standard Kalam argument.
Correction: This is a classic argument used by the Mutakallimun to establish the necessity of a free agent. It is not merely a critique of philosophers but a positive argument for free will in causation.

Checkpoint

D) The philosophers failed to prove the existence of the First Cause

Key Takeaway
The first objection against necessary emanation uses a logical dilemma: a necessitating cause cannot produce a single effect (due to infinite regress) or multiple effects (unstated here), so God must be a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God is absolutely free in His actions, or do you sometimes act as if He is bound by some necessity—like the expectation that He must answer your du'a in a certain way? How can you cultivate a deeper reliance on His free will rather than on fixed patterns?
?
If God were a necessitating cause, every event would be forced and causally linked, leaving no room for genuine choice—either for you or for God. How does knowing that God is a free agent impact the way you see your own moral responsibility and repentance? What would change if God were not free?
?
The argument shows that a necessitating God would produce a world of rigid causal links, making your very existence a mere link in an endless chain. Since God freely chose to create you, how should that affect your gratitude and your sense of purpose today? Will you thank Him for that free choice right now?

The Three Aspects and the Emanation of the Outermost Sphere
Exposition of the three-aspect emanation scheme

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers derive three aspects from the first effect and use them as causes for the three constituents of the outermost sphere.

Core Idea
Possibility, existence, and necessity-by-another are the three conceptual causes of all celestial reality.

The philosophers argue that from the First Principle only one effect can proceed directly. But that effect, being contingent in itself, has three aspects: its possibility, its existence, and its necessity by another. These three must be the causes of the three components of the first celestial sphere: matter, form, and soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Then read the translation.
Notice the compact style; each sentence carries a logical step.
2
Identify the three aspects
The passage lists three things that arise: possibility, existence, and necessity by another. Underline them.
These are not separate beings but conceptual considerations (iʿtibārāt).
3
Map each aspect to its effect
See how each aspect becomes a cause for a distinct reality: possibility → matter, existence → form, necessity by another → intellect.
The rule 'the nobler cause for the nobler effect' governs the assignment.
4
Understand the iterative descent
The scheme repeats: from each intellect proceeds another intellect and a sphere, down to the active intellect.
This explains multiplicity while preserving the principle that only one proceeds directly from the First.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يجب لم يوجد ، فقد حصل ههنا أمور ثلاثة : الإمكان والوجود والوجوب بالغير . فوجب جعل هذه الاعتبارات الثلاثة عللا لموجودات ثلاثة ، ويجب جعل الأشرف علة للأشرف ، والأخس علة للأخس ، فالإمكان علة لهيولى الفلك الأقصى [ والوجود علة لصورته ، والوجوب بالغير، علة للفعل الثاني الذي هو العقل المحرك للفلك الأقصى (١) ] وبهذا الطريق ، فإنه صدر عن كل عقل : عقل وفلك . حتى ينتهي إلى الفلك الأخير الذي هو العقل الفعال . واعلم أن الشيخ الرئيس قد أدرج في أثناء هذا الكلام نوعاً آخر من التقرير . فقال : إن العقل الأول يعقل ماهية نفسه، ويعقل وجود نفسه ، ويعقل وجوبه (۲) بالأول . ويعقل الأول فجعل (۳) عقله لماهية نفسه : علة لهيولى الفلك الأقصى، وجعل عقله لوجود نفسه علة لصورة الفلك الأقصى. وجعل عقله لوجوبه بالأول علة لنفس الفلك الأقصى . وجعل عقله بالأول ، علة لعقل الفلك الأقصى . لما ثبت : أنه يجب جعل الأشرف [ علة (1) ] للأشرف فهذا تفصيل أقوالهم في هذا الباب
When it was not necessary that it exist, three things arise here: possibility, existence, and necessity by another. So these three considerations must be made causes for three existents, and the nobler must be made cause of the nobler, and the baser cause of the baser. So possibility is cause of the matter of the outermost sphere, and existence is cause of its form, and necessity by another is cause of the second act which is the intellect moving the outermost sphere. And by this way, from every intellect proceeds an intellect and a sphere, until it ends with the last sphere which is the active intellect. Know that the Chief Master (Ibn Sina) included in the midst of this discourse another type of exposition. He said: the First Intellect intellects its own quiddity, and intellects its own existence, and intellects its necessity by the First, and intellects the First. He made its intellection of its quiddity a cause for the matter of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of its existence a cause for the form of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of its necessity by the First a cause for the soul of the outermost sphere, and its intellection of the First a cause for the intellect of the outermost sphere, because it is established that the nobler must be made cause of the nobler. This is the detailed account of their statements on this topic.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage defines the Neoplatonic emanation scheme as presented by the philosophers, particularly Avicenna. It is an exposition of how the First Principle produces multiplicity. The author describes three aspects in the first effect: possibility (imkan), existence (wujud), and necessity by another (wujud bil-ghayr). These three must serve as causes for three distinct existents: the matter, form, and soul of the outermost sphere. Then the scheme iterates: from each intellect proceeds another intellect and a sphere until the active intellect. The author then adds Avicenna's refinement: the First Intellect has four acts of intellection—of its own quiddity, existence, necessity by the First, and the First itself—each producing a distinct reality. The rule is that the nobler cause corresponds to the nobler effect. The passage ends by stating that this is the full summary of their view.

Key Terms
إمكان
وجود
وجوب بالغير
هيولى
صورة
عقل فعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the three aspects are separate real parts of the First Intellect.
Correction: They are conceptual considerations (iʿtibārāt), not real divisions.

Checkpoint

A) Existence
B) Possibility
C) Necessity by another
D) Intellection of the First

Key Takeaway
The three conceptual aspects of the first effect are the efficient causes of the three constituents of the outermost sphere, initiating a chain of emanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the first effect's existence is purely dependent on the First, what does that say about your own existence? In what ways do you live as though you are self-sufficient, and how can you actively remind yourself that you are contingent upon God every moment?
?
The principle 'the nobler cause for the nobler effect' is used here to assign causes. When you achieve something good, do you attribute it to the highest cause (God) or to yourself? What practical step can you take today to redirect credit for your successes to the true source?
?
This passage describes a chain of intermediaries emanating from the First. Do you recognize any 'intermediaries' in your spiritual life—things or people you depend on between you and God? Are these helping or hindering your direct reliance on Him?

Ibn Sina's Refinement: The Four Intellection of the First Intellect
Ibn Sina's refined emanation via four intellections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina explains the emanation of multiplicity through the four acts of intellection of the First Intellect.

Core Idea
The First Intellect's four objects of intellection produce the four components of the celestial sphere.

After presenting the standard scheme, the author adds Ibn Sina's more detailed account. The First Intellect (the first effect) intellects four things: its own quiddity, its existence, its necessity by the First, and the First itself. Each of these intellections becomes the cause of a distinct reality: matter, form, soul, and intellect respectively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four intellections
Read the Arabic and translation. List the four things the First Intellect intellects: its quiddity, its existence, its necessity by the First, and the First itself.
These are simultaneous in reality but distinct conceptually.
2
Match each intellection to its effect
For each intellection, note which component of the outermost sphere it causes: quiddity → matter, existence → form, necessity by First → soul, intellection of First → intellect.
The principle 'nobler cause for nobler effect' applies here too.
3
Understand the rationale
Ibn Sina's refinement provides a more detailed explanation of how one simple intellect can produce multiple effects without composition.
The intellections are not separate acts but aspects of a single intellectual act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina explains the plurality of the celestial sphere through the four objects of intellection of the First Intellect, each producing a distinct component.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The First Intellect knows itself only in relation to the First. Do you truly know yourself as utterly dependent on God, not just in theory but in every moment of your life? What would change in your daily routine if you lived this awareness?
?
If the noblest intellection produces the noblest effect, what does your mental focus produce? Are your thoughts directed toward what is most noble, or are they scattered among trivial concerns? Choose one thought pattern to redirect today.
?
This passage shows that understanding leads to creation. What spiritual reality are you creating through your current understanding of God? Are you willing to seek a deeper understanding that transforms your soul?

First Objection Against Emanation and Its Refutation
Presents the first objection against emanation and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the emanation scheme and the philosophers' counter-response, along with the author's dismissal of it.

Core Idea
A necessitating cause can only produce one effect, leading to absurd linear causation.

The author has previously argued against the Ash'ari claim that God is a necessitating cause. Now he presents the first objection from the proponents of the free agent: if God were such a cause, His effect would be either one or many. Both are impossible. He then explains why a single effect is absurd and introduces the philosophers' attempted rebuttal, which he dismisses as weak. This lesson walks through that logical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection from the free agent advocates
Read the passage to grasp the claim that if God were a necessitating cause, His effect must be either one or many. Both are false, so the necessitating cause view fails.
The objection is attributed to those who affirm al-fa'il al-mukhtar.
2
Follow the argument against a single effect
The author explains why a single effect is impossible: it would force all possible things into a linear causal chain, which is absurd.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the philosophers' counter-suggestion
The philosophers ask: why not say the first effect has three aspects (possibility, existence, necessity by another) that serve as causes for three effects?
This tries to salvage multiple effects from a single cause.
4
Note the author's dismissal and cross-reference
The author claims to have refuted this in many ways and urges the reader to review that material to see the weakness of the philosophers' position.
The passage itself does not give the full refutation; it points elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه من وجوه : الأول : إنا نقلنا عن القائلين بالفاعل المختار ، أنهم قالوا : لو كان موجباً ، لكان : معلوله إما أن يكون واحداً ، وإما أن يكون أكثر من واحد . والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بكونه علة موجبة . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون معلوله شيئاً واحداً (*) لأنه إما أن يكون معلول ذلك المعلول : أيضاً واحداً . فيلزم أن لا يوجد في جميع الممكنات شيئان ، إلا وأحدهما علة للآخر ، ومعلول له . وهو محال . ثم أوردنا (۱) عليه سؤ الا للفلاسفة : وهو أنه : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المعلول الأول فيه : إمكان ، ووجود ، ووجوب بالغير . وهذه الثلاثة تكون عللاً لمعلولات ثلاثة ، ثم إنا أجبنا عنه ، وبينا بالوجوه الكثيرة : أنه لا يجوز جعل الإمكان والوجود : عللا لهذه الموجودات . فمن انتهى إلى هذا الفصل ، وجب [ عليه (١) ] أن يتأمل في تلك الوجوه حتى يظهر له ضعف هذه المقالة
The objection to it is from several aspects: The first: We have transmitted from those who affirm the free agent that they said: If He were a necessitating cause, then His effect would be either one or more than one. Both divisions are false, so the statement that He is a necessitating cause is false. We said: It is impossible that His effect be a single thing, because then the effect of that effect would also be one, necessitating that there would not exist in all possibles two things unless one is the cause of the other and an effect of it, which is absurd. Then we raised against them the question of the philosophers: Why is it not permissible to say that the first effect contains possibility, existence, and necessity by another, and these three are causes for three effects? Then we answered it, and explained with many points that it is not permissible to make possibility and existence causes for these existents. So whoever reaches this section must ponder those points so that the weakness of this doctrine becomes clear to him.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the emanation scheme and its answer. The objection is transmitted from the proponents of the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar): if God were a necessitating cause, His effect would be either one or many; both are false. The author explains why one effect is impossible: it would lead to all possibles being linearly ordered as cause and effect, which is absurd. Then the philosophers' counter-question is introduced: why not say the first effect has three aspects that generate three effects? The author claims to have answered this elsewhere with many points, and urges the reader to examine those to see the weakness of the philosophers' position. The passage thus serves as a cross-reference to earlier refutations.

Misconception: One might think the author agrees with the philosophers' counter-suggestion because he presents it neutrally.
Correction: The author explicitly says he has refuted it and calls it weak; he does not endorse it.

Key Takeaway
The first objection against emanation is that a necessitating cause can only produce one effect, leading to absurd linear causation; the philosophers' attempt to derive multiple effects from a single cause via aspects of the first effect is deemed weak by the author.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your own thinking, do you ever try to rationalize away the freedom of God by reducing His actions to necessary consequences? Second Additional Objection: Possibility as a Cause
Argues that possibility cannot be a cause whether it is non-existent or an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility (imkan) cannot be a cause for emanation, whether it is regarded as non-existence or an existing attribute.

Core Idea
Possibility collapses into contradiction if treated as a cause.

In this second objection, the mutakallimun present a dilemma against the philosophers' claim that the First Essence is a cause for the possibility of the spheres. The objection forces the opponent to classify possibility either as non-existence or as an attribute, and then shows that both alternatives lead to absurd consequences when brought into causal relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the Arabic passage once silently. Then read the translation. Note the two clear alternatives introduced by 'faimma... aw'.
The objection is structured as a conditional with two possibilities.
2
Analyze the first horn
If possibility is pure non-existence, it cannot be a cause because non-existence cannot produce existence. Why? Because a cause must have some reality.
Think about the axiom: 'Ex nihilo nihil fit' (nothing comes from nothing).
3
Analyze the second horn
If possibility is an existing attribute, then the quiddity's 'requisition' of its own possibility must be prior to the quiddity's existence. This leads to a thing causing an attribute in itself before it exists – an absurd self-causation.
The key phrase is 'اقتضاء الماهية لإمكان نفسها، سابق على وجودها' – requisition precedes existence.
4
Consider the overall refutation
Both horns end in impossibility, so the claim that possibility is a cause is refuted. This strengthens the kalam critique of emanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : وهو أنا إذا جعلنا تلك الذات علة لذلك الإمكان ، فإما أن يكون ذلك الإمكان عدماً محضاً ، أو صفة موجودة . فإن كان الأول امتنع جعله علة الفلك الأقصى ، لأن المعدوم لا يكون علة للموجود . وإن كان الثاني فنقول : اقتضاء الماهية لإمكان نفسها ، سابق على وجودها ، فيلزم أن يكون الشيء قبل وجود نفسه ، علة لقيام صفة موجودة [ به (۱) ] وذلك محال .
The second question: That if we make that essence a cause for that possibility, then that possibility is either pure non-existence or an existing attribute. If the first, it is impossible to make it a cause of the outermost sphere, because non-existence cannot be a cause of existence. If the second, we say: The requisition of the quiddity for its own possibility is prior to its existence, so it would follow that a thing, before its own existence, is a cause for the subsistence of an existing attribute in it, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents a second additional objection against the emanation scheme. It examines the status of possibility (imkan) when it is said that the essence is the cause of its own possibility. The objection presents a dilemma: either possibility is pure non-existence or an existing attribute. If non-existence, it cannot be a cause of something existing (the sphere). If an existing attribute, then the quiddity's requisition of its own possibility must precede the quiddity's existence, which means that before the thing exists, it is causing an attribute in itself – an absurd self-causation. Thus the philosophers' use of possibility as a cause is refuted in both cases.

Key Terms
إمكان
عدم
صفة موجودة
اقتضاء
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility (imkan) can be a valid intermediate cause in emanation.
Correction: The objection shows that if possibility is non-existence, it cannot cause; if it is an attribute, it leads to self-causation absurdity.

Checkpoint
What is the problem if possibility is an existing attribute?
A) It would be eternal
B) It would require the thing to exist before it exists
C) It would be created by the First
D) It would be multiple

Key Takeaway
Possibility (imkan) cannot function as a cause in the emanation framework because whether it is non-existence or an attribute, it leads to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own possibilities—talents, opportunities, plans—do you treat them as causes that guarantee outcomes? How does this logical dismantling of possibility as a cause expose a flaw in that mindset? Will you stop assuming that something is possible means it will happen?
?
Have you ever tried to build a sense of self-worth on what you might become rather than what you are? The philosophers treated possibility as an attribute of the essence before it exists—do you treat your potential as a reality you can claim before you actually achieve it? What would change if you saw possibility as non-existent until actualised?
?
The absurdity of self-causation appears when possibility is treated as an attribute causing itself. Is there an area in your life where you are trying to cause something in yourself before you even exist in that state—like demanding virtue without practice, or expecting knowledge without study? Can you name one such area and commit to a concrete action to change it today?

Al-Razi's Objection: Is Possibility a Cause?
Al-Razi presents a dilemma on the nature of possibility as a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's dilemma that possibility cannot be a cause because it is either non-existence or an existing attribute, both leading to absurdity.

Core Idea
Possibility cannot be a cause: if non-existent, it causes nothing; if existent, it causes before existence.

Al-Razi raises a penetrating objection against treating the essence's possibility as a cause for the existence of the outermost sphere. He forces us to consider what kind of thing possibility itself is. If it is pure non-existence, it cannot produce an effect. If it is an existing attribute, it would require the very thing it qualifies to exist before it does. This passage sharpens our understanding of the ontological status of possibility in kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the dilemma's structure.
Notice the conditional phrasing 'either...or...' which sets up the two horns.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities for what imkan could be: pure non-existence ('adam mahd) or an existing attribute (sifa mawjuda). Note the consequence drawn for each.
3
Reflect on why both horns are problematic
Ask yourself: Why can't non-existence cause something? Why is it absurd for a thing to cause an attribute in itself before it exists? Connect this to the broader issue of causality.
This objection challenges any attempt to make possibility a real cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : وهو أنا إذا جعلنا تلك الذات علة لذلك الإمكان ، فإما أن يكون ذلك الإمكان عدماً محضاً ، أو صفة موجودة . فإن كان الأول امتنع جعله علة الفلك الأقصى ، لأن المعدوم لا يكون علة للموجود . وإن كان الثاني فنقول : اقتضاء الماهية لإمكان نفسها ، سابق على وجودها ، فيلزم أن يكون الشيء قبل وجود نفسه ، علة لقيام صفة موجودة به وذلك محال .
The second question: If we make that essence a cause of that possibility, then either that possibility is pure non-existence or an existing attribute. If the first, it is impossible to make it a cause of the outermost sphere, because non-existence cannot be a cause of an existent. And if the second, we say: the quiddity's requirement of its own possibility is prior to its existence, so it follows that a thing before its own existence is a cause of the subsistence of an existing attribute in it, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is the second objection (السؤال الثاني). Al-Razi examines the nature of imkan (possibility) when considered as a cause. If the essence is the cause of its own possibility, then possibility itself must be either pure non-existence ('adam mahd) or an existing attribute (sifa mawjuda). If it is non-existence, it cannot serve as a cause for the outermost sphere (al-falak al-aqsa), since non-existence cannot bring about existence. If it is an existing attribute, then the quiddity's requiring its own possibility must occur before the quiddity exists, which would mean that a thing, before it exists, is a cause of an attribute in itself—an absurd priority.

Key Terms
إمكان
عدم
صفة موجودة
فلك أقصى
اقتضاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility (imkan) is a real attribute that can be a cause.
Correction: The objection shows that treating imkan as an existing attribute leads to an absurd priority, so it cannot be a cause.

Checkpoint
What is the problem if possibility (imkan) is considered an existing attribute?
A) It would be a cause of itself
B) It would require the quiddity to exist before causing itself
C) It would be eternal
D) It would not be necessary

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's objection reveals that treating possibility as a cause leads to absurdity whether possibility is non-existent or existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows the absurdity of attributing causal power to something non-existent or to a pre-existing attribute. In your life, do you sometimes act as if fears or hopes about the future—which are non-existent—have power to determine outcomes? How can this logical clarity help you trust in Allah alone as the true Cause?
?
Al-Razi forces us to see that if possibility were a real attribute, it would require a thing to cause itself before existing. Is there any aspect of your faith where you unconsciously assign causal power to Allah's attributes (like His knowledge or power) as if they operate independently of His will? How does this argument redirect your reliance back to Allah's essence?
?
The dilemma leaves no room for possibility to be a cause: it is either non-existent and powerless, or existing and leads to absurdity. Do you ever treat your own potential as a concrete force that guarantees success? Commit today to acknowledging that all actual outcomes come solely from Allah, not from mere possibility.

The Six Components of the Celestial Sphere
Enumeration of six celestial components and their incompatibility with emanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' own description of the celestial sphere's six components contradicts their principle that only one effect can proceed from a single simple cause.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's six components cannot all emanate from the First Intellect's single aspect.

The fourth objection strikes at the heart of the emanationist system: if the First Intellect is truly one in all respects, how can it be the source of a celestial sphere that, by the philosophers' own admission, comprises multiple distinct elements? The objector lists six components and argues that attributing them to the three aspects of the First Intellect would violate the 'one from one' principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's opening
Read the Arabic passage once and identify the six components listed by the objector.
Notice that the objector first recaps the three aspects of the First Intellect.
2
Compare with the three aspects
Understand how the six components are supposed to relate to the three aspects (possibility, existence, necessitation by another).
Each aspect should produce one effect, but here we have six components.
3
Understand the logical implication
The objector argues that attributing six components to three aspects necessitates more than one effect per aspect, contradicting the 'one from one' principle.
This is a reductio ad absurdum against the emanationist framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: وهو أنكم ذكرتم جهات ثلاثة في العقل الأول وهي: الإمكان والوجود والوجوب بالغير، ثم ذكرتم إن الفلك الأقصى ليس عبارة عن مجموع ثلاثة أشياء، بل هو عبارة عن الهيولى، والصورة الجسمية، والصورة النوعية الفلكية، والنفس الحيوانية المدركة للجزئيات المباشرة للحركات الجزئية، والنفس الناطقة فإن المختار عنده في كتاب الإشارات أن لكل فلك نفسين إحداهما الحيوانية المدركة للجزئيات والثانية النفس الناطقة المدركة للكليات، وأيضاً العقل الثاني. فهذه ستة أشياء: الهيولى، والصورة الجسمية، والصورة النوعية، والنفس الحيوانية، والنفس الناطقة، والعقل المجرد. فإسناد هذه الستة إلى الجهات الثلاث يقتضي أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد. بل نقول: جوهر الفلك لا يخلو من المقولات العشر التي هي أعراض، فله مقدار خاص، وله شكل خاص، وله وضع خاص، وله من كل مقولة نوع واحد أو أنواع. فإذا وزعنا هذه الاعتبارات على الجهات الثلاث، لزم أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد. بل نقول: لا شك أن جوهر الفلك قابل للقسمة الوهمية، وسنبين في مسألة الجوهر الفرد بالدلائل القاهرة أن كل ما يقبل القسمة المقدارية الوهمية فإن ذاته مؤلفة ومركبة من الأجزاء. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كانت ذات الفلك الأعلى مركبة من أجزاء كثيرة لا يعلم عددها إلا الله. فكيف يمكن إسنادها إلى الجهة الواحدة؟ وأيضاً: فإن أصحاب علم الأحكام اتفقوا على أن طبائع الروح مختلفة بحسب التأثيرات. واختلاف الآثار والألوان يدل على اختلاف الملزومات. فهذا يقتضي أن تكون طبائع البروج مختلفة في الماهيات. فإسنادها بأسرها إلى الجهة الواحدة يقتضي أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد.
The fourth question: You mentioned three aspects in the First Intellect: possibility, existence, and necessitation by another. Then you mentioned that the outermost sphere is not composed of three things, but rather of matter, corporeal form, specific celestial form, the animal soul which perceives particulars and directly manages particular motions, and the rational soul – for his chosen view in the Book of Indications is that each sphere has two souls: one animal perceiving particulars, and the second the rational soul perceiving universals – and also the Second Intellect. So these are six things: matter, corporeal form, specific form, animal soul, rational soul, and the separate intellect. Attributing these six to the three aspects necessitates that more than one issue from the One. Rather we say: the substance of the sphere is not devoid of the ten categories which are accidents; it has a specific quantity, specific shape, specific position, and for each category it has one type or types. If we distribute these considerations onto the three aspects, it necessarily follows that more than one issues from the One. Rather we say: there is no doubt that the substance of the sphere is subject to imaginary division, and we will show in the problem of the indivisible atom by compelling proofs that everything subject to quantitative imaginary division has a nature composed and compounded of parts. If so, then the essence of the highest sphere is composed of many parts whose number is known only to God. How then can it be attributed to a single aspect? Also, the practitioners of judicial astrology agree that the natures of the spirit differ according to effects. The difference of traces and colors indicates difference of correlates. This entails that the natures of the constellations differ in essences. So attributing all of them to a single aspect requires that more than one issue from the One.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed objection against the emanationist claim that the First Intellect, with its three aspects (possibility, existence, necessitation by another), is the sole source of all multiplicity. The objector points out that the outermost celestial sphere, according to the philosophers themselves, is not a simple entity but comprises six distinct components: matter, corporeal form, specific form, animal soul, rational soul, and the Second Intellect. Attributing these six to the three aspects of the First Intellect would mean that multiple effects issue from a single cause, contradicting the principle 'from the one only one proceeds.' The objector then strengthens the argument by noting that the sphere's substance also possesses all ten Aristotelian categories as accidents, each with multiple species. Furthermore, the sphere is subject to imaginary division, implying it is composite and thus cannot arise from a simple cause. Finally, the objector appeals to astrology, arguing that the diverse natures of the constellations indicate essential differences, again requiring multiple sources. The argument systematically demonstrates internal inconsistencies in the emanationist framework.

النفس الناطقة
العقل الثاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection is merely about numerical multiplicity.
Correction: It is fundamentally about the incompatibility between the philosophers' own admission of complexity in celestial spheres and their principle of emanation.

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) Six
C) Ten

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' own description of six celestial components contradicts their principle that only one effect can proceed from a single aspect of the First Intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers thought they had a neat system, but the opponent exposed a contradiction from their own premises. Is there a deeply held belief you have that, if honestly examined, would collapse under its own internal contradictions? Will you set aside time this week to test it?
?
The six components of the celestial sphere represent a more complex reality than the simple emanation model allowed. In your spiritual life, do you oversimplify your own condition (e.g., 'I am a good Muslim') while ignoring the many contradictory components (e.g., negligence, arrogance)? Will you ask someone you trust to point out where your self-description is inconsistent?
?
The scholars in this debate were willing to follow the argument where it led, even if it meant dismantling their own system. When you encounter an argument that threatens your comfort zone, do you engage it honestly or retreat? Will you read a critique of one of your own positions with the intent to understand, not to defend?

Further Arguments: Accidents, Divisibility, and Astrology
Three additional arguments: accidents, divisibility, and astrological diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional reasons why the celestial sphere's complexity cannot be explained by emanation from a single simple cause.

Core Idea
The sphere's accidents, divisibility, and astrological diversity further disprove emanation from the One.

After dismantling the emanationist account with the six components, the objector presents three more arguments: the sphere possesses all ten categories of accidents; it is subject to imaginary division into infinite parts; and the diverse natures of celestial spirits and constellations indicate distinct essences. Each argument independently shows that the sphere cannot arise from a single, simple cause.

Guided Path
1
Examine the argument from the ten categories
The sphere has many accidental attributes (quantity, shape, position, etc.) each with multiple types, leading to multiplicity.
The ten categories are the Aristotelian classification of accidents.
2
Understand the argument from imaginary division
The sphere is divisible into parts, so it is composite; a composite cannot arise from a simple cause.
The objector references the problem of the indivisible atom.
3
Consider the astrological argument
Different constellations have different natures and effects, indicating essential diversity that cannot trace to a single aspect.
This argument relies on the consensus of astrologers (ilm al-ahkam).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع: وهو أنكم ذكرتم جهات ثلاثة في العقل الأول وهي: الإمكان والوجود والوجوب بالغير، ثم ذكرتم إن الفلك الأقصى ليس عبارة عن مجموع ثلاثة أشياء، بل هو عبارة عن الهيولى، والصورة الجسمية، والصورة النوعية الفلكية، والنفس الحيوانية المدركة للجزئيات المباشرة للحركات الجزئية، والنفس الناطقة فإن المختار عنده في كتاب الإشارات أن لكل فلك نفسين إحداهما الحيوانية المدركة للجزئيات والثانية النفس الناطقة المدركة للكليات، وأيضاً العقل الثاني. فهذه ستة أشياء: الهيولى، والصورة الجسمية، والصورة النوعية، والنفس الحيوانية، والنفس الناطقة، والعقل المجرد. فإسناد هذه الستة إلى الجهات الثلاث يقتضي أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد. بل نقول: جوهر الفلك لا يخلو من المقولات العشر التي هي أعراض، فله مقدار خاص، وله شكل خاص، وله وضع خاص، وله من كل مقولة نوع واحد أو أنواع. فإذا وزعنا هذه الاعتبارات على الجهات الثلاث، لزم أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد. بل نقول: لا شك أن جوهر الفلك قابل للقسمة الوهمية، وسنبين في مسألة الجوهر الفرد بالدلائل القاهرة أن كل ما يقبل القسمة المقدارية الوهمية فإن ذاته مؤلفة ومركبة من الأجزاء. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كانت ذات الفلك الأعلى مركبة من أجزاء كثيرة لا يعلم عددها إلا الله. فكيف يمكن إسنادها إلى الجهة الواحدة؟ وأيضاً: فإن أصحاب علم الأحكام اتفقوا على أن طبائع الروح مختلفة بحسب التأثيرات. واختلاف الآثار والألوان يدل على اختلاف الملزومات. فهذا يقتضي أن تكون طبائع البروج مختلفة في الماهيات. فإسنادها بأسرها إلى الجهة الواحدة يقتضي أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد.
The fourth question: You mentioned three aspects in the First Intellect: possibility, existence, and necessitation by another. Then you mentioned that the outermost sphere is not composed of three things, but rather of matter, corporeal form, specific celestial form, the animal soul which perceives particulars and directly manages particular motions, and the rational soul – for his chosen view in the Book of Indications is that each sphere has two souls: one animal perceiving particulars, and the second the rational soul perceiving universals – and also the Second Intellect. So these are six things: matter, corporeal form, specific form, animal soul, rational soul, and the separate intellect. Attributing these six to the three aspects necessitates that more than one issue from the One. Rather we say: the substance of the sphere is not devoid of the ten categories which are accidents; it has a specific quantity, specific shape, specific position, and for each category it has one type or types. If we distribute these considerations onto the three aspects, it necessarily follows that more than one issues from the One. Rather we say: there is no doubt that the substance of the sphere is subject to imaginary division, and we will show in the problem of the indivisible atom by compelling proofs that everything subject to quantitative imaginary division has a nature composed and compounded of parts. If so, then the essence of the highest sphere is composed of many parts whose number is known only to God. How then can it be attributed to a single aspect? Also, the practitioners of judicial astrology agree that the natures of the spirit differ according to effects. The difference of traces and colors indicates difference of correlates. This entails that the natures of the constellations differ in essences. So attributing all of them to a single aspect requires that more than one issue from the One.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed objection against the emanationist claim that the First Intellect, with its three aspects (possibility, existence, necessitation by another), is the sole source of all multiplicity. The objector points out that the outermost celestial sphere, according to the philosophers themselves, is not a simple entity but comprises six distinct components: matter, corporeal form, specific form, animal soul, rational soul, and the Second Intellect. Attributing these six to the three aspects of the First Intellect would mean that multiple effects issue from a single cause, contradicting the principle 'from the one only one proceeds.' The objector then strengthens the argument by noting that the sphere's substance also possesses all ten Aristotelian categories as accidents, each with multiple species. Furthermore, the sphere is subject to imaginary division, implying it is composite and thus cannot arise from a simple cause. Finally, the objector appeals to astrology, arguing that the diverse natures of the constellations indicate essential differences, again requiring multiple sources. The argument systematically demonstrates internal inconsistencies in the emanationist framework.

القسمة الوهمية
الجوهر الفرد
علم الأحكام

A) It is indivisible.
B) It is composite.
C) It is eternal.
D) It is simple.

Key Takeaway
Whether through its accidents, divisibility, or astrological diversity, the celestial sphere displays a multiplicity that cannot be reconciled with emanation from a single simple cause.

How might acknowledging that complexity draw you closer to the Creator who alone is truly simple?
?

?

The Fifth Objection and Ibn Sina's Answer
Ibn Sina responds to the multiplicity objection by positing multiple spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against the Neoplatonic emanation system concerning the fixed stars and Ibn Sina's proposed solution of multiple spheres.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina suggests multiple spheres to preserve 'from one only one'.

In this passage, the objector challenges the emanation model by pointing to the many fixed stars, each differing in nature. Ibn Sina responds by denying that the sphere of fixed stars is a single sphere, positing instead multiple spheres each with its own intellect and soul. This lesson explores that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the objector asks about the sphere of fixed stars.
Notice the objector specifies differences in nature, color, magnitude.
2
Ibn Sina's response
Read Ibn Sina's reply that the sphere is actually multiple spheres, each with its own intellect and soul.
This solves the immediate problem but raises further questions.
3
Understand the principle
Understand that the principle 'from one only one' is maintained by making each sphere a separate emanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس: هب أنكم اكتفيتم في الفلك الأقصى بتلك الاعتبارات الثلاث. فكيف قولكم في فلك الثوابت، مع أنه حصلت الكواكب الكثيرة فيه، وكل واحد منها مخالف للآخر في الطبيعة وفي اللون والمقدار؟ فعند هذا قال الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي: إنه لم يظهر لي أن فلك الثوابت كرة واحدة، بل كرات متعددة ينظر بعضها إلى بعض، فإنه بتقدير أن تكون تلك الكرة كرات مختلفة. وتكون لكل واحدة من تلك الكرات عقل ونفس على حدة. فحينئذ يندفع السؤال. إلا أنه يبقى الاعتراض من وجه آخر، وهو أن يقال: هب أنه كرات كثيرة، إلا أنه لا بد وأن يحصل في كل كرة كوكب ولا شك أن جرم الكوكب ممتاز عن جرم الفلك. وحينئذ يحصل الإلزام فيه.
The fifth question: Suppose you are satisfied with those three aspects for the outermost sphere. Then what do you say about the sphere of fixed stars, given that many stars occur in it, each differing from the other in nature, color, and magnitude? At this, the chief master Abu Ali said: It has not become clear to me that the sphere of fixed stars is a single sphere; rather, it is multiple spheres, some of them contiguous to others. For if we suppose that sphere to be different spheres, and each of those spheres has its own intellect and soul, then the question is repelled. However, there remains an objection from another angle: it may be said: Suppose they are many spheres, yet it is necessary that in each sphere there occurs a planet, and there is no doubt that the body of the planet is distinct from the body of the sphere. Then the difficulty still applies to it.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and a partial response by Ibn Sina. The objector asks: even if the outermost sphere is explained by three aspects, what about the fixed stars sphere that contains numerous stars differing in nature, color, and magnitude? Ibn Sina responds by denying that the fixed stars sphere is a single sphere; instead, he posits multiple spheres, each with its own intellect and soul, thus preserving the principle that from one (intellect) only one (sphere) proceeds. However, the objector counters that even with multiple spheres, each sphere still contains a distinct planetary body, creating multiplicity within each sphere. This rejoinder suggests the problem remains unresolved, as the planetary body and the sphere's body are two distinct entities, again requiring multiple effects from a single intellect.

فلك الثوابت
الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي
عقل
نفس
كرات متعددة

A) A single sphere with many stars
B) Multiple spheres, each with its own intellect and soul
C) A sphere with a single intellect for all stars
D) A sphere without any distinct structure

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina resolves the multiplicity of fixed stars by proposing multiple spheres each with its own intellect and soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which path leads to truth, and which to self-deception? This week, identify one assumption you hold about your faith or life that you have never questioned—will you examine it with the same honesty Ibn Sina showed in saying 'it has not become clear to me'?
?
Ibn Sina proposed a complex solution (multiple spheres) to protect a principle. Are there principles or systems in your life (e.g., a habit, a relationship, a worldview) that you are protecting with complex justifications, perhaps ignoring simpler realities? What would it take for you to accept that your initial assumption might be wrong, and how would that change your actions?

The Unresolved Difficulty: Planetary Bodies Distinct from Spheres
The objector points out that even multiple spheres cannot avoid multiplicity within each sphere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remaining objection that even with multiple spheres, the problem of multiplicity persists because each sphere contains a distinct planetary body.

Core Idea
Even multiple spheres don't resolve the issue if each sphere contains a planet distinct from the sphere.

After Ibn Sina's response, the objector levels a further challenge: even if there are multiple spheres, each sphere must contain a planet, and the planet's body is distinct from the sphere's body. This creates multiplicity within a single sphere, contradicting the 'one from one' principle. This lesson examines that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the remaining objection
Read the second half of the passage where the objector presses the issue.
Focus on the logic: each sphere would need to produce both the sphere and its planet.
2
Identify the new difficulty
Note that the objector says the planetary body is distinct from the sphere body, so even if spheres are multiple, each sphere still has two distinct entities (sphere and planet).
This undermines Ibn Sina's solution.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider how this objection challenges the entire emanation framework.

Sixth Objection: The Active Intellect Causes Multiplicity
Objection that Active Intellect implies multiplicity from unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that the Active Intellect's governance of the sublunary world implies unacceptable multiplicity from a single cause.

Core Idea
One cause cannot directly produce multiple distinct effects without violating the principle of unity.

This lesson presents the sixth objection against the emanationist model of the Active Intellect. The objection targets the claim that the Active Intellect manages all things beneath the moon, arguing that this would make it the cause of every new form. Since there are many different forms, this would require a single cause to produce multiple effects, which contradicts the established principle that from one simple thing only one thing proceeds. The passage is incomplete, but the core challenge is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: the objection starts by affirming the philosophers' claim, then draws an implication.
The passage is a fragment; the objection is not fully developed.
2
Identify the principle violated
The objection assumes a key principle: from one simple cause only one effect can directly proceed. The Active Intellect is simple, yet it is said to produce many forms — that is the violation.
This principle is central to the emanationist critique.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is serious for the emanation theory. If the Active Intellect is the cause of all sublunary forms, how can it remain one? This forces proponents to introduce intermediaries or other solutions.
The answer to this objection is not in the passage; think about possible responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السادس: ألستم قلتم إن العقل الفعال هو المدير لما تحت فلك القمر؟ فقد حكمتم بأنه هو العلة لوجود كل ما يحدث في هذا العالم من الصور
The sixth question: Did you not say that the Active Intellect is the manager of what is beneath the sphere of the moon? Thus you have ruled that it is the cause of the existence of every form that comes to be in this world.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage begins the sixth objection against the emanationist framework. The objection points out an inconsistency: the philosophers claim that the Active Intellect (al-`aql al-fa``al) manages the sublunary world and is the cause of all forms that come into existence there. However, the diversity of forms in the sublunary realm—both in species and individuals—would require multiple effects from this single cause, again violating the principle that from one simple thing only one thing proceeds. The text cuts off before the objection is fully developed or answered, leaving it incomplete. This lesson is therefore a fragment, but it illustrates the pattern of arguments against the emanation theory: any claim that a single cause produces multiple distinct effects is problematic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a single cause can directly produce many different effects without any intermediary, as the philosophers claim for the Active Intellect.
Correction: The principle of emanationism holds that from one simple thing only one thing proceeds. Therefore, if the Active Intellect is simple, it cannot directly cause multiple distinct forms; intermediaries or a more complex causal chain are required.

Refuting 'From One Only One' via the Celestial Sphere's Composition and Diversity
The author presents two arguments refuting the emanationist principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the celestial sphere's divisibility and the varying natures of astral bodies refute the philosophical principle that from one only one can proceed.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere's divisibility and variety disprove the 'one from one' principle.

Philosophers argued that from a simple cause only one effect can emerge. But the celestial sphere – the first effect in their system – is divisible and composite, and the astral bodies have varied natures. In this passage, the author uses these observations to challenge the foundational principle of emanationist metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses two distinct arguments separated by 'wa-ayḍan' (furthermore).
2
Identify the two arguments
The first argument is about the divisibility of the celestial sphere (al-qisma al-wahmiyya). The second argument draws on astrological agreements about differing natures. Identify the premises and conclusion of each.
3
Understand the refutation strategy
The author uses the philosophers' own principles (e.g., composition from parts) to show that the celestial sphere cannot be the effect of a single simple cause. Ask yourself why each argument is effective against the 'from one only one' principle.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه الاعتبارات على الجهات الثلاث، لزم أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد . بل نقول : لا شك أن جوهر الفلك قابل للقسمة الوهمية ، وسنبين في مسألة الجوهر الفرد ، بالدلائل القاهرة أن كل ما يقبل القسمة المقدارية الوهمية ، فإن ذاته مؤلفة ومركبة من الأجزاء. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك كانت ذات الفلك الأعلى ، مركبة من أجزاء كثيرة ، لا يعلم عددها إلا الله . فكيف يمكن إسنادها إلى الجهة الواحدة ؟ وأيضاً : فإن أصحاب علم الأحكام اتفقوا على أن طبائع الروح مختلفة بحسب التأثيرات. واختلاف الآثار والألوان ، يدل على اختلاف الملزومات. فهذا يقتضى أن تكون طبائع البروج مختلفة في الماهيات. فإسنادها بأسرها إلى الجهة الواحدة، يقتضى أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد
These considerations regarding the three aspects necessitate that more than one should issue from the One. Rather we say: There is no doubt that the substance of the sphere is divisible by estimation, and we will explain in the discussion of the indivisible atom, with compelling proofs, that everything that is divisible by quantitative estimation is inherently composed and composite of parts. If so, then the substance of the uppermost sphere is composed of many parts, whose number is known only to God. So how can it be attributed to a single source? Furthermore, the practitioners of judicial astrology agreed that the natures of the spirit (or zodiac) differ according to effects. The variation of effects and colors indicates variation of the underlying realities. This necessitates that the natures of the zodiac signs are different in essence. So attributing all of them to one source necessarily implies that more than one issues from the One.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophical principle "from one only one proceeds" (ex uno non fit nisi unum). The author uses two arguments. First, the celestial sphere is divisible in estimation (al-qisma al-wahmiyya), meaning it can be thought of as having parts, so it is composite. In the discussion of the indivisible atom, it will be shown that any such divisible thing is composed of parts. Thus the uppermost sphere is composite, so it cannot be the effect of a simple cause without multiplicity. Second, the astrologers (ashab ‘ilm al-ahkam) affirm that the natures of the astral bodies differ, so they cannot all originate from a single simple source. The argument is structured from the material to the immaterial, using the philosophers' own principles against them.

Key Terms
الجهة الواحدة
الواحد
الفلك الأعلى
القسمة الوهمية
الجوهر الفرد
الملزومات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the principle 'from one only one' is self-evident and cannot be challenged.
Correction: This passage shows that the principle leads to contradictions when applied to the celestial sphere, which is composite and diverse, so it is not self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the composite nature of the sphere refute the principle 'from one only one'?
A) Because the sphere is divisible into parts, so it cannot come from a simple source.
B) Because the sphere is eternal.
C) Because the sphere is made of atoms.
D) Because the sphere has no parts.

Key Takeaway
The celestial sphere's composite nature and the diversity of astral bodies refute the claim that a simple cause produces only one effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God is One, do you ever subconsciously assume His creation must be simple or uniform? In what specific area of your life are you limiting God's creative diversity because you want a simple, predictable outcome? What would change if you truly believed His oneness produces infinite variety?
?
The author says the number of parts in the celestial sphere is 'known only to God'. Do you try to fully understand and control the details of your life, forgetting that only God comprehends its complexity? What is one area you need to surrender to God's sole knowledge and leave the intricate details to Him?
?
The diversity of astral bodies reflects different essences from one Creator. Look at the differences among people around you—your family, strangers, even enemies. Do you resent some of these differences? How can you see each unique person as a direct creation from the One, reflecting His limitless creativity, and respond with awe instead of irritation?

Fifth Objection: The Sphere of Fixed Stars and Ibn Sina's Response
Objection, response, and counter-objection regarding fixed stars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection to the emanationist scheme concerning the fixed stars, Ibn Sina's multiple-spheres response, and the persistent difficulty it faces.

Core Idea
Multiplicity in the fixed stars challenges emanationism; Ibn Sina's solution only defers the problem.

This lesson presents a critical objection to the emanationist model. The opponent argues that the diversity of fixed stars—differing in nature, color, and magnitude—cannot originate from a single simple cause. Ibn Sina attempts to salvage the model by proposing that the sphere of fixed stars is actually multiple spheres, each with its own intellect and soul. However, a further objection arises: even with many spheres, each sphere contains a planet distinct from itself, reintroducing multiplicity within a single sphere.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to grasp the argument's flow. Note the three components: the opponent's question, Ibn Sina's response, and the counter-objection.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical progression.
2
Identify the core objection
The opponent argues that the fixed stars are many and diverse, which cannot emerge from one simple cause (the emanationist principle). Find the specific properties mentioned: nature, color, magnitude.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's response
Ibn Sina denies that the sphere of fixed stars is a single sphere; he asserts it consists of multiple spheres, each with its own intellect and soul. This preserves the principle that each sphere originates from a distinct cause.
Note the phrase 'لم يظهر لي' which indicates uncertainty.
4
Analyze the remaining objection
Even granting multiple spheres, the author points out that each sphere contains a planet distinct from the sphere's body. This introduces a new multiplicity (planet vs. sphere) that requires explanation.
This objection shows that Ibn Sina's response does not fully resolve the problem.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this exchange illustrates the difficulties of applying the 'from one only one emerges' principle to the observed diversity of the cosmos.
This connects to broader themes in Islamic philosophy and kalam.

السؤال الخامس: هب أنكم اكتفيتم في الفلك الأقصى بتلك الاعتبارات الثلاث. فكيف قولكم في فلك الثوابت، مع أنه حصلت الكواكب الكثيرة فيه، وكل واحد منها مخالف للآخر في الطبيعة وفي اللون والمقدار؟ فعند هذا قال الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي: إنه لم يظهر لي أن فلك الثوابت كرة واحدة، بل كرات متعددة ينظر بعضها إلى بعض، فإنه بتقدير أن تكون تلك الكرة كرات مختلفة. وتكون لكل واحدة من تلك الكرات عقل ونفس على حدة. فحينئذ يندفع السؤال. إلا أنه يبقى الاعتراض من وجه آخر، وهو أن يقال: هب أنه كرات كثيرة، إلا أنه لا بد وأن يحصل في كل كرة كوكب ولا شك أن جرم الكوكب ممتاز عن جرم الفلك. وحينئذ يحصل الإلزام فيه.
The fifth question: Suppose you are satisfied regarding the outermost sphere with those three considerations. How do you say regarding the sphere of the fixed stars, when many stars occur in it, each differing from the other in nature, color, and magnitude? At this point, the Chief Master Abu Ali said: It has not become clear to me that the sphere of the fixed stars is a single sphere, but rather multiple spheres, some of them interpenetrating others. On the assumption that that sphere consists of different spheres, and each of those spheres has a separate intellect and soul. Then the question is repelled. However, an objection remains from another direction: it could be said: Suppose they are many spheres, yet necessarily there is in each sphere a planet, and there is no doubt that the body of the planet is distinct from the body of the sphere. Then the difficulty applies.

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection to the emanationist scheme. The opponent notes that within the sphere of fixed stars, there are many stars with different natures, colors, and magnitudes, so they cannot all come from one simple cause. Ibn Sina (the Chief Master Abu Ali) responds by denying that the sphere of fixed stars is a single sphere; he posits multiple spheres, each with its own intellect and soul, thus preserving the principle that each sphere originates from a distinct intellectual principle. However, the author points out a remaining difficulty: even if there are multiple spheres, each sphere has a planet distinct from the sphere itself, so we still have multiplicity within a single sphere (the planet and the sphere body) requiring an explanation.

The Seventh Objection: Infinite Regress of Intellects
The opponent argues that the triple aspects in later intellects should produce effects, leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the triple aspects should cause infinite regress, the philosophers' reply that essences differ, and the author's critique that this makes the number of intellects unknowable.

Core Idea
If aspects are not always causes, the number of intellects becomes unknowable.

This passage presents a key objection against the emanation scheme of the philosophers. The opponent argues that if the triple aspects in the first intellect produce three effects, then the same aspects in later intellects should continue producing three effects, leading to infinite regress. The philosophers reply that the essences differ, but the author points out that this leads to an unknown number of intellects—undermining their claim to certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the argument's flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: objection, reply, objection to the reply.
2
Identify the objection
The opponent says: if the triple aspects in the first intellect produce three effects, and the same aspects exist in later intellects (like the Active Intellect), those aspects should also produce three effects, leading to an infinite regress.
This is a standard tuq (objection) in kalam.
3
Understand the philosophers' reply
The philosophers answer that the essences of intellects are different, so the same aspects in different intellects do not necessarily have the same causal power.
This reply is accepted as 'good' by the author, but he then raises a problem.
4
Grasp the author's critique
The author points out that if the causal power of the aspects is not constant, then we cannot know how many intellects exist. It is possible that many intellects produce only one effect each, and only at some point does an intellect produce the three effects. This makes the number unknown.
The key phrase: 'لا يبعد' (it is not unlikely).
5
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this is a problem for the philosophers: they claim to know the number of intellects (ten or eleven), but the author's objection shows that the number could be vast and uncertain.
This ties into the larger debate about whether reason alone can determine the details of the emanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السابع: إنه إذا كانت الجهات الثلاث في العقل الأول موجبة لعقل ونفس وفلك. فهذه الجهات الثلاث حاصلة في العقل الفعال، الذي هو العقل الأخير. فلم لا يصدر عن هذه الجهات الثلاث الموجودة في العقل الفعال تلك المعلولات الثلاثة وهكذا إلى ما لا آخر له؟ أجابوا عنه فقالوا: إن ماهيات العقول مختلفة، فلا يلزم من كون الجهات الثلاث الحاصلة في بعضها عللاً لهذه المعلولات الثلاثة أن تكون هذه الجهات الثلاث الحاصلة في كلها عللاً لهذه المعلولات الثلاثة. فنقول: هذا الكلام حسن. إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يصير عدد العقول معلوماً. فإنه لا يبعد، إنه وإن حصلت الجهات الثلاث في العقل الأول، إلا أنه لا يصدر عنه إلا عقل واحد. وكذلك يصدر عن العقل الثاني عقل واحد، وهو العقل الثالث. وهكذا إلى ألف ألف مرتبة، ثم إنه يحصل بعد ذلك عقل، تكون الجهات الثلاث الحاصلة فيه عللا للفلك والنفس والعقل. ثم يحصل من ذلك العقل عقول مترتبة إلى العدد الذي لا يعلمه إلا الله.
The seventh question: If the three aspects in the first intellect necessitate an intellect, a soul, and a sphere, then these three aspects exist in the Active Intellect, which is the last intellect. So why do not these three aspects existing in the Active Intellect produce those three effects, and so on ad infinitum? They answered: The essences of the intellects are different, so it does not follow that the three aspects existing in some of them are causes of these three effects, such that these three aspects existing in all of them would be causes of these three effects. So we say: This speech is good, except that on this supposition the number of intellects becomes unknown. For it is not unlikely that even though the three aspects exist in the first intellect, only one intellect proceeds from it, and similarly from the second intellect only one intellect proceeds, which is the third, and so on up to a thousand thousand degrees. Then after that there comes into existence an intellect in which the three aspects are causes of the sphere, soul, and intellect. Then from that intellect proceed intellects in succession up to a number known only to God.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection regarding infinite regress. If the triple aspects (being-in-itself, being-for-itself, and being-for-another) in the first intellect produce three effects (intellect, soul, sphere), then since the Active Intellect also has these three aspects, it should produce three effects, and so on infinitely. The philosophers reply that the essences of intellects differ, so the same aspects in different intellects may not be causes. The author acknowledges this but points out a problem: if the causal power of the aspects is not constant, then we cannot know the number of intellects. It is possible that many intellects produce only one intellect each, and only at some point does an intellect produce the three effects. This leads to an unknown number, which contradicts the philosophers' claim that they know the number of intellects.

العقل الفعال
عدد العقول
علل
ألف ألف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' answer that essences differ fully resolves the problem of infinite regress.
Correction: The author shows that while the answer blocks infinite regress, it introduces indeterminacy: the number of intellects becomes unknown, which contradicts the philosophers' claim to know it.

Checkpoint
What problem does the author raise with the philosophers' response to the infinite regress objection?
A) It leads to an infinite number of intellects.
B) It makes the number of intellects unknown.
C) It contradicts the principle of causality.
D) It eliminates the need for the first intellect.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers cannot consistently maintain both the productive power of the aspects and a determinate number of intellects.

Seventh Objection: Why Not from the Active Intellect?
Seventh objection to the principle 'from one only one proceeds' and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh objection to the principle 'from one only one proceeds', the philosophers' answer based on differing essences, and the critique that this answer makes the number of intellects unknowable.

Core Idea
The three aspects in any intellect should produce three effects, but differences in essence break the pattern.

This lesson presents the seventh objection against the principle that from one only one can proceed. The objection challenges the consistency of the three-aspect theory across different intellects. The philosophers offer an answer based on the distinct essences of each intellect, but the author raises a critical flaw: if each intellect's essence determines its effects, then the total number of intellects becomes unknowable.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the logic of the three aspects in the first intellect producing three effects, and why the same should apply to the active intellect.
2
Examine the philosophers' answer
Note that they argue the essences of intellects differ, so the same aspects do not necessarily produce the same effects.
3
Analyze the author's critique
See why the answer leads to an unknown number of intellects, making the system indeterminate.
4
Reflect on the implication
Recognize how this objection challenges the entire emanationist framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السابع : إنه إذا كانت الجهات الثلاث في العقل الأول موجبة ، لعقل ونفس وفلك . فهذه الجهات الثلاث حاصلة في العقل الفعال ، الذي هو العقل الأخير. فلم لا يصدر عن هذه الجهات الثلاث الموجودة في العقل الفعال : تلك المعلولات الثلاثة وهكذا إلى ما لا آخر له ؟ أجابوا عنه فقالوا : إن ماهيات العقول مختلفة ، فلا يلزم من كون الجهات الثلاث الحاصلة في بعضها عللاً لهذه المعلولات الثلاثة . أن تكون هذه الجهات الثلاث الحاصلة في كلها عللاً لهذه المعلولات الثلاثة . فنقول : هذا الكلام حسن . إلا أن على هذا التقدير لا يصير عدد العقول معلوماً .
The seventh objection: If the three aspects in the first intellect necessitate an intellect, a soul, and a sphere, then these three aspects are present in the active intellect, which is the last intellect. So why do not these three aspects in the active intellect produce those three effects, and so on ad infinitum? They answered: The essences of the intellects are different, so it does not follow that the three aspects present in some of them are causes for these three effects, that these three aspects present in all of them would be causes for these three effects. We say: This speech is good, except that on this assumption the number of intellects would not be known.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objection against the philosophical principle that from one only one can proceed, and the philosophers' answer to it. The objection argues that if the three aspects (necessity of the cause, knowledge of the cause, and possibility of the effect) in the first intellect produce an intellect, a soul, and a sphere, then since these same three aspects exist in the active intellect (the last intellect), it should also produce three corresponding effects, leading to an infinite regress. The philosophers answer by saying the essences of the intellects are different, so what holds for one does not necessarily hold for all. The author (Sanusi?) then comments that this answer is fine in itself, but it has a flaw: if the production of effects depends on the specific essence of each intellect, then the number of intellects becomes unknowable, because we cannot determine how many intellects must exist before a particular essence arises that can produce a sphere, etc.

الجهات الثلاث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The seventh objection is about the active intellect itself, not about the consistency of the three aspects across intellects.
Correction: The objection actually focuses on the fact that the three aspects (necessity, knowledge, possibility) present in the first intellect are also present in the active intellect, so by consistency they should produce similar effects.

Key Takeaway
The seventh objection shows that the three-aspect theory leads to an infinite regress, and the philosophers' answer based on differing essences makes the number of intellects unknowable.
