The Arbitrariness of the Number of Intellects
Sanusi uses a hypothetical chain to show the philosophers' number of intellects is arbitrary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers' own logic makes the number of intellects arbitrary, undermining their specific claims.

Core Idea
If three aspects cause one intellect, then any number is possible—ten or fifty is baseless.

Sanusi now drives home a devastating consequence of the philosophers' answer: their principle that the three aspects in each intellect cause the next allows for any number of intellects, not just ten or fifty. By following their logic to its extreme, he shows that the specific numbers they assert are completely arbitrary. This passage illustrates how the philosophers' own reasoning unravels their system.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi builds a hypothetical chain starting from the first intellect.
The passage uses a repetitive structure to emphasise the arbitrariness.
2
Identify the hypothetical scenario
Sanusi presents a scenario where from the first intellect only one intellect proceeds, then from that one another, and so on up to a thousand thousand levels. Trace this progression in the text.
The numbers are deliberately exaggerated to show any number is possible.
3
Understand the conclusion
After showing that the chain can continue indefinitely and produce intellects at any stage with the three aspects suitable for spheres, Sanusi concludes that the philosophers' specific claims (ten or fifty) are invalid.
The final sentence ties this back to their arguments about possibility, existence, and necessity.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If the number of intellects can be arbitrarily large, what does that do to the philosophers' entire system? This shows a fatal flaw in their causal explanation.
This connects to the broader critique of the emanation theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه لا يبعد ، إنه وإن حصلت الجهات الثلاث في العقل الأول ، إلا أنه لا يصدر عنه إلا عقل واحد . وكذلك يصدر عن العقل الثاني عقل واحد ، وهو العقل الثالث . وهكذا إلى ألف ألف مرتبة ، ثم إنه يحصل بعد ذلك عقل ، تكون الجهات الثلاث الحاصلة فيه عللا للفلك والنفس والعقل . ثم يحصل من ذلك العقل : عقول مترتبة إلى العدد الذي لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى ثم يحصل عقل تكون الجهات الثلاث التي فيه صالحة لعلية الفلك الثاني ولعقله ولنفسه . وعلى هذا التقدير فيفسد قولهم بالعقول العشرة ، أو الخمسين . بالغير فهذه السؤالات كلها واردة على ما ذكروه في الإمكان والوجود والوجوب
For it is not unlikely that, even though the three aspects occur in the first intellect, only one intellect proceeds from it. Likewise from the second intellect proceeds one intellect, which is the third intellect, and so on up to a thousand thousand levels. Then after that an intellect occurs such that the three aspects in it are causes for the sphere, the soul, and the intellect. Then from that intellect proceed intellects arranged in a sequence up to a number known only to Allah. Then an intellect occurs such that the three aspects in it are suitable for causality of the second sphere, its intellect, and its soul. And on this assumption their theory of ten or fifty intellects is invalid. By another. So all these objections apply to what they mentioned about possibility, existence, and necessity.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the consequence of the philosophers' answer that the specific essence of each intellect determines what proceeds from it. The author (Sanusi) drives home the arbitrariness: it is perfectly possible that from the first intellect only one other intellect proceeds, not three, and then from that second intellect another single intellect, and so on for a thousand thousand steps, until finally an intellect occurs that has the three aspects fit to produce a sphere, soul, and intellect. Then from that intellect a chain of intellects proceeds up to an unknown number, and then another intellect suitable for the second sphere, etc. On this picture, the claimed fixed numbers—ten intellects or fifty—are baseless. The final line 'بالغير' likely introduces a new point or is a scribal note; it says 'By another.' Then the author states that all these objections apply to the philosophers' discussion of possibility, existence, and necessity.

Correction: The author is not endorsing any number; he is showing that the philosophers' own logic allows any number, making their specific claims baseless.

Refuting the Four Sciences Argument
Dilemma refuting knowledge as principle of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma used to refute the claim that divine knowledge can be the principle of multiple existents without circularity.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge cannot explain multiplicity without falling into circularity or an infinite regress.

This passage tackles a sophisticated objection from those who argue that God's knowledge of possibilities and existents serves as the principle for their existence. The author (Sanusi) uses a classic logical dilemma to show that this claim leads to either repeating earlier objections or raising a new problem of multiplicity. Let's examine how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Start by reading the passage and noting the opponent's statement: 'The four sciences are the principles for the four existents.' This is the target of the refutation.
The 'four sciences' likely refer to divine knowledge of possibility, existence, etc.
2
Understand the first horn
Follow the first part of the dilemma: if the knowledge is identical to the objects (possibility and existence), then previous objections against those objects apply directly to this knowledge.
This horn shows that claiming identity does nothing to solve the original problem.
3
Understand the second horn
Now consider the second alternative: if the knowledge is different from the objects, then we need a cause for these multiple acts of intellection, which brings back the problem of multiplicity from a single source.
This horn forces the opponent to explain how multiple knowledges arise from one God.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that the entire theory is extremely weak and confused. Reflect on why both horns are problematic.
The dilemma effectively blocks the attempt to use divine knowledge as a principle for existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله ثانياً : « إن العلوم الأربعة هي المبادىء للموجودات الأربعة ، فنقول : علمه بالإمكان وبالوجود . إن كان نفس الإمكان والوجود . فكل ما أوردناه على الإمكان والوجود فهو وارد على هذا العلم ، وإن كان مغايراً لهما ، عاد البحث في علة وجود هذه التعقلات الكثيرة . فقد ظهر بما ذكرنا : أن الذي قالوه في ترتيب الوجود : كلام في غاية الضعف والخبط
As for his second statement: 'The four sciences are the principles for the four existents,' we say: His knowledge of possibility and existence, if it is identical to possibility and existence, then everything we objected regarding possibility and existence applies to this knowledge. And if it is different from them, then the inquiry returns to the cause of the existence of these multiple acts of intellection. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned that what they said about the arrangement of existence is extremely weak and confused speech.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage refutes another argument from the philosophers (or perhaps from the same proponent of emanation). They claimed that four types of divine knowledge (e.g., knowledge of possibilities, etc.) are principles for four corresponding types of existents. The author (Sanusi) argues by dilemma: If this knowledge is identical to the possibility and existence themselves, then the same objections already raised against possibility and existence apply to this knowledge. If the knowledge is different from them, then we still need to explain the cause of these multiple acts of intellection (the knowledge themselves), thus the same problem of multiplicity from One recurs. Therefore, the entire theory of the arrangement of existence is weak and confused.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dilemma is about the nature of divine knowledge itself (whether it is identical to its objects or not).
Correction: The dilemma is actually about whether divine knowledge can serve as a principle for existence without circularity or infinite regress. Both horns show that the claim fails.

Checkpoint
What is the first horn of the dilemma regarding the four sciences?
A) If knowledge is the same as existence, then previous objections apply.
B) If knowledge is different from existence, then it is not a principle.
C) If knowledge is the same as existence, then it is perfect.
D) If knowledge is different, then it is unknowable.

Key Takeaway
The claim that divine knowledge can be the principle for multiple existents fails on both horns of the dilemma, revealing the theory as weak and confused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God's knowledge is identical to His essence, then every thought you have about God must align with His absolute simplicity. Does your heart truly submit to this, or do you find yourself imagining 'things' in God's mind? Take a moment to repent and ask for true tawhid.

God is the Direct Cause of All
Author's conclusion: reject 'from one only one', affirm God's direct creation of all.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects the principle 'from one only one' and affirms that God is the direct cause of all existence and essences.

Core Idea
God creates everything directly, without intermediaries.

This passage presents the author's final conclusion on the topic of divine causation. He identifies the root error—the maxim 'from one only one proceeds'—and reminds us that its proofs have already been refuted. He then states the correct position: God is the direct principle of all contingent beings, their genera, species, and individuals, as supported by the Quran and rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the flow: the author first mentions the principle he rejects, then gives the correct view.
Notice the shift from criticism to positive affirmation.
2
Identify the rejected principle and the objections
Locate the statement 'الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد' (from one only one proceeds). Understand that the author considers its proofs weak and the objections against it irrefutable.
This principle was used by philosophers to posit intermediaries.
3
Understand the positive affirmation
The author asserts that God is 'المبدأ لوجود جميع الممكنات' (the principle of existence of all contingent beings) and also the cause of their quiddities. Note the Quranic evidence (Maryam 93) and the claim of conclusive proofs.
This affirms direct creation without intermediate causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتحقيق في هذا الباب : أن الأصل الذي فرعوا عليه هذه الكلمات هو قولهم : ( الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، وقد بينا ضعف دلائلهم في تقرير هذا الأصل، وأما الإشكالات اللازمة على هذا الأصل فهي الأسئلة ، التي لا محيص عنها البتة . فكان اللائق بالقائلين بالموجب ، أن يقولوا : إنه تعالى هو المبدأ لوجود جميع الممكنات ، أجناسها وأنواعها وأشخاصها . كما جاء في الكتاب الإلهي : إن كُل مَن في السموات والأرض إلا أتى الرحمن عبداً ) وكما أنه سبحانه هو المؤثر في وجود الكل ، فهو المؤثر في ماهية الكل. على ما بينا بالدلائل القاهرة : إن المؤثر كما يؤثر في الوجود ، فهو المؤثر أيضاً في الماهيات. وههنا آخر الكلام في تفاصيل أقوال القائلين بالموجب [ والله ولي التوفيق ]
The investigation in this chapter is that the principle on which they based these statements is their saying: 'From one only one proceeds.' As for the objections consequent upon this principle, they are the questions from which there is absolutely no escape. So it was fitting for those who affirm necessitation to say: He, exalted, is the principle of the existence of all contingent beings, their genera, species, and individuals, as it came in the divine book: 'There is no one in the heavens and the earth but that he comes to the Most Merciful as a servant' (Maryam 93). And just as He, glorified, is the efficient cause in the existence of all, so He is the efficient cause in the quiddity of all, according to what we have demonstrated with conclusive proofs: the efficient cause, just as He effects existence, He also effects quiddities. And here ends the speech on the details of the statements of those who affirm necessitation. [And Allah is the grantor of success.]

Commentary
This passage is the author's concluding analysis. He identifies the root principle behind all the erroneous theories: the maxim 'from one only one proceeds.' He reminds that its proofs have been shown weak, and the objections against it are irrefutable. Therefore, the correct position, fitting for those who accept the necessitation (i.e., that God is the true agent), is to affirm that God Almighty is the direct principle of existence for all contingent things, including their genera, species, and individuals. He supports this with a Qur'anic verse (Maryam 93) showing all beings are servants of God. Furthermore, God's causation extends not only to existence but also to quiddities (essences), as the author claims to have proven with strong arguments. The passage ends the discussion on the details of the necessitarians' views.

Refutation of the Ten Intellects via Three Aspects
Refutes the philosophers' claim of ten intellects via the three aspects of each intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle 'from one only one proceeds' is used to refute the philosophers' scheme of ten intellects by showing that each intellect has three distinct aspects.

Core Idea
Each intellect has three aspects, so from one intellect would proceed three things, violating the principle of emanation.

The philosophers claim that from the First Intellect only one thing proceeds, leading to a chain of ten intellects. But if each intellect has three aspects—being a cause for the next sphere, its own intellect, and its own soul—then from one intellect three things would proceed, contradicting their own principle. This passage lays out that refutation and connects it to broader objections about possibility, existence, and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the three aspects mentioned: causing the second sphere, its intellect, and its soul.
2
Identify the three aspects
Identify the three aspects of each intellect: one that makes it a cause for the next sphere, one that is its own intellect, and one that is its own soul. List them in your mind.
These three aspects are the crux of the refutation.
3
Understand the invalidation
Reflect on how having three aspects means each intellect would cause three things, not one. This violates the philosophers' own principle 'from one only one proceeds', thus invalidating the entire scheme of ten (or fifty) intellects.
The principle is not explicitly in the passage but is central to the argument.
4
Connect to broader objections
Notice the final sentence linking this refutation to earlier objections about possibility, existence, and necessity. See how this is part of a larger critique of emanationist metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يحصل عقل تكون الجهات الثلاث التي فيه صالحة لعلية الفلك الثاني ولعقله ولنفسه . وعلى هذا التقدير فيفسد قولهم بالعقول العشرة ، أو الخمسين . بالغير فهذه السؤالات كلها واردة على ما ذكروه في الإمكان والوجود والوجوب
Then an intellect occurs possessing three aspects that are suitable for causing the second sphere, its intellect, and its soul. And according this estimation, their claim of ten intellects, or fifty, becomes invalid. With the other, all these objections are applicable to what they mentioned concerning possibility, existence, and necessity.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' theory of ten (or fifty) intellects based on the principle 'from one only one proceeds'. The argument points out that each intellect in their system has three distinct aspects: one that makes it a cause for the next sphere, one that is its own intellect, and one that is its own soul. If each intellect has three aspects, then from the First Intellect three things would proceed, not one. Thus the principle 'from one only one proceeds' is violated, and their entire scheme of ten (or fifty) intellects collapses. The author concludes that all the objections raised earlier about possibility, existence, and necessity also apply here.

Correction: Each intellect has three aspects, so from one intellect three things would proceed, violating the principle 'from one only one proceeds' and thus invalidating the scheme.

Refuting the Four Sciences as Principles
Refutation of the four sciences as principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that the four sciences are principles of the four existents and the author's refutation of it.

Core Idea
The four sciences cannot be principles without leading to contradiction or infinite regress.

Philosophers claimed that God's knowledge of possibility, necessity, etc., serves as the principle for the four levels of existence. The author presents a dilemma: if that knowledge is identical to the realities themselves, previous objections stand; if it is different, we need a cause for the multiplicity of cognitions. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the philosophical claim
Read the opening lines where the philosophers' statement is presented: 'The four sciences are the principles for the four existents.' Pay attention to the terms.
The four sciences are knowledge of possibility, existence, etc.
2
Analyze the dilemma
The author gives two options: if God's knowledge is identical to the objects, then previous objections about the One causing many apply; if different, we must find the cause of the multiplicity of cognitions. Identify the logical force of each horn.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the verdict
The author concludes that the philosophers' arrangement is extremely weak and confused. The principle 'From the One only one proceeds' is baseless and leads to unanswerable questions.
This sets up the correct view in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله ثانياً : « إن العلوم الأربعة هي المبادىء للموجودات الأربعة ، فنقول : علمه بالإمكان وبالوجود . إن كان نفس الإمكان والوجود . فكل ما أوردناه على الإمكان والوجود فهو وارد على هذا العلم ، وإن كان مغايراً لهما ، عاد البحث في علة وجود هذه التعقلات الكثيرة . فقد ظهر بما ذكرنا : أن الذي قالوه في ترتيب الوجود : كلام في غاية الضعف والخبط والتحقيق في هذا الباب : أن الأصل الذي فرعوا عليه هذه الكلمات هو قولهم : ( الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد ، وقد بينا ضعف دلائلهم في تقرير هذا الأصل، وأما الإشكالات اللازمة على هذا الأصل فهي الأسئلة ، التي لا محيص عنها البتة . فكان اللائق بالقائلين بالموجب ، أن يقولوا : إنه تعالى هو المبدأ لوجود جميع الممكنات ، أجناسها وأنواعها وأشخاصها . كما جاء في الكتاب الإلهي : إن كُل مَن في السموات والأرض إلا أتى الرحمن عبداً وكما أنه سبحانه هو المؤثر في وجود الكل ، فهو المؤثر في ماهية الكل. على ما بينا بالدلائل القاهرة : إن المؤثر كما يؤثر في الوجود ، فهو المؤثر أيضاً في الماهيات وههنا آخر الكلام في تفاصيل أقوال القائلين بالموجب
As for his second statement: 'The four sciences are the principles for the four existents,' we say: His knowledge of possibility and existence. If it is identical to possibility and existence, then everything we have objected to concerning possibility and existence applies to this knowledge. And if it is different from them, the inquiry returns to the cause of the existence of these multiple cognitions. So it has become clear from what we have mentioned that what they said about the arrangement of existence is extremely weak and confused speech. And the verification in this matter is that the principle upon which they built these words is their statement: 'From the One only one proceeds.' And we have already shown the weakness of their proofs in establishing this principle. As for the objections necessary to this principle, they are questions from which there is absolutely no escape. So it was fitting for those who affirm the necessary cause to say that He, exalted, is the principle of the existence of all possible things, their genera, species, and individuals, as it came in the divine book: 'There is none in the heavens and the earth but comes to the Most Merciful as a servant' (Maryam 93). And just as He, glorified, is the effecter in the existence of the whole, so He is the effecter in the quiddity of the whole. According to what we have demonstrated with overwhelming proofs: that the effecter, just as He effects in existence, so He also effects in quiddities. And here ends the discussion concerning the details of the statements of those who affirm the necessary cause.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' claim that the four sciences (knowledge of possibility, necessity, etc.) are principles for four existents (possibly the intellects, souls, spheres, etc.). The author presents a dilemma: if God's knowledge of possibility and existence is identical to possibility and existence themselves, then previous objections about the unicity of the First and the multiplicity of effects apply. If it is different, then we need to find a cause for the multiplicity of cognitions, which again leads to an infinite regress or violation of the principle. The conclusion is that the philosophers' arrangement is extremely weak and confused. Then the author turns to the correct view: the proper position for those who believe in a necessary cause is that God is the principle of existence for all possible things, including their genera, species, and individuals, as confirmed by the Quran. Moreover, God is the effecter not only of existence but also of quiddities (essences). This is based on overwhelming proofs that the effecter influences both existence and essence. This passage thus refutes the emanationist scheme and affirms God's direct causal power over all things in their entirety.

المبادىء
الإمكان
الوجود
التعقلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's knowledge is a separate entity that can serve as a principle for multiple existents without issue.
Correction: The passage shows that either knowledge is identical to objects (leading to old objections) or different (leading to new problems), so it cannot be a principle.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the claim that the four sciences are principles?
A) He proves that God's knowledge is identical to the objects, so the claim is true.
B) He presents a dilemma that leads to contradiction or infinite regress.
C) He argues that the four sciences do not exist.
D) He asserts that the philosophers are infidels.

Key Takeaway
The four sciences cannot be principles because the dilemma forces either a contradiction or an infinite regress.

?

God as the Cause of All Existence and Quiddity
Affirmation that God is the cause of all existence and quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's positive assertion that God is the principle of all possible things and the effecter of both existence and quiddity.

Core Idea
God directly creates every existent, including its essence and existence.

Having refuted the philosophical emanation scheme, the author now presents the correct Islamic position. He argues that God is the principle of existence for all possible things—genera, species, individuals—and that He is the effecter of both existence and essence. This lesson examines the scriptural and rational basis for this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the affirmative argument

The author now speaks as a theologian affirming Islamic teachings.
2

This is a rejection of the intermediary intellects.
3

The author adds that God is also the effecter in quiddity (essence). This goes beyond occasionalism—even the 'whatness' of things is from God.
This is a strong claim: God creates the very definitions of things.
4
Review the proof basis
The author refers to 'overwhelming proofs' and a Quranic verse. Reflect on how reason and revelation support this view.
The verse is Maryam 93: all creation comes to God as servants.

Proof for Prime Matter and the Indivisible Atom
The author presents the proof for matter and identifies it with indivisible atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical argument for prime matter (hayula) and its identification with indivisible atoms that are indivisible in existence but divisible in imagination.

Core Idea
Matter consists of indivisible atoms that are ultimate constituents of events.

This lesson introduces the concept of prime matter (hayula) and its identification with indivisible atoms. The passage presents the core thesis of atomism within Islamic philosophical theology, distinguishing between existential indivisibility and imaginary divisibility. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping later debates on the nature of substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a reference to proofs, an identification, a qualification, a promise of further discussion, and a statement on the original state of atoms.
The passage is compact; every phrase carries weight.
2
Focus on the divisibility distinction
The passage explicitly says atoms are indivisible in existence but divisible in imagination. Ask yourself why this distinction matters for arguments about the physical world and theological debates.
This distinction prevents misunderstanding about atomism.
3
Reflect on the eternal state
The passage ends by stating that these atoms were separate, stationary, and at rest in eternity. Consider how this sets up the need for a cause of motion and change.
This leads into the proof for a Creator.

وأما إثبات الهيولى : فقد ذكرنا دلائل القائلين بأن الحوادث لا بد لها من هيولى . ثم قال : « محمد بن زكرياء » : ( وهذه الهيولى هي الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ بحسب الوجود ، وإن كانت قابلة للتجزئة بحسب الوهم . . وسنذكر الحال فيها في مسألة الجوهر الفرد . قال : ( وتلك الأجزاء كانت متفرقة وواقفة ساكنة في الأزل .
As for the proof of matter: We have mentioned the proofs of those who say that events must have matter. Then he said: 'Muhammad ibn Zakariya': (And this matter is the indivisible particles according to existence, even though they are divisible according to imagination. And we will mention the state of it in the issue of the indivisible atom. He said: (And those particles were separate, stationary, and at rest in eternity.)
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage discusses the proof for the existence of prime matter (hayula). It refers to the arguments of those who say that events require a substrate. It quotes Muhammad ibn Zakariya (likely al-Razi) identifying matter with indivisible atoms (ajza' la tatajzza). These atoms are indivisible in existence but divisible in imagination. It promises further discussion in the topic of the indivisible atom (jawhar al-fard). It also states that these atoms were originally separate, stationary, and at rest in eternity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'indivisible in existence' means atoms are purely theoretical and not real.
Correction: The passage affirms that atoms are real and exist as indivisible units; their indivisibility is a property of existence, not a mental fiction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how are the atoms described in terms of divisibility?
A) Indivisible both in existence and imagination
B) Divisible in existence but not imagination
C) Indivisible in existence but divisible in imagination
D) Divisible both ways

Key Takeaway
This passage establishes that prime matter consists of indivisible atoms that are separable only in imagination, not in existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on the fact that every object you see, including your own body, is made up of atoms that were once separate and at rest. How does this realization affect your understanding of your own dependence on Allah for every moment of your existence?
?
The passage distinguishes between atoms divisible in imagination but not in reality. Think of a sin you have justified in your mind as 'small' or 'harmless'—are you treating it as divisible in your imagination while it is indivisible in its reality before Allah? What will you do to stop justifying it?
?
The atoms were originally separate and at rest. What areas of your life are currently 'at rest'—spiritually stagnant or motionless—that need the external cause of Allah's guidance to move? What concrete step will you take today to seek that movement?

Al-Razi's Atomic Definition of the Hyle
Al-Razi defines the hyle as indivisible parts and describes their eternal state.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's definition of the hyle as parts indivisible in existence but divisible in imagination, and its primordial state.

Core Idea
The hyle is made of atoms — indivisible in reality, divisible only in thought.

This passage presents a key definition from al-Razi on the nature of the hyle (prime matter). After mentioning that proofs for the hyle have been given, al-Razi specifies that the hyle consists of parts that are indivisible in actual existence, though they can be divided in imagination. He also describes these parts as originally scattered and stationary in eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the quotation structure: the author is citing al-Razi.
2
Focus on the key distinction
Identify the phrase 'لا تتجزأ بحسب الوجود، وإن كانت قابلة للتجزئة بحسب الوهم'. This is the core definition: indivisible in existence but divisible in imagination. Ask yourself why this distinction matters for understanding atomism.
This distinction is unique to al-Razi's atomism.
3
Consider the primordial state
Al-Razi describes the parts as 'متفرقة وواقفة ساكنة في الأزل'. Think about what this implies: scattered, stationary, and eternal. This sets the stage for later discussions of motion and change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثبات الهيولى : فقد ذكرنا دلائل القائلين بأن الحوادث لا بد لها من هيولى . ثم قال : « محمد بن زكرياء » : ( وهذه الهيولى هي الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ بحسب الوجود ، وإن كانت قابلة للتجزئة بحسب الوهم . وسنذكر الحال فيها في مسألة الجوهر الفرد . قال : ( وتلك الأجزاء كانت متفرقة وواقفة ساكنة في الأزل .
As for the proof of the hyle: we have mentioned the proofs of those who say that contingencies must have a hyle. Then he said: 'Muhammad ibn Zakariya' said: 'And this hyle is the parts that are indivisible according to existence, even if they are divisible according to imagination. And we will mention the state of it in the issue of the indivisible substance.' He said: 'And those parts were scattered, standing, stationary in eternity.'
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage discusses the proof for the hyle (prime matter). The author states that he has already mentioned the proofs of those who claim that contingent events must have a hyle as their substratum. Then he quotes Muhammad ibn Zakariya (al-Razi) defining the hyle as the parts that are indivisible in actual existence (لا تتجزأ بحسب الوجود), though they are divisible in imagination (بحسب الوهم). This is a reference to the atomic theory. Al-Razi promises to discuss this further in the topic of the indivisible substance (الجوهر الفرد). He also adds that these parts were scattered (متفرقة), standing (واقفة), and stationary (ساكنة) in eternity (في الأزل). This describes the primordial state of matter before motion.

Misconception: The indivisibility of atoms means they cannot be divided even in thought.
Correction: Al-Razi specifies that atoms are indivisible in existence (actual reality) but divisible in imagination (mental conception).

B) Divisible in existence and imagination
C) Indivisible in existence but divisible in imagination

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defines the hyle as real atoms that are indivisible in actuality but divisible in thought, and these atoms were initially scattered, standing, and stationary in eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Razi describes these atoms as 'standing, stationary' in eternity. Does your heart ever feel as if it is standing still, waiting for something to move it? In that stillness, are you aware that it is Allah alone who grants motion to all things?
?
If the fundamental building blocks of the world were once scattered and inert, what does that imply about the absolute need for a Divine Actor to bring order? When you look at the order in your own life, do you truly attribute it to Allah, or do you unconsciously assume the atoms arranged themselves?

Proof of the Soul's Eternity
Proof of soul's eternity via divine free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof for the eternity of the soul based on the Creator being an agent by choice.

Core Idea
The soul's eternity is deduced from the need for an ignorant agent to initiate creation.

This lesson examines the second station of proving the soul: its eternity. The argument hinges on the nature of divine agency. If God is a free agent, His act cannot be eternal; an eternal ignorant agent—the soul—must have prompted the first act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on two stations
Read the Arabic passage that outlines the two stations: proving the soul is not the body, and proving it is eternal. Focus on the second station's proof.
The first station is assumed from philosophical proofs.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that the proof begins from the established fact that the Creator is an agent by choice (fa'il bi al-ikhtiyar), not a necessitator by essence (mujib bi al-dhat).
3
Follow the deduction
Trace how the act of a free agent must be originated (muhdath), and that origination requires a preceding cause. The cause here is an ignorant agent who initiated the act.
The argument is that a wise free agent cannot begin to act without a prior cause.
4
Conclude the soul's eternity
The passage identifies that ignorant agent as the soul, and argues that it must be eternal to explain the first act.
This sets up the premise for al-Razi's five eternals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إثبات النفس ففيه مقامان: الأول: إثبات أن النفس غير البدن، ودلائل الفلاسفة في إثبات هذه النفس مشهورة. والمقام الثاني: إثبات أنها قديمة. والدليل على قدمها: أنه لما ثبت بالدليل أن إله العالم فاعل بالاختيار، لا موجب بالذات، امتنع أن يكون فعله أزلياً. لأن الفاعل بالاختيار هو الذي يفعل بواسطة القصد والاختيار، ومن كان فعله بواسطة القصد، فإن فعله يكون محدثاً مسبوقاً بالعدم، وكل ما كان كذلك، فإنه لا بد وأن يصير فاعلاً، بعد أن كان غير فاعل من الأزل، إلى ذلك الوقت. فإنه يستحيل أن يبتدىء بالفعل في ذلك الوقت، إلا بعد أن يتقدمه فاعل جاهل، شرع في الفعل بجهله، وثبت أن ذلك الفاعل الجاهل هو النفس، وهذا يقتضي أن النفس يجب أن تكون قديمة. وأما إثبات أن الخلاء والدهر موجودان في الأزل، فسيأتي دلائل ذلك في كتاب المكان والزمان.
وعند هذا، قال محمد بن زكرياء الرازي: «فقد ثبت القول بهذه القدماء الخمسة» ثم زعم: أن كل واحد منها واجب الوجود لذاته، وطعن في دلائل من قال: «إن واجب الوجود يمتنع أن يكون أكثر من واحد»، وزعم شيئاً منها لا يبقى على البحث الصحيح.
فهذا هو إحدى المقدمات، التي لا بد من الوقوف عليها في هذا الباب.
As for the proof of the soul, it has two stations: The first: proving that the soul is not the body—the proofs of the philosophers in establishing this soul are well-known. The second station: proving that it is eternal. The proof for its eternity: since it has been established by proof that the God of the world is an agent by choice (fa‘il bi al-ikhtiyār), not a necessitator by essence (mūjib bi al-dhāt), it is impossible that His act be eternal. Because the agent by choice is the one who acts through intention (al-qaṣd) and choice (al-ikhtiyār), and the one whose act is through intention—his act is originated (muḥdath) preceded by non-existence, and everything that is so must become an agent after having been not an agent from eternity, up to that time. For it is impossible that He begin the act at that time except after He be preceded by an ignorant agent who commenced the act out of his ignorance, and it is established that that ignorant agent is the soul, and this necessitates that the soul must be eternal. As for proving that void (al-khalā’) and time (al-dahr) exist in eternity, its proofs will come in the Book of Place and Time.
At this point, Muḥammad ibn Zakariyā al-Rāzī said: “So the statement of these five eternals (al-qudamā’ al-khamsah) is established.” Then he claimed that each one of them is necessary in existence by its essence (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī), and he attacked the proofs of those who said: “The necessary in existence cannot be more than one,” and he claimed something from them that does not survive sound investigation.
So this is one of the premises that must be understood in this chapter.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents the first premise concerning the soul's eternity and the doctrine of the five eternals. The author divides the proof of the soul into two stations: first, demonstrating that the soul is distinct from the body, which is supported by well-known philosophical arguments; second, proving that the soul is eternal. The proof of its eternity begins from the established premise that the Creator is a free agent (fa‘il bi al-ikhtiyār) rather than a necessary cause (mūjib bi al-dhāt). If He were a necessary cause, His act would be eternal and coeternal with Himself. But since He is a free agent, He acts through intention and choice, which means His act is originated (muḥdath), preceded by its non-existence. Therefore, there must be a point at which He begins to act after not having acted from eternity. However, it is impossible for a perfectly wise agent to begin to act without prior ignorance or external motive. Thus, there must exist an ignorant agent who initiated the act out of ignorance, and that ignorant agent is identified as the soul. Hence, the soul must be eternal to account for the origination of the world. The author then notes that proofs for the eternity of void and time will follow later. Next, the passage reports Muḥammad ibn Zakariyā al-Rāzī's claim that five eternal entities (God, soul, matter, space, time) are established, each being necessary in existence by its essence (wājib li-dhātihī), and his criticism of the standard argument that the necessary of existence must be unique. The author remarks that this claim does not survive critical examination. This entire discussion constitutes one of the necessary premises for the subsequent arguments.

Key Terms
النفس
فاعل بالاختيار
موجب بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the soul's eternity is proven independently without relying on the nature of divine agency.
Correction: The passage explicitly derives the soul's eternity from the premise that the Creator is an agent by choice.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage conclude that the soul must be eternal?
A) Because the soul is distinct from the body.
B) Because the Creator's act as a free agent requires an eternal ignorant agent to initiate it.
C) Because al-Razi said so.
D) Because the soul is necessary by essence.

Key Takeaway
The soul must be eternal to explain why the free Creator began to act in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is eternal, what does that mean for how you live each day? Are you acting with the awareness that your soul has no beginning and will have no end, accountable to the One who created it? What is one habit you will change today to align your soul with divine guidance instead of ignorance?
?
The proof says an ignorant agent prompted God's act. In your own life, where are you acting out of ignorance, delaying the good that God wants for you? What step will you take this week to replace that ignorance with knowledge, especially of your own soul?
?
The soul is eternal yet not God. Does your daily worship reflect that your soul is a created being that depends entirely on its Creator? How can you deepen your reliance on God alone, rather than on the created things of this world?

Al-Razi's Five Eternals
Presentation and critique of al-Razi's five eternals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's doctrine of five eternal entities and the author's critical response.

Core Idea
Al-Razi claimed five eternal beings, each necessary by essence, but his argument fails.

After establishing the soul's eternity, the passage introduces al-Razi's doctrine of five eternals: God, soul, matter, space, time. Al-Razi argued each is necessary and criticized the standard proof for God's uniqueness. The author dismisses this as unsound.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's claim
Read the Arabic passage from 'And at this point' to the end. Note the five eternals and al-Razi's assertion.
2
Understand the five eternals
Al-Razi claims that God, soul, matter, space, and time are all eternal and each is necessary in existence by its essence.
This is a polytheistic doctrine from an Islamic perspective.
3
Recognize the critique
The author notes that al-Razi attacked the proof that the necessary of existence must be unique, but his own claim does not withstand sound investigation.
The author does not elaborate the critique here but implies rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the proof of the soul, it has two stations: The first: proving that the soul is not the body—the proofs of the philosophers in establishing this soul are well-known. The second station: proving that it is eternal. The proof for its eternity: since it has been established by proof that the God of the world is an agent by choice (fa‘il bi al-ikhtiyār), not a necessitator by essence (mūjib bi al-dhāt), it is impossible that His act be eternal. Commentary
This passage presents the first premise concerning the soul's eternity and the doctrine of the five eternals. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Razi's arguments did not survive sound investigation. When was the last time you honestly examined one of your deeply held beliefs under the light of the Quran and sound reason, and are you willing to do that today?
?
If tawhid is rationally undeniable, why do you still find yourself acting as if other things are ultimate? What one step can you take today to align your daily life with the fact that only Allah is necessary?

Types of Efficient Cause: Natural Necessity and Voluntary Action
Introduces the two main types of efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two fundamental types of efficient cause according to Islamic theology: natural necessitation and voluntary action.

Core Idea
Every cause either acts by necessity or by choice.

This lesson introduces the second premise, which classifies efficient causes (mu’aththir) into two basic categories. The first is natural necessity, where the cause produces its effect by inherent nature, like fire burning. The second is voluntary action, where the cause acts through power and choice. Understanding this distinction is essential for later arguments about divine action and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice the clear division into two types.
Focus on the defining examples for each type.
2
Understand the first type
Natural necessity (al-ṭab‘ wa al-ījāb) – causes that act without choice, like fire burning.
These causes are deterministic.
3
Understand the second type
Voluntary action (al-qudra wa al-ikhtiyār) – causes that act with power and choice.
This type involves free will.
4
Reflect on significance
Why is this classification important for kalam? It sets the stage for discussing God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية هي: إن المؤثر إما أن يكون مؤثراً على سبيل الطبع والإيجاب مثل: تأثير النار في الإحراق، وتأثير الماء في التبريد. وإما أن يكون مؤثراً على سبيل القدر والإختيار. وهذا على قسمين: أحدهما: الفاعل الحكيم الذي يكون فعله على وفق الحكمة والمصلحة. والثاني: الذي يكون فعله على وفق العبث والاتفاق. مثل: الصبيان والمجانين. وقد سبق جواز صدور مثل هذا الفعل عن الرجل الحكيم. ومثاله: ما إذا كان الإنسان مشتغل الفكرة بمهم عظيم. فإنه في أثناء تلك الفكرة قد يعبث بشعرة واحدة من شعرات لحيته، وقد يأخذ نبتة من الطريق ويعبث بها، وهو وإن كان يعبث بتلك الشعرة، وبتلك النبتة، إلا أنه يكون كالغافل عن تلك الأفعال، ولا يكون فعله وتركه منطبقاً على قانون الحكمة والمصلحة، بل على محض الاتفاق والعبث.
The second premise: the efficient cause (al-mu’aththir) is either an efficient cause by way of nature and necessitation (al-ṭab‘ wa al-ījāb), like the effect of fire in burning and the effect of water in cooling; or it is an efficient cause by way of power and choice (al-qudra wa al-ikhtiyār). And this latter has two divisions: one of them is the wise agent (al-fā‘il al-ḥakīm) whose action is in accordance with wisdom and benefit; and the second is the one whose action is in accordance with futility and chance (al-‘abath wa al-ittifāq), such as children and the insane. And it has already been established [that it is possible for such an act to issue from a wise man. An example of this is when a man is absorbed in thought about a great matter: during that thinking he may play with a single hair of his beard, or take a plant from the road and play with it; and although he plays with that hair and that plant, he is like one unaware of those actions; his action and omission do not conform to the law of wisdom and benefit, but purely to chance and futility.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage defines the second premise, which classifies efficient causes (mu’aththir) into two fundamental types. The first type is natural necessitation (al-ṭab‘ wa al-ījāb), where the cause produces its effect by inherent nature without choice, analogous to fire burning or water cooling. The second type is voluntary causation (al-qudra wa al-ikhtiyār), where the cause acts through power and choice. The voluntary type is further subdivided into two: the wise agent (al-fā‘il al-ḥakīm) who acts according to wisdom and benefit (al-ḥikma wa al-maṣlaḥa), and the agent whose action follows futility and chance (al-‘abath wa al-ittifāq), such as children or the insane. The author then notes that it is possible for a wise person to perform an action that appears futile or arbitrary, but only when he is distracted or preoccupied. The example given is a man deeply concentrated on a significant problem: while thinking, he might absentmindedly fiddle with a hair of his beard or pluck a plant and play with it. Although he is a wise man, that particular action is not governed by wisdom or benefit but by sheer chance and aimlessness. This classification is important for later arguments about divine action and creation.

Key Terms
المؤثر (al-mu’aththir)
الطبع والإيجاب (al-ṭab‘ wa al-ījāb)
القدر والاختيار (al-qudra wa al-ikhtiyār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a cause acting by natural necessity?
A) A person choosing to eat.
B) Fire burning.
C) An angel performing a miracle.
D) God creating the universe.

Key Takeaway
Efficient causes are either natural and necessitated or voluntary and chosen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of something that happens in your life that you usually attribute to natural causes, like rain falling or your heart beating. According to this classification, does that cause act by nature or by choice? How does realizing that even natural causes are secondary to Allah’s voluntary power change your awareness of His presence in that moment? Before you sleep tonight, pick one such event and consciously say in your heart: 'This is from Allah by His choice.'
?
The passage gives examples of fire burning and water cooling as natural causes. But your everyday reliance on these causes may lead you to forget that they act only by Allah’s permission. Do you functionally trust the 'nature' of things more than the will of Allah? What specific step will you take tomorrow to remind yourself that every 'natural' effect is really an act of Allah’s voluntary agency?
?
You are an efficient cause that acts by choice. But how often do you act on autopilot, driven by habit like a natural cause? This lesson distinguishes the two. Identify one habit you do without thinking (e.g., checking your phone) and commit to doing that action tomorrow with a conscious intention (niyyah) for Allah’s sake, transforming it from a natural routine into a voluntary act of worship.

Subdivisions of Voluntary Causation: Wise Agent vs. Arbitrary Agent
Subdivides voluntary causation into wise and arbitrary, with an example of a wise agent acting arbitrarily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of voluntary agents: the wise agent who acts with wisdom and the arbitrary agent who acts with futility, and how a wise person can sometimes act arbitrarily due to distraction.

Core Idea
Not all voluntary actions are wise; some are arbitrary.

In this lesson, we delve into the subdivisions of voluntary causation. The passage teaches that voluntary agents are either wise, acting according to wisdom and benefit, or arbitrary, acting according to futility and chance. It also explains that even a wise person can perform seemingly arbitrary actions when distracted. This distinction helps clarify that God, as the Wise Agent, acts with perfect wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation about the two divisions of voluntary causation.
Notice the clear two-part structure and the example.
2
Identify the two types
The wise agent (al-fā‘il al-ḥakīm) and the arbitrary agent (al-‘abath wa al-ittifāq) exemplified by children and the insane.
3
Understand the exception
The passage notes that a wise man can also perform arbitrary actions when distracted or preoccupied, but such actions are not his deliberate wise actions.
This nuance prevents misunderstanding about divine actions.
4
Reflect on the example
A man deeply thinking may play with his beard or a plant absentmindedly – this is arbitrary, not wise.

Agents: Natural, Volitional, and Wise
Classifies agents into natural/compelled and volitional, then subdivides volitional agents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic philosophy classifies agents based on their mode of causation, distinguishing natural necessity from volitional choice, and within that, wise versus foolish action.

Core Idea
Agents act either by nature or by choice; wise agents act with purpose, but even the wise may act foolishly when distracted.

This passage introduces a foundational classification of agents that underpins discussions of causality and divine action. It distinguishes between agents that act by natural necessity, like fire burning, and those that act by power and choice—namely, humans and other volitional beings. Within volitional agents, a crucial further distinction is made between the wise agent who acts according to wisdom and benefit, and the foolish agent who acts randomly. Understanding this taxonomy clarifies how occasional foolish acts from a wise person do not undermine their essential wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall distinction between agents acting by nature and those acting by choice.
Pay attention to the two main categories introduced at the beginning.
2
Focus on the subdivision
Identify the two types of volitional agents: the wise agent (الفاعل الحكيم) and the foolish agent (العبث والاتفاق). Notice how each is defined.
The passage gives examples: children and the insane for the foolish type.
3
Examine the example
Analyze the example of a person preoccupied with a great thought who idly plays with his beard or a plant. Understand why this does not contradict the person's essential wisdom.
The key concept is 'heedlessness' (الغفلة) during distraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية
هي : إن المؤثر. إما أن يكون مؤثراً على سبيل الطبع والإيجاب مثل : تأثير النار في الإحراق، وتأثير الماء في التبريد . وإما أن يكون مؤثراً على سبيل القدر والإختيار . وهذا على قسمين :
أحدهما : الفاعل الحكيم الذي يكون فعله على وفق الحكمة والمصلحة .
والثاني : الذي يكون فعله على وفق العبث والاتفاق . مثل : الصبيان والمجانين . وقد سبق جواز صدور مثل هذا الفعل عن الرجل الحكيم . ومثاله : ما إذا كان الإنسان مشتغل الفكرة بمهم عظيم . فإنه في أثناء تلك الفكرة قد يعبث بشعرة واحدة من شعرات لحيته ، وقد يأخذ نبتة من الطريق ويعبث بها ، وهو وإن كان يعبث بتلك الشعرة ، وبتلك النبتة ، إلا أنه يكون كالغافل عن تلك الأفعال ، ولا يكون فعله وتركه منطبقاً على قانون الحكمة والمصلحة ، بل على محض الاتفاق والعبث .
The second introduction: It is that an agent either acts by nature and necessity, like the effect of fire in burning and the effect of water in cooling; or acts by power and choice. This is of two types: One: the wise agent whose action is in accordance with wisdom and benefit. The second: one whose action is in accordance with folly and chance, like children and the insane. And it has preceded the permissibility of such an act occurring from a wise man. Its example: when a person is preoccupied with a great important thought, then during that thought he may play with one strand of his beard, or he may take a plant from the road and play with it—and even though he plays with that strand and that plant, he is like one heedless of those actions, and his action and abandonment are not in accordance with the law of wisdom and benefit, but rather according to pure chance and folly.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a classification of agents (المؤثر) into types based on their mode of causation. It begins by stating that an agent is either one that acts by nature and necessity (الطبع والإيجاب) like fire burning or water cooling—these are compelled causes—or one that acts by power and choice (القدر والإختيار). The latter, volitional agents, are then divided into two further types. The first is the wise agent (الفاعل الحكيم) whose action is consistent with wisdom (الحكمة) and benefit (المصلحة). The second is the agent whose action follows folly (العبث) and chance (الاتفاق), exemplified by children and the insane. The author notes that it has been previously established that such a foolish act can occur from a wise man. The example given is a person deeply preoccupied with a great matter; during that preoccupation, he may idly play with a strand of his beard or pick up a plant and fidget with it. Although he is acting foolishly, he is like one heedless of those actions, and his action and abstention do not conform to the law of wisdom and benefit, but rather to pure chance and folly. This clarifies that occasional distracted actions do not negate the agent's essential wisdom.

Key Terms
مؤثر
طبع
إيجاب
قدر
اختيار
فاعل حكيم
عبث
اتفاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A wise man's occasional foolish act would contradict his wisdom, so such acts cannot occur from him.
Correction: The passage explains that when a wise man is preoccupied or heedless, his actions may deviate from wisdom, but this does not negate his overall attribute of wisdom.

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Agents are divided by mode of causation into natural and volitional; volitional agents are further divided into wise and foolish, but even the wise can act foolishly when distracted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act carelessly or sin, do you brush it off as a small mistake? The passage shows that even a wise man can do foolish things when distracted. What 'great thought' occupies your mind that might be causing you to be heedless in your daily actions? Commit today to bring more conscious awareness to every small deed.
?
In the example, the man plays with his beard or a plant while preoccupied. Look at your own habits: how many of your actions are done on autopilot, without intentionality? Are you living as a wise agent acting with purpose, or are you drifting into folly? This lesson calls you to examine the quality of your choices, not just their outcomes.
?
The passage distinguishes agents who act by wisdom and benefit from those who act by folly and chance. Reflect on a recent decision you made. Was it guided by wisdom and benefit, or was it a product of distraction or impulse? What would it take for you to align more of your actions with the way of the wise agent (الفاعل الحكيم)? Pray for mindfulness and take one deliberate action today.

Causality and Created Things
advanced

The Problem of Specifying a Time for Creation
First sophism: the time-specification problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first sophism against a wise deity beginning creation: the argument that no specific time can have a property that justifies it being the moment of creation.

Core Idea
If the time of creation had a special property, that leads to absurd consequences.

This lesson examines the first part of a philosophical objection to the idea that a wise God created the world at a particular moment after eternal inaction. The objection argues that if the time was chosen because of a property, that property cannot be explained without circularity or undermining the argument for God's existence. Let's see how the reasoning unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the objection first establishes premises about God's nature.
2
Identify the two options
The passage presents two options: either the time of creation has a special property or it does not. The first option is then refuted via a threefold analysis.
3
Follow the refutation of the first option
The property cannot be essential to the time or a concomitant, as that would lead to necessitating all events. Nor can it be accidental, as that leads to circularity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The first option is dismissed, leaving only the second option (no property). This sets up the next sophism.
Think about why this argument challenges the idea of a wise creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : قد ثبت بالدليل : أن إله العالم ليس علة موجبة بالذات ، بل فاعل بالاختيار ، وثبت : أنه متى كان فاعلاً مختاراً ، واجب أن يكون فعله محدثاً . وإذا ثبت أن فعله محدث، لزم أن يقال : إنه كان تاركاً للفعل من الأزل إلى ذلك الوقت ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنه لا يجوز أن يبتدىء الإله الحكيم بالفعل ، بعد أن كان تاركاً له مدة غير متناهية . والذي يدل عليه وجوه [ من الشبهات : الشبهة ] الأولى : إنه إما أن يقال : بأن هذا الوقت اختص بخاصية ، لأجلها استحق أن يخصه الفاعل الحكيم بالشروع في التكوين والتخليق فيه وإما أن يقال : إنه لم توجد هذه الخاصية . والأول باطل . لان اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك الخاصية ، إما أن يكون لذات ذلك الوقت ، أو لشيء من لوازمه ، أو لا لذاته ، ولا لشيء من لوازم ذاته . والأول والثاني باطلان إذا لو جاز أن يكون ذلك الوقت المعين، موجباً لتلك الخاصية المعينة ، جاز أيضاً أن يوجب سائر الحوادث . وإذا جوزنا ذلك ، فحينئذ لا يمكننا الاستدلال بحدوث الحوادث على وجود الإله الحكيم. والثالث أيضاً باطل . لأن الكلام الذي ذكرناه في تخصيص ذلك الوقت ، بالشروع في إحداث العالم ، عائد بعينه في تخصيص ذلك الوقت بتلك الخاصية . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو أن يقال : الإله الحكيم شرع في الإحداث والتكوين في ذلك الوقت ، بعد أن كان تاركاً له مدة غير متناهية، من غير سبب اختص به ذلك الوقت : كان ذلك محض العبث . ومثل هذا الفعل لا يليق البتة بالفاعل الحكيم . الا ترى أنا إذا رأينا رجلاً حكيماً عاقلاً ، أمسك عن نوع من أنواع الفعل سنين طويلة ، وجلس في زاوية داره ساكتاً ، تاركاً لذلك الفعل بالكلية ، ثم إنه بعد الأدوار الطويلة قفز دفعة واحدة ، وشرع في العمل بالجد العظيم ، والاجتهاد التام فإن كل عاقل يقول له : ما السبب الذي اقتضى انتقالك من الإعراض إلى الشروع، ومن الترك إلى الفعل ؟ فإن قال : لم يحدث البنة سبب اقتضى ذلك ، فكل أحد من العقلاء يقول : إن هذا لا يليق بالعقلاء والحكماء ، فإنه إن كان الترك أصوب ، فلم انتقلت منه إلى الفعل لا لسبب ؟ وإن كان الفصل اصوب ، فلم تركته قبل ذلك لا لسبب ؟ فيثبت : أن الانتقال من الترك الأزلي ، إلى الفعل من غير سبب البتة لا يليق بالفاعل الحكيم البتة . أما الفاعل السفيه ، فإنه لا يليق به ذلك. ألا ترى أن الصبيان والمجانين قد ينتقلون من الفعل إلى الترك تارة ، ومن الترك إلى الفعل أخرى ، من غير سبب يقتضى ذلك ومن غير موجب يوجبه . والعقلاء لا يستبعدون حدوث مثل هذه الحالة عن الفاعل الجاهل العابث. فيثبت أن ابتداء القصد إلى التكوين لا يليق بالفاعل الحكيم. وإنما يليق بالفاعل الجاهل، فوجب إثبات
They said: It has been established by proof that the God of the world is not a necessary cause by essence, but rather a free agent by choice. And it is established that whenever He is a free agent, it is necessary that His action be originated. And if it is established that His action is originated, it follows that He must be said to have been refraining from action from eternity until that time. And when this is established, we say: It is not permissible for the wise deity to begin action after having been refraining from it for an infinite duration. And what indicates this are aspects [of the sophisms: the first sophism]: namely, it is either said that this time was distinguished by a specific property, on account of which it deserved that the wise agent specify it for beginning composition and creation, or it is said that this property did not exist. The first is false. For the specification of that time with that property is either due to the essence of that time, or to something from its concomitants, or not due to its essence nor to something from the concomitants of its essence. The first and second are false, because if it were permissible for that specific time to necessitate that specific property, it would also be permissible for it to necessitate all other accidents. And if we permit that, then we cannot infer from the occurrence of accidents the existence of the wise deity. The third is also false, because the discussion we mentioned regarding the specification of that time for beginning the origination of the world returns exactly to the specification of that time with that property. As for the second division: it is that it is said: The wise deity began origination and composition at that time, after having been refraining from it for an infinite duration, without any cause specific to that time: that would be pure futility. And such an action is absolutely not befitting the wise agent. Do you not see that if we see a wise, intelligent man who refrains from a type of action for long years, sitting silently in a corner of his house, completely abandoning that action, then after long periods he jumps up all at once and begins working with great earnestness and complete diligence, then every intelligent person would say to him: What is the reason that necessitated your transition from turning away to beginning, and from abandonment to action? If he says: No cause at all occurred to necessitate that, then every rational person would say: This is not befitting the rational and wise. For if abandonment was more correct, why did you transition from it to action without cause? And if action was more correct, why did you abandon it before without cause? So it is established: that the transition from eternal abandonment to action without any cause at all is not at all befitting the wise agent. As for the foolish agent, that is not befitting him. Do you not see that children and madmen sometimes transition from action to abandonment and from abandonment to action without a cause necessitating that and without a necessitator necessitating it. And rational people do not deem it unlikely for such a state to occur from an ignorant, frivolous agent. So it is established that the beginning of intention to compose is not befitting the wise agent; rather it is befitting the ignorant agent. So it is necessary to affirm.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a complex objection (shubha) against the claim that a wise deity can begin creating the world after an infinite period of inaction. The argument is presented as a reductio ad absurdum, aiming to show that such an action would be incompatible with divine wisdom.

It begins by establishing two premises: (1) God is not a necessary cause but a free agent; (2) a free agent's action must be temporally originated (muhdath). From this it follows that if God created the world, He must have been refraining from action for an infinite time before creation. The objection then argues that it is impossible for a wise deity to begin acting after such eternal inaction.

The proof is given through two 'aspects' (wujuh) or sophisms (shubuhat).

The first sophism considers the specific time of creation: either that time had a special property that made it the appropriate moment for creation, or it did not. The first option is refuted by analyzing how that time could have such a property. The property cannot be essential to the time itself or a concomitant of the time, because then that time would necessitate all other events, undermining the argument from contingency for God's existence. Nor can the property be accidental, because the same problem of specification recurs for the property itself. Thus the first option fails, leaving only the second option.

The second sophism directly argues that if there is no reason for choosing that particular time, then beginning creation after infinite inaction is pure futility (mahd al-'abath). Such arbitrary action is unbecoming of a wise agent. The author draws an analogy: if we see a rational man suddenly start working after years of inactivity with no reason, we would deem him irrational. This is because the transition from eternal abandonment to action without a cause is only expected from foolish agents (like children or madmen), not from a wise deity. Therefore, the objection concludes, beginning intention to create is not befitting the wise agent; it would only befit an ignorant one. Hence, the objector argues, one must affirm that God did not begin creation after eternal inaction—implying either that the world is eternal or that God's action is not truly originated. The passage ends abruptly with "so it is necessary to affirm" (fawajaba ithbat), leaving the conclusion open, but the thrust is clear: this is a polemic against the orthodox view that God created the world in time.

Key Terms
فاعل بالاختيار
علة موجبة بالذات
محدث
فاعل حكيم
شبهة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any time can be chosen arbitrarily without reason, but this objection shows that arbitrariness leads to absurdity.
Correction: The objection does not deny God's power but argues that a wise agent must have a reason for acting at a specific time.

Checkpoint
According to the first sophism, what is false about the first option (that the time of creation has a special property)?
A) The property can be essential to the time, but that would make the time eternal.
B) The property cannot be explained without circularity or undermining the argument for God's existence.
C) The property is not needed because God can act without reason.
D) The property is known only to God, so we cannot judge.

Key Takeaway
The first sophism argues that no specific time can have a justifying property for creation without logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's will does not need a reason outside of itself? When you face trials you don't understand, are you willing to trust that His choice of the moment is wise even if you see no justifying property?
?
Have you ever demanded a 'special property' from God to justify His timing in your life? How does this lesson challenge you to surrender your need for explanations and rest in His free agency?
?
If the universe had a reason to be created at a particular moment, that reason would have to be God Himself. Does your heart live in that reality, or do you still seek signs from the world to validate God's actions?

The Futility of Beginning After Eternal Inaction
Second sophism: futility argument via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second sophism: that beginning creation after infinite inaction without a cause is pure futility, which is incompatible with divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Acting without reason after infinite inaction is foolish, not wise.

In this lesson, we explore the second and more direct objection: even if we could somehow explain why this particular time is appropriate (which we cannot), the very transition from eternal inaction to action without any cause is absurd. The author uses a powerful analogy of a wise man suddenly starting to work after years of idleness. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and analogy
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the analogy of the wise man who suddenly starts working after years of silence.
Notice the structure: proposition, analogy, conclusion.
2
Understand the analogy's force
The analogy shows that such a transition without cause is deemed irrational by all sensible people.
3
Contrast with foolish agents
The author contrasts with children and madmen, who act arbitrarily. This highlights that arbitrary action is unworthy of a wise agent.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The objection concludes that beginning intention to create is only befitting an ignorant agent, not the wise deity.
This challenges the opponent to affirm that God either had a reason (which was already refuted) or is not wise.

They said: It has been established by proof that the God of the world is not a necessary cause by essence, but rather a free agent by choice. And it is established that whenever He is a free agent, it is necessary that His action be originated. And if it is established that His action is originated, it follows that He must be said to have been refraining from action from eternity until that time. And when this is established, we say: It is not permissible for the wise deity to begin action after having been refraining from it for an infinite duration. And what indicates this are aspects [of the sophisms: the first sophism]: namely, it is either said that this time was distinguished by a specific property, on account of which it deserved that the wise agent specify it for beginning composition and creation, or it is said that this property did not exist. The first is false. For the specification of that time with that property is either due to the essence of that time, or to something from its concomitants, or not due to its essence nor to something from the concomitants of its essence. The first and second are false, because if it were permissible for that specific time to necessitate that specific property, it would also be permissible for it to necessitate all other accidents. And if we permit that, then we cannot infer from the occurrence of accidents the existence of the wise deity. The third is also false, because the discussion we mentioned regarding the specification of that time for beginning the origination of the world returns exactly to the specification of that time with that property. As for the second division: it is that it is said: The wise deity began origination and composition at that time, after having been refraining from it for an infinite duration, without any cause specific to that time: that would be pure futility. And such an action is absolutely not befitting the wise agent. Do you not see that if we see a wise, intelligent man who refrains from a type of action for long years, sitting silently in a corner of his house, completely abandoning that action, then after long periods he jumps up all at once and begins working with great earnestness and complete diligence, then every intelligent person would say to him: What is the reason that necessitated your transition from turning away to beginning, and from abandonment to action? If he says: No cause at all occurred to necessitate that, then every rational person would say: This is not befitting the rational and wise. So it is established that the beginning of intention to compose is not befitting the wise agent; rather it is befitting the ignorant agent. So it is necessary to affirm.

Commentary
This passage is a complex objection (shubha) against the claim that a wise deity can begin creating the world after an infinite period of inaction. The argument is presented as a reductio ad absurdum, aiming to show that such an action would be incompatible with divine wisdom.
